The Two Universal Maxims on Conditions in Contracts
Two universal maxims on contractual conditions from Ibn Qayyim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply Ibn Qayyim's two universal maxims to evaluate the validity of contractual conditions within the Hanafi madhhab.

Core Idea
Every condition that contradicts Shariah is void; every condition that does not is binding if stipulated.

This passage introduces two foundational maxims from Ibn Qayyim that govern conditions in contracts. While originating from Hanbali thought, these maxims are universally applicable and align with Hanafi qawaid. Understanding them is essential for a mufti assessing contractual terms, as they provide a clear framework to distinguish valid from void conditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Universal Maxims
Read the passage to extract the two qadiyat kulliyat: (1) Any condition contradicting Allah's ruling or His Book is void. (2) Any condition that does not contradict His ruling and can be granted or waived without stipulation is binding when stipulated.
These maxims are derived from Quran, Sunnah, and Ijma according to Ibn Qayyim.
2
Understand the Criterion: Contradiction with Shariah
The key test for a condition's validity is whether it conflicts with a clear nass (text) or a definitive legal principle. Conditions that permit the haram or prohibit the halal fall under the first maxim.
Hanafi: Conditions against the essence of the contract (e.g., stipulating no liability for gross negligence) are void.
3
Apply the First Maxim: Void Conditions
Identify conditions that directly oppose a Quranic ruling or established Sunnah. For example, stipulating a loan with pre-agreed interest (riba) is void and corrupts the contract if essential.
In Hanafi, if a void condition is attached to a contract, the contract may become fasid (corrupt) if the condition is integral.
4
Apply the Second Maxim: Valid and Binding Conditions
If a condition does not contradict any nass and is a matter that could be voluntarily granted or waived, then stipulating it makes it binding. Example: stipulating a specific delivery date or a penalty for delay.
Hanafi: Such conditions are considered part of the contract and must be fulfilled.
5
Relate to Hanafi Qawaid and Fatwa Methodology
These maxims resonate with Hanafi principles like 'al-umur bi-maqasidiha' and the classification of shurut into sahih, batil, and fasid. A mufti should refer to the established Hanafi categorizations when applying the maxims.
The Hanafi school has detailed rulings on specific conditions (e.g., in sales, marriage) that refine the application of these universal maxims.

Chapter (42): Ijtihadat on the Authority of Contractual Will. - Indeed, he has in this regard a precious, immortal statement that is considered among the glories of juristic views. For after he reviewed in his book I'lam al-Muwaqqi'in some texts of Shariah and transmitted hadith traditions concerning the principle of conditions and the established narrations from Imam Ahmad regarding suspending contracts and restricting them with conditions, he says verbatim: 'The intended point is that conditions have a status in the eyes of the Lawgiver that is not held by many jurists. For some of them nullify conditions that the Lawgiver did not nullify, and thereby corrupt the contract without any corruption that necessitates its invalidity, and they are contradictory regarding which contracts accept suspension by conditions and which do not, so they have no consistent, comprehensive principle supported by evidence. The correct view is the Shariah principle indicated by the texts, consisting of two universal propositions: First: Every condition that contradicts Allah's ruling and contradicts His Book is void, whatever it may be. Second: Every condition that does not contradict His ruling nor contradict His Book, and may be granted or foregone without stipulation, is binding by stipulation. Nothing is exempted from these two propositions; they are supported by the Book of Allah, the Sunnah of His Messenger, and the consensus of the Companions, and no attention is paid to objections from sectarian issues and opinion-based statements, for they do not undermine any principle of Shariah.' (I'lam al-Muwaqqi'in, Muniriyya edition, vol. 3, pp. 339-340, abridged). 22 - On occasion of Ibn Qayyim's statement, we recall to memory that great constitutional statement of his teacher, Shaykh al-Islam Ibn Taymiyya, may Allah have mercy on him, which we previously quoted at [page 542], where he says: 'The basis of contracts is the consent of the contracting parties, and their outcome is what they have imposed upon themselves through contracting.' This magnificent expression is what should rightly be considered the Islamic constitutional principle for the authority of contractual will. Fiqh 569. Sixth Section: Theory of Contracts and Related Matters in Fiqh. We have taken care to elaborate on Hanbali ijtihad here because it is the one that, in the subject of the authority of contractual will, aligns with modern legal theories and is suitable as a basis for comparison (1). (1) The venerable scholar Shaykh Muhammad Abu Zahra says in his book 'Ibn Hanbal' after presenting Ahmad ibn Hanbal's doctrine on freedom of contracting and contractual conditions, verbatim: 'Thus you see that Imam who made the effects of the Salaf his teacher, graduated upon them, and was guided by their guidance, ended up in contracts and many transactions of people with expansion instead of restriction, and permission instead of prohibition. And thereby proof was established that those who claim that returning to the ways of the righteous Salaf imposes restrictions on people do not know the reality of these effects, nor how the Companions traversed the path, nor how they dealt with the problems that arose with the spirit of a religion that came as a mercy to people and did not come to burden them or restrict them. These contracts upon which global markets depend today already had space in Ahmad's fiqh, and they were facilitated for them.' (See: Kitab Ibn Hanbal, p. 570, 233).
Source pages p. 539

Commentary
This passage is a legal philosophy discussion on the principle of party autonomy (sultan al-irada al-'aqdiyya) in contracts. The author, Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa, quotes Ibn Qayyim al-Jawziyya from I'lam al-Muwaqqi'in, who presents two universal maxims (qadiyatan kulliyatan) regarding conditions (shurut) in contracts. The first maxim: any condition that contradicts a ruling of Allah or His Book is void. The second: any condition that does not contradict Shariah and can be voluntarily given or waived without stipulation is binding if stipulated. Ibn Qayyim asserts these maxims are derived from the Quran, Sunnah, and consensus of the Companions, and they are not subject to exceptions. The author then connects this to Ibn Taymiyya's statement that the basis of contracts is mutual consent, which he calls the 'constitutional principle' of Islamic contract law. The author explains that he chose to elaborate on Hanbali ijtihad because it aligns with modern legal theories on freedom of contract. He further quotes Muhammad Abu Zahra, who highlights that Imam Ahmad's school adopted a broad approach of permissibility rather than restriction, reflecting the merciful spirit of Shariah. In the Hanafi school, similar principles apply: conditions that contradict the essence of the contract or are prohibited by Shariah are void (e.g., stipulating a loan with interest), while conditions that are beneficial and not prohibited are valid and binding (e.g., stipulating a penalty clause for delay). This passage underscores the Islamic legal philosophy that contractual freedom is the default, limited only by explicit Shariah prohibitions.

Key Terms
شرط (shart): A condition stipulated in a contract that may affect its validity or alter its effects.
سلطان الإرادة العقدية (sultan al-irada al-'aqdiyya): The principle that parties are free to stipulate terms in their contracts as long as they do not contradict Shariah.
قضية كلية (qadiya kulliyya): A universal proposition or general maxim that applies to all relevant cases without exception.
لازم بالشرط (lazim bil-shart): Binding by stipulation: a condition that becomes obligatory to fulfill because it was agreed upon.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the two universal maxims, what is the ruling on a condition that does not contradict Shariah and may be voluntarily granted or waived?
A) It is void because it is not explicitly mentioned in the Quran.
B) It is binding and must be fulfilled.
C) It is valid but not enforceable in court.
D) It is void if it burdens the other party.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Qayyim's two universal maxims provide a clear framework: conditions contradicting Shariah are void; all other conditions are valid and binding if stipulated.

Defining Ta'liq: Suspension in Hanafi Contracts
Defines ta'liq with linguistic and philosophical precision

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of ta'liq, identify its linguistic structure, and apply the condition of 'non-existence with possibility of occurrence' to distinguish valid from invalid suspended contracts.

Core Idea
Ta'liq suspends a contract's existence on a future possible event; the condition must be non-existent and possible.

This passage from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Aam classifies contractual conditions into three types: ta'liq, taqyeed, and idafa. The author focuses on ta'liq, defining it rigorously: it ties the contract's very existence to a future uncertain event using conditional tools. For a mufti, understanding this definition is essential when evaluating contracts that include suspensive conditions, as misclassification can lead to incorrect fatwas.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of contractual conditions
Read the passage to locate the three types: ta'liq (suspension), taqyeed (restriction), and idafa (deferment). Note that only ta'liq suspends the existence of the contract.
The passage states: 'الثالث: الشرط الذي ليس فيه تعليق وجود العقد، ولا تعديل مقتضياته وأحكامه الأصلية، وإنما يقصد به تأخير سريان تلك الأحكام إلى زمن مستقبل معين.' This is idafa.
2
Define ta'liq and its opposite
Find the definition: ta'liq is 'ربط حصول أمر بحصول أمر آخر' (tying the occurrence of one matter to another). Its opposite is tanjiz (immediate effectiveness).
The passage contrasts ta'liq with tanjiz: 'فهو عكس التنجيز الذي يكون فيه العقد مطلقاً ساري الحكم منذ صدوره.'
3
Analyze the linguistic structure
Identify the components: a conditional tool (in, idha, mata, kullama) linking two sentences. The first is the condition (shart), the second is the consequence (jaza').
The author emphasizes that the order of sentences does not matter; only the linking by the tool matters.
4
Determine the validity requirement
The condition must be non-existent at the time of statement and possible of occurrence (ma'dum 'ala khatar al-wujud). It cannot be certain or already occurred.
The passage explicitly states: 'يجب أن يكون الأمر المعلق عليه، وهو الشرط، معدوماً على خطر الوجود أي معدوماً محتمل الوقوع.'
5
Apply to an example
Consider the kafala example: 'إن سافر مدينك فأنا كفيل بما لك عليه.' Verify that the travel is future and uncertain, making it valid ta'liq.
If the condition were 'إذا مات' (when he dies) - but death is certain - it would not be valid ta'liq in the true sense.

Chapter (43): Types of contractual conditions. This is called: restriction by condition as in the second example. And it may be called: conjunction with condition, and the contract is described with it as restricted by condition, or conjoined with it as opposed to the suspended contract. Third: the condition in which there is neither suspension of the existence of the contract nor modification of its original requirements and rulings, but rather it is intended to delay the effect of those rulings to a specific future time. And this is called: deferment to the future as in the third example. We will explain in the following sections of the research: First: a summary of the juristic views and considerations in each of these three types (suspension, restriction, and deferment). Second: the differences between the requirements of these types. Third: the capability of types of transactions and contracts for these types of conditions. First Section: Summary of juristic views on suspension, restriction, and deferment. Firstly: Suspension upon a condition 3/43: Indeed, suspension upon a condition, as indicated by the definitions of jurists, is: tying the occurrence of a matter to the occurrence of another matter. It is the opposite of immediate execution, wherein the contract is absolute and effective from its issuance. So when a person says to another, for example: "If your debtor travels, then I am a guarantor for what you have against him," the speaker has tied the formation of the guaranty to the realization of the debtor's travel. This is a suspension of the guaranty (1). 4/43: And suspension is usually formulated with one of the conditional tools that link two actions, such as "in" (if), "idha" (when), "mata" (whenever), and "kullama" (every time); because the matter upon which suspension is based consists of actual actions or events, and these conditional tools enter the verbal sentence and make the occurrence of the event contained therein a condition for the occurrence of the suspended conditioned matter. Thus, in suspension, there must be two sentences linked by a conditional tool. The sentence that the conditional tool enters is called: the condition or the condition sentence, and it indicates the matter upon which suspension is based; and the other sentence is called: the consequence, and it indicates the suspended creative matter of a contract or the like. And it is irrelevant which of the two sentences precedes or follows, such as: "If my specific goods arrive today, I have appointed you as my agent to sell them," or: "I appoint you as my agent to sell my specific goods if they arrive today." 5/43: And for the speech to be a suspension in the true sense in the terminology of jurists, it is required that the matter upon which suspension is based, i.e., the condition, be non-existent at risk of existence, i.e., non-existent and possible of occurrence: (1) The jurists define suspension as: tying the occurrence of the content of one sentence to the occurrence of the content of another sentence. Or as: ordering a thing that has not occurred upon a thing that has not occurred. See: al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir with the marginalia of al-Hamawi, al-Fann al-Thalith (2/224). In the mentioned example, the speaker has ordered the guaranty, which is the content of the second sentence, upon the travel of the debtor, which is the content of the first sentence; and both, i.e., the travel and the guaranty, have not materialized in existence; rather, the speaker committed to the guaranty upon the occurrence of the travel that is possible to occur. And the definition that we have extracted and adopted is the most concise and clear.
Source pages p. 543

Commentary
This passage from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Aam by Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa is a legal-philosophical exposition classifying contractual conditions (shurut 'aqdiyya). The author introduces three types: ta'liq (suspension), taqyeed (restriction), and idafa (deferment), but focuses here on defining ta'liq. He explains that ta'liq is the opposite of tanjiz (immediate execution); it ties the existence of a contract or disposition to a future event. The passage clarifies the linguistic structure: ta'liq uses conditional tools (in, idha, mata, kullama) to link two clauses—the condition (shart) and the consequence (jaza'). The author stresses that the condition must be non-existent at the time of the statement and possible of occurrence (ma'dum 'ala khatar al-wujud). He illustrates with the example of a guaranty (kafala) suspended on the debtor's travel. The footnote cites al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir, indicating the definition's roots in Hanafi legal maxims. This lesson is foundational for understanding how Hanafi jurists conceptualize conditional contracts, which is essential for fatwa issuance regarding suspended transactions.

Key Terms
التعليق (al-ta'liq): Rabi hukm al-'aqd bi hukm shart mustaqbil; tying the occurrence of a contract to the occurrence of a future condition.
التنجيز (al-tanjiz): The opposite of ta'liq; immediate effectiveness of a contract from its issuance without suspension.
الشرط (al-shart): In the context of ta'liq, the condition upon which the contract is suspended; must be a future uncertain event.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is required for a statement to be considered a valid ta'liq (suspension) in the terminology of Hanafi jurists?
A) The condition must be certain to occur in the future.
B) The condition must be a past event.
C) The condition must be non-existent at the time of statement and possible of occurrence.
D) The condition must be expressed using the word 'if' only.

Key Takeaway
Ta'liq is a valid contractual mechanism in the Hanafi school, defined by the requirement that the suspending condition be non-existent and possible at the time of the contract's formation.

Types of Contractual Conditions: Suspension, Restriction, and Deferment
Defines and distinguishes three types of contractual conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define ta‘līq, taqyīd, and iḍāfah, distinguish them based on their legal effects, and classify contractual statements accordingly.

Core Idea
The timing and existence of a contract depend on whether the condition is suspensive, restrictive, or deferring.

In Hanafi contract theory, not all conditions are the same. Some conditions suspend the very existence of the contract until a future event, others add an obligation to an already binding contract, and still others delay the legal effects while the contract itself is formed immediately. This lesson breaks down these three categories—ta‘līq, taqyīd, and iḍāfah—using definitions and examples from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-‘Āmm by Muṣṭafā Aḥmad al-Zarqā. Mastering this distinction is essential for analyzing contractual validity and obligations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three terms and their core definitions
From the passage, extract the definitions of ta‘līq (suspension), taqyīd (restriction), and iḍāfah (deferment). Note how each affects the existence and effect of a contract.
Focus on the key phrases: 'arranging something non-existent upon something non-existent' for ta‘līq, 'undertaking something non-existent in something already existent' for taqyīd, and 'delaying the ruling of a spoken disposition to a specified future time' for iḍāfah.
2
Distinguish iḍāfah from ta‘līq by linguistic form
The passage emphasizes that iḍāfah uses adverbial time expressions (e.g., 'from next month'), while ta‘līq uses conditional tools (e.g., 'if', 'when'). If a conditional tool is used, the statement becomes ta‘līq, not iḍāfah.
Example: 'I lease you this house from next month' = iḍāfah; 'When next month comes, I lease you this house' = ta‘līq.
3
Compare ta‘līq and taqyīd in terms of contract existence
In ta‘līq, the contract is non-existent until the condition is fulfilled. In taqyīd, the contract is existent and definitive (mubtāt) from the outset, but an additional obligation (ḥukm zā’id) is imposed.
E.g., a sale suspended on the arrival of a car: no sale yet. A sale with a condition to teach driving: the sale is binding immediately, and the seller must teach.
4
Analyze the example of a bequest (waṣiyyah) as iḍāfah
A will defers the transfer of ownership to after death without using a conditional tool. This is iḍāfah, not ta‘līq, because the will is made now but its effect is postponed to a known future event (death).
Contrast with a gift during life: no deferment.
5
Apply the distinctions to new contractual scenarios
Given a statement, determine which category it falls into and explain the legal consequence: is the contract formed now? When do obligations begin?
This prepares you for fatwa application where misclassification leads to incorrect rulings.

Chapter (43): Types of Contractual Conditions

Third: Deferment to the Future
8/43 - As for deferment (idaafah), it is: delaying the ruling of the spoken disposition (insha) to a specified future time, such as when the lessor says: 'I lease you this house for a year for such amount starting from the beginning of next month'; or the principal says: 'I appoint you as my agent in all my affairs from the beginning of the coming year.'
And the future time may be implied, so the disposition is deferred without explicit wording, as in a bequest (wasiyyah), for example the testator says: 'I bequeath one third of my wealth to so-and-so, or to such-and-such cause.' For the bequest indicates the meaning of deferment to after death. As for a completed donation during life, it is a gift (hibah) or charity (sadaqah).
Deferment is usually formulated by mentioning the time as an adverbial for the constructive disposition, as in the previous examples.
If the deferment is phrased as a suspension upon the arrival of the time, and some of the conditional suspension tools previously explained are used, it is considered pure suspension, not deferment, as in: 'When such month comes, I have leased you my house for a year for such amount.' Then it takes the rulings of suspension, not deferment, because the original formation of the contract then is suspended upon the arrival of the specified time and tied to it as the conditioned is tied to its condition, like suspending it on any other event, and it is not contracted for the present with the ruling delayed to a future time in the manner explained earlier in the technical meaning of deferment (see al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir by Ibn Nujaym, second art, Book of Divorce, vol. 1 p. 255).
Second Subsection: Differences between the Implications of Suspension, Restriction, and Deferment
9/43 - We have seen that suspension makes the suspended disposition dependent in its existence upon the condition on which it is suspended, which may or may not occur.
A - The implication of suspension is that the contract suspended by a condition, whatever its type, is non-existent before the occurrence of the condition upon which it is suspended.
B - As for restriction (taqyeed), its implication is that the contract restricted by a condition is considered existent and definitive between the parties, and only an additional ruling amending its original effect is undertaken within it; and the meaning of restriction indicates the existence of the restricted contract, as previously mentioned (para 43).
Accordingly, one who suspends a sale, for example, by saying to another: 'If my such car comes from its journey today, I have sold it to you for such amount,' and the addressee agrees, then by the very agreement of the parties the owner of the car did not intend to be a seller from the time of suspending the contract before its arrival that day, nor does anyone consider him a seller by initiating this suspension, especially since the car may not arrive at the specified time, so the condition on which the sale was suspended is not fulfilled.
As for one who restricts a sale with a condition, for example saying to a car owner: 'I bought this car from you for such amount on condition that you teach me its driving,' and the other agrees, then the parties are actually sellers and buyers through this restricted contract, and the sale is considered by their agreement as executed and definitive between them, and the seller becomes obligated to teach driving and obliged to fulfill his commitment (1).
C - As for deferment (idaafah), it resembles suspension in one aspect, because the ruling of the contract...
Hence the jurists say in distinguishing between suspension upon a condition and restriction by a condition: the first is arranging something that does not exist upon something that does not exist; the second is undertaking something that does not exist in something that already exists, as previously explained in the definition of suspension and restriction. And they said: The suspensive condition (shart ta'liqi) is that upon which the origin of the act is suspended, and the restrictive condition (shart taqyeedi) is decisiveness in the origin of the act and stipulating another matter in it. (See Hashiyat al-Hamawi 'ala al-Ashbah, third art, vol. 2 p. 225, quoting from Qawa'id al-Zarkashi).
Source pages p. 547

Commentary
This passage from Mustafa al-Zarqa's al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm explains three key concepts in contract theory: ta'liq (suspension), taqyeed (restriction), and idaafah (deferment). The author first defines idaafah as delaying the effect of a spoken disposition to a specified future time, giving examples from lease and agency. He notes that idaafah can be implicit, as in a will (wasiyyah), where the effect is deferred to after death. Crucially, he distinguishes idaafah from ta'liq: if the future time is phrased using conditional tools (e.g., 'if the month comes'), it becomes a pure suspension, not deferment. Then, in the second subsection, the author contrasts the three. Ta'liq makes the contract non-existent until the condition occurs. Taqyeed makes the contract existent and definitive, but with an additional obligation modifying its original effect. Idaafah resembles ta'liq in that the effect is not immediate, but unlike ta'liq, the contract itself is formed from the outset, only its consequences are delayed. The passage ends with a concise juristic formula: ta'liq is arranging something non-existent upon something non-existent; taqyeed is undertaking something non-existent in something already existent. This distinction is critical for understanding when a contract is binding and when obligations arise. The examples of a conditional sale and a restricted sale illustrate the practical difference. Hanafi jurists rely on this classification to determine the validity and timing of contracts.

Key Terms
التعليق (al-ta‘līq): جعل وجود التصرف مرتبطاً بشرط قد يوجد أو لا يوجد
التقييد (al-taqyīd): إلزام حكم زائد في عقد موجود مبتوت بين الطرفين
الإضافة (al-iḍāfah): تأخير حكم التصرف القولي المنشأ إلى زمن مستقبل معين

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the practical consequence when a lessor says: 'When such month comes, I have leased you my house for a year for such amount'?
A) The lease is an idaafah because it refers to a future time.
B) The lease is a ta‘liq because the conditional tool 'when' makes it suspended on the arrival of the month.
C) The lease is a taqyeed because the condition restricts the lease.
D) The lease is invalid because it combines deferment and suspension.

Key Takeaway
The distinction between suspension, restriction, and deferment determines when a contract is formed and when its effects commence.

Legal Philosophy and Theory
intermediate
6 min
Three Types of Conditions in Contracts: Suspension, Restriction, Addition
Defining and distinguishing three types of conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between ta'liq, taqyid, and idafa, and apply each classification to a contractual scenario to determine the legal effect under Hanafi fiqh.

Core Idea
A suspended contract is non-existent until the condition occurs; a restricted contract exists immediately with an added obligation; an added contract exists as a cause for a future effect.

In Hanafi contract theory, not all conditions are alike. This lesson unpacks three distinct types: ta'liq (suspension), taqyid (restriction), and idafa (addition). Each has a different effect on the existence and enforceability of the contract. Understanding these distinctions is essential for drafting contracts and issuing fatwas on contractual obligations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types
From the passage, list the three types of conditions: ta'liq, taqyid, and idafa. Note their Arabic names and core definitions.
2
Analyze the legal effect of each type
For each type, determine whether the contract is considered existent or non-existent at the time of the agreement. Ta'liq: non-existent. Taqyid: existent. Idafa: existent as a cause.
The key distinction is existence vs. non-existence of the contract.
3
Apply to the given examples
Work through the sale and emancipation examples from the passage. Confirm how each type affects the rights of the parties (e.g., can the car owner sell the car before the condition? Can the slave owner sell the slave?).
4
Formulate the Hanafi ruling
State the Hanafi position on each type: ta'liq is valid but suspends the contract; taqyid binds the contract and the condition; idafa creates an immediate cause for a future ruling.
Note the contrast with other schools where relevant.

ولكن شبهها بالتقييد أقوى لأنَّ المؤخّر فيها ليس هو أصل العقد كما في التعليق، بل حكمه فقط .
١٠/٤٣ - وعن هذا كان مقتضى الإضافة في نظر الفقهاء أن العقد المضاف ينعقد في الحال سبباً للحكم المستقبل، فهو عقد قائم بين الطرفين منذ إنشاء الإضافة كما في حالة التقييد وليس معدوماً للحال كما في التعليق .

أنه ١١/٤٣ - يتضح مما تقدم في حالة التعليق إذا وجد الشرط المعلق عليه فإنما يثبت الحكم المعلق مقتصراً لا مستنداً، أي إنما يعتبر موجوداً عند وجود الشرط لا منذ التعليق؛ إذ لو اعتبر له بعد تحقق الشرط (۱) مثل ذلك لو صيغت الإضافة بطريق التعليق على الزمن المستقبل كما لو قال المولى في المثال المذكور: إذا جاء غد فأنت حرا، فإنه يملك بيعه قبل حلول الغد، لأن الإضافة عندئذ تصبح تعليقاً يربط فيه أصل العقد بمجيء الزمن المستقبل (ر: ف ٤٦/ ٥٧٩ .(Y.
Chapter Six: Theory of Contracts and Related Matters in Fiqh. By the condition upon which it is suspended, which may exist or not. A - Suspension requires that the contract suspended by a condition, whatever its type, is non-existent before the occurrence of the condition upon which it is suspended. B - As for restriction, it requires that the contract restricted by a condition is considered existing and final between the two parties, and only an additional ruling modifying its original effect is undertaken within it; and the meaning of restriction indicates the existence of the restricted contract as previously mentioned (Section 43). Accordingly, whoever suspends a sale, for example, by saying to another, 'If my such-and-such car arrives today from its trip, I have sold it to you for such amount,' and the addressee agrees, then according to the agreement of the parties themselves, the owner of the car did not intend to be a seller from the time of suspending the contract before the car arrives on that day, and likewise no one considers him a seller by this suspension, especially since the car may not arrive at the specified time, and thus the condition upon which the sale was suspended is not fulfilled. As for the one who restricts the sale by a condition, for example by saying to a car owner, 'I have bought this car from you for such amount on the condition that you teach me its market,' and the other agrees, then the two parties become actual buyers and sellers by this restricted contract, and the sale is considered immediate and final by virtue of their agreement, and the seller becomes obligated to teach the market and is required to fulfill his obligation. (1) The jurists say in distinguishing between suspension by a condition and restriction by a condition: the first is the arrangement of a thing that does not exist upon a thing that does not exist, while the second is the undertaking of a thing that does not exist in a thing that has already existed, as previously explained in the definition of suspension and restriction. And they said: the suspensive condition is that upon which the origin of the act is suspended, and the restrictive condition is a determination with the origin of the act and a condition of another matter in it. C - As for addition, it resembles suspension in one aspect, because the ruling of the added contract is delayed in appearance, so it only begins in the specified future; and it resembles restriction in another aspect because the time to which it is added is certain to come and is not at risk of existence or non-existence like the condition upon which suspension is based. But its resemblance to restriction is stronger because the delayed element in it is not the origin of the contract as in suspension, but only its ruling. 10/43 - Based on this, the requirement of addition in the view of the jurists is that the added contract is concluded immediately as a cause for the future ruling. So it is a contract existing between the parties since the creation of the addition, as in the case of restriction, and not non-existent at present as in suspension. Accordingly, they ruled in emancipation that whoever adds the emancipation of his slave to the future, for example by saying to him, 'You are free tomorrow,' he no longer has the right to sell him despite his remaining a slave before tomorrow, because the cause of freedom has been concluded by this addition. Thus the emancipation is considered existing, but its ruling's appearance is delayed to a specified term, contrary to if he suspended his emancipation by saying, for example, 'If my son returns safely from his trip, you are free,' then despite the validity of the suspension, it is not considered a concluded cause for emancipation at present, but the master has the right to sell him before his son's return because the suspended matter is non-existence before the existence of the condition. 11/43 - It is clear from the above that in the case of suspension, if the condition upon which it is suspended occurs, the suspended ruling is established as a new creation, not as a reinforcement, i.e., it is considered existing at the time of the condition's occurrence, not from the time of suspension; for if it were considered after the condition's fulfillment, (1) similarly if the addition is formulated as a suspension on a future time, as if the master in the mentioned example says, 'If tomorrow comes, you are free,' then he has the right to sell him before the arrival of tomorrow, because the addition then becomes a suspension binding the origin of the contract to the arrival of the future time (See: Section 46/579).
Source pages p. 548

Commentary
This passage from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm by Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa presents a foundational classification of conditions in contracts within the Hanafi school. The author distinguishes three types: ta'liq (suspension), taqyid (restriction), and idafa (addition). The passage is a legal definition and comparison exercise. 

First, ta'liq: Here the contract is suspended on a future uncertain event. Before the condition occurs, the contract has no legal existence; it is 'adam (non-existence). The example given is a conditional sale: 'If my car arrives today, I have sold it to you.' Until the car arrives, there is no binding sale. The Hanafi view is that this is a valid suspension (ta'liq) but the contract is not yet operative. 

Second, taqyid: Here the contract is fully concluded and binding (mubtatt) from the moment of agreement, but an additional condition modifies its terms. The example: 'I buy this car on condition that you teach me its market.' The sale is immediate, and the seller has an obligation to teach. The condition does not suspend the contract's existence; it adds a restriction. The Hanafi jurists define taqyid as undertaking a thing that does not exist (the condition) in a thing that already exists (the contract). 

Third, idafa: This is a contract whose legal effect is postponed to a definite future time. It is like suspension in that the effect is delayed, but like restriction in that the contract itself is concluded immediately as a cause (sabab) for the future ruling. The example from emancipation: saying to a slave 'You are free tomorrow' creates an immediate cause for freedom, so the master cannot sell the slave, even though he remains a slave until tomorrow. In contrast, a suspended emancipation ('If my son returns, you are free') does not create a cause; the master can sell the slave before the condition occurs. 

The passage emphasizes that the key difference lies in whether the contract is considered existent at the time of the statement or not. For ta'liq, it is non-existent; for taqyid and idafa, it is existent. The Hanafi school uses these distinctions to determine ownership, rights, and obligations pending the condition or time.

Key Terms
التعليق (al-ta'liq): Suspending the existence of a contract on a future uncertain condition.
التقييد (al-taqyid): Completing the contract immediately with an additional modifying condition.
الإضافة (al-idafa): Creating the contract immediately while postponing its legal effect to a definite future time.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi classification, if a master says to his slave 'You are free tomorrow,' which type of condition is this and what is the ruling?
A) Ta'liq (suspension); the master can sell the slave before tomorrow.
B) Taqyid (restriction); the master must free the slave tomorrow and cannot sell.
C) Idafa (addition); the cause of freedom is created immediately, so the master cannot sell.
D) Idafa (addition); the slave is free immediately.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the classification of a condition as ta'liq, taqyid, or idafa determines whether the contract is existent, non-existent, or a cause, which in turn affects ownership and obligations.

The Correct Restrictive Condition in Hanafi Contracts: Definition and Binding Effect
Explanation of valid contractual conditions and the legal capacity of rescission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three categories of valid restrictive conditions in Hanafi law and apply the principle that the rescission of a contract mirrors the original in accepting conditions.

Core Idea
A contractual condition is binding if stipulated by Sharia, customary, or suitable; rescission mirrors the original contract.

The Hanafi school carefully distinguishes between conditions that are valid and binding and those that are corrupt (fasid) and invalidate a contract. This lesson clarifies the three types of valid restrictive conditions as defined by Al-Zarqa and examines the principle that rescission (faskh) is treated like the original contract in its capacity for conditions. Understanding these categories is essential for a mufti to advise on contractual stipulations in both classical and modern contexts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the bases for a valid restrictive condition
Recognize that a correct restrictive condition (al-shart al-taqyidi al-sahih) must be based on one of three grounds: stipulated by Sharia (e.g., khiyar), accepted by custom (urf), or suitable for the contract (mula'im) such as demanding a guarantor for deferred price.
A condition that does not fall into these categories is considered corrupt and may invalidate the contract.
2
Understand the principle of rescission (faskh) regarding conditions
According to the Hanafi jurists, the rescission of a contract (faskh) is treated like the original contract in its capacity to accept conditions of suspension (ta'liq), restriction (taqayyud), and addition (idafa). This is illustrated with examples from sale and lease.
This principle is considered a general juristic view, extending beyond sale and lease to all contracts.
3
Relate classical Hanafi positions to modern legal codes
The passage notes that while classical Hanafi ijtihad limited conditions, modern civil codes (e.g., Ottoman Code) now validate suspension, restriction, and addition in all contracts, aligning with views from the Hanbali school. This reduces the practical impact of classical restrictions for contemporary fatwa.
A mufti should be aware of both the classical theory and the operative legal framework.

Source Text

Chapter (43): Types of Contractual Conditions. With the correct restrictive condition, fulfillment of the obligations imposed by the condition is binding. (1) As for rescission of contracts, the apparent meaning from the statements of the jurists is that the rescission of every contract is considered like its original contract in its capacity to accept these conditions of suspension, restriction, and addition. They explicitly stated that the rescission of sale and lease accepts the conditions that their contracts accept (see: al-Durar 202/2). And this legal generalization is in agreement with some ijtihad opinions and schools in Islamic jurisprudence, such as the Hanbali school as previously explained (see: F 4/42 and beyond). (1) The explanation of the correct restrictive condition in the view of Hanafi ijtihad has preceded, which is a condition that has been stipulated by the Sharia, such as stipulating an option in sale, or is accepted by custom, or is suitable for the contract, such as the seller stipulating on the buyer to provide a guarantor or a pledge for the deferred price, for in this condition there is securing of the contractual obligations, and the contract is suitable for securing (see: F 3/42). Commentary
This passage is a discussion on the types of contractual conditions in Hanafi jurisprudence. It begins by affirming that a correct restrictive condition (shart ta'yidi sahih) is binding and must be fulfilled. The author then addresses the rescission of contracts, stating that according to the Hanafi jurists, rescission of a contract (faskh) is treated like the original contract in terms of its ability to accept conditions such as suspension (ta'liq), restriction (taqayyud), and addition (idafa). This is illustrated by explicit statements regarding sale and lease. The author opines that this is a general principle applicable to all contracts, not just sale and lease. The passage then summarizes the effect of conditions, noting that while Hanafi ijtihad historically recognized certain conditions, modern civil codes (like the Ottoman Code) now generally validate suspension, restriction, and addition in all contracts, aligning with some other Islamic schools like the Hanbali. Footnotes clarify what constitutes a correct restrictive condition (e.g., options in sale, customary conditions, or conditions suitable for the contract like demanding a guarantor or pledge) and provide references to major Hanafi works where the theory is elaborated, noting some confusion in those texts.

Key Terms
الشرط التقييدي الصحيح (al-shart al-taqyidi al-sahih): A condition that is valid and legally binding, which has been stipulated by Sharia, accepted by custom, or is suitable for the contract (e.g., stipulating an option or demanding a guarantor).
فسخ (faskh): Rescission of a contract, which in Hanafi law is considered analogous to the original contract in its capacity to accept conditions of suspension, restriction, and addition.
تعليق (ta'liq): Suspending the effect of a contract or condition on a future event.

C) It only accepts conditions related to the original price.
D) It is considered a new contract that must be free of all conditions.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a contractual condition is valid and binding if based on Sharia, custom, or suitability; rescission mirrors the original contract; modern codes have generalized this, reducing the classical restrictions.

The Principle of Contractual Will: Definition and Limits
Definition and limitation of contractual will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the principle of contractual will and identify the limit that legislation imposes on it.

Core Idea
Contractual will is supreme but bounded by law – a balance between freedom and public interest.

This lesson introduces a key concept from Western jurisprudence that a mufti may encounter when dealing with contracts influenced by foreign legal systems. The principle of contractual will (سلطان الإرادة العقدية) asserts the primacy of party autonomy, but with constraints imposed by legislation for the sake of individual and social interests.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core claim
What does the principle state about who has authority in creating a contract and defining its obligations?
The text says: 'إن السلطان الأصلي في إنشاء العقد وفي تحديد التزاماته إنما هو لإرادة العاقدين'
2
Identify the limit
What restricts that authority? The text mentions 'ولكن في حدود معينة يحددها التشريع'.
The limit is determined by legislation according to various interests.
3
Recognize the basis of limits
On what basis does legislation set these limits? The text lists: individual, social, economic, and political interests.
These are the same types of considerations a mufti uses when applying public interest (maslaha).

Chapter Forty-Four: The Authority of Contractual Will in Foreign Jurisprudence. First Section: Origin of the Theory and its Development. 1/44 - The legal principle that has finally been settled in modern foreign jurisprudence in Europe regarding the authority of contractual will is summarized as follows: The original authority in creating the contract and in determining its obligations belongs to the will of the contracting parties, but within certain limits determined by legislation according to individual, social, economic, and political interests. And contractual will in Europe remained paralyzed and shackled by formalities and narrow restrictions throughout ancient times and many centuries of the Middle Ages, until it began to be freed little by little, and authority was gradually established for it at the end of the Middle Ages. Then in recent times, the principle of the authority of the will in all its aspects came to dominate social and legislative ideas, especially in the era of the French Revolution, which was influenced by a special revolutionary philosophy on all social conditions, and the illusory social contract theory advocated by Rousseau spread, so that the era became saturated with the spirit of individual freedom and the independence of the individual's will, considering it a source for all aspects of social life, and that was a gust and impetus to escape the heaviness of the dark ages in Europe. And these ideas of extreme freedom gave birth to a legal theory in
Source pages p. 554

Commentary
This passage opens Chapter 44, discussing the principle of contractual will (sultan al-irada al-'aqdiyya) in Western jurisprudence. It begins by stating the settled modern principle: the primary authority in creating and defining contractual obligations belongs to the will of the parties, but limited by legislation for broader interests. The author then traces the historical development: contractual will was restricted by formalities in ancient and medieval times, gradually freed at the end of the Middle Ages, and fully dominated in modern times, especially after the French Revolution influenced by Rousseau's social contract theory and the spirit of individual freedom.

Key Terms
سلطان الإرادة العقدية (sultan al-irada al-'aqdiyya): The principle that the will of the contracting parties is the primary source of authority in creating contracts and defining their obligations, subject to legal limits.
العقد الاجتماعي (al-'aqd al-ijtima'i): The social contract theory, particularly as advocated by Rousseau, which influenced the principle of contractual will.

Key Takeaway
The principle of contractual will is fundamental in Western law but always subject to legislative constraints for public interest.

Historical Development of Contractual Will in Europe
Historical trajectory of contractual will in Europe.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe the historical phases of contractual will in Europe and identify factors that led to its dominance.

Core Idea
From shackled to supreme: contractual will was freed gradually, culminating in the French Revolution's individualist philosophy.

This lesson traces the evolution of contractual freedom in European legal history. A mufti benefits from understanding this background to appreciate the context of modern contract law and to better analyze contracts that carry the legacy of this development.

Guided Path
1
Note the initial state
What was the state of contractual will in ancient and medieval times? The text says it was 'paralyzed and shackled by formalities and narrow restrictions'.
This indicates that party autonomy was heavily constrained.
2
Identify the turning point
When did contractual will begin to be freed? At the end of the Middle Ages, gradually.
The text says 'بدأت تتحرر شيئاً فشيئاً، ويقوم لها سلطان بالتدريج أواخر العصور الوسطى'.
3
Highlight the French Revolution influence
What event and philosophy caused the principle to dominate? The French Revolution, influenced by Rousseau's social contract theory and extreme ideas of individual freedom.
The text says the era became saturated with the spirit of individual freedom.
4
Recognize the result
What was the outcome? The principle of contractual will came to dominate social and legislative ideas.
This set the stage for modern Western contract law.

The Western Theory of Contractual Will: Origins and Core Doctrine
Exposition of a foreign legal theory's origin and doctrine

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the core doctrine of the authority of contractual will in Western law and identify its historical roots in the French Revolution and social contract theory.

Core Idea
The Western principle made the individual's abstract will the sole source of all legal obligations.

This passage introduces a foreign legal theory that once dominated European jurisprudence: the principle that contractual will is supreme. You will learn how this idea evolved from ancient restrictions to unprecedented freedom during the French Revolution, and its philosophical basis in Rousseau's social contract. Understanding this theory is essential to later contrast it with the Hanafi approach.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal principle
Read the first paragraph (1/44). State the principle in one short sentence: the contracting parties' will is the original authority in creating and determining the contract, but within legislative limits.
Note the contradiction: despite claiming absolute will, the theory itself acknowledges legislative limits.
2
Trace the historical evolution
From ancient times to the late Middle Ages, contractual will was 'crippled and shackled'. Notice the turning point at the French Revolution. Why did liberation occur then?
The author emphasizes that this was a reaction against oppression, not an inherent legal development.
3
Understand the social contract basis
The theory rests on Rousseau's idea that individuals voluntarily joined a legal community, thereby consenting to all its laws. This makes even imposed obligations seem voluntary.
This is described as 'illusory' (وهمية) by the author, hinting at its flaw from an Islamic perspective.
4
Summarize the extreme formulation
The pure will of the contracting parties alone creates the contract and determines its effects, without dependence on anything else. This is the core of absolute contractual will.
This extreme version was the target of later critiques.

Source Text

إن السلطان الأصلي في إنشاء العقد وفي تحديد التزاماته إنما هو لإرادة العاقدين، ولكن في حدود معينة يحددها التشريع وفقاً للمصالح الفردية والاجتماعية والاقتصادية والسياسية.
وقد ظلت الإرادة العقدية في أوروبة شلاء مغلولة بالشكليات والقيود الضيقة طوال العصور القديمة، وقروناً عديدة من العصور الوسطى، حتى بدأت تتحرر شيئاً فشيئاً، ويقوم لها سلطان بالتدريج أواخر العصور الوسطى .
ثم كان في العصور الأخيرة لمبدأ سلطان الإرادة بكامل نواحيه سيادة في الأفكار الاجتماعية والتشريعية وخاصة في عصر الثورة الفرنسية الذي كان متأثراً بفلسفة خاصة ثورية على سائر الأوضاع الاجتماعية، وتفشت فيه نظرية العقد الاجتماعي الوهمية التي نادى بها روسو» إذ ذاك، حتى أصبح العصر مشبعاً بروح الحرية الفردية واستقلال إرادة الفرد، واعتبارها مصدراً لكل نواحي الحياة الاجتماعية وكان ذلك هبوباً واندفاعاً للخروج من وطأة العصور المظلمة في أوروبة.
فهذه الأفكار في الحرية المتطرفة قد تمخضت عن نظرية قانونية في
الباب السادس : نظرية العقود وما إليها في الفقه المصدر
مبدأ سلطان الإرادة تقوم على أساس أن الإرادة المجردة للشخص هي الوحيد لكل الحقوق والواجبات فإليها ترجع وبها تحدد وتفسّر جميع الالتزامات العقدية ،وغيرها حتى الالتزامات التي مصدرها القانون في شؤون الأسرة والمصالح العامة لأن الفرد يعتبر قد قبل هذه الالتزامات باختياره حينما ضوى (۱) طوعاً إلى مجتمع قانوني يفرض على أفراده واجبات وتبعات .
ومن فروع ذلك أن إرادة العاقدين المحضة هي التي توجد العقد دون توقف على شيء آخر وهي التي يجب أن تحدد آثاره وأحكامه.
٢/٤٤ - ثم هوجمت هذه النظرية بردود قوية متينة فندتها تفنيداً على أيدي رجال المذهب الاشتراكي، ورجال مذهب التضامن الاجتماعي، وغيرهما من المذاهب المناهضة لمذهب الفردية» المتطرف. فنكص على عقبيه مبدأ سلطان الإرادة التعاقدية بين رجال المذاهب الاجتماعية والحقوقية ؛ ولا سيما أن نظرية سلطان الإرادة المطلق تحول دون تقرير مبدأ مسؤولية الطفل والمجنون ومبدأ المسؤولية المادية (۲)، مما اتجهت القوانين الحديثة إلى اعتباره ناحية أساسية في نظام العدالة .
(1) يقال ضوى يضوي ضُوِياً (بضم الضاد في المصدر وكسر الواو وتشديد الياء) وزان مضى يمضي مُضِيّاً إذا انضم إلى جماعة أو مكان ملتجناً (ر : القاموس المحيط). (۲) نظرية المسؤولية المادية responsabilite objective تجعل الإنسان مسؤولاً عن كل ضرر لغيره ينشأ مما في ملكه أو تحت حراسته من الأشياء، ولو لم يكن عن خطأ منه أو تقصير وهي نظرية حديثة تحدوها عوامل اشتراكية غلا فيها أصحابها لحماية العمال من أخطار المعامل الآلية العصرية. وقد جاءت معاكسة لنظرية المسؤولية الشخصية respond subjective التي بنيت عليها المادة / ۱۳۸۲/ وما بعدها من القانون المدني الفرنسي، وهي تشترط لمسؤولية المالك أو الحارس ثبوت الخطأ أو التقصير منه . والقوانين الحديثة تتجه إلى الأخذ بنظرية المسؤولية المادية في نطاق محدود كتشريع العمل، وبقيود تحفظية ..
كولان وكابيتان ج ٢ / ٢٨٩/ و / ٣٧٠ ، و الموجز في نظرية الالتزامات للأستاذ السنهوري / ٣٧٣ - ٣٧٥/ ، و القانون المدني المقارن للأستاذ محسن شفيق ص ١٢٤ - ١٢٧).
Chapter Forty-Four: The Authority of Contractual Will in Foreign Jurisprudence. First Section: Origin and Development of the Theory. 1/44 - The legal principle that has recently settled in modern Western jurisprudence in Europe regarding the authority of contractual will can be summarized as follows: The original authority in creating the contract and in determining its obligations belongs to the will of the contracting parties, but within certain limits set by legislation according to individual, social, economic, and political interests. Contractual will in Europe remained crippled and shackled by formalities and narrow restrictions throughout ancient times and many centuries of the Middle Ages, until it began to liberate gradually, and its authority gradually emerged at the end of the Middle Ages. Then, in recent times, the principle of the authority of will in all its aspects gained dominance in social and legislative ideas, especially during the era of the French Revolution, which was influenced by a particular revolutionary philosophy against all social conditions, and the illusory theory of the social contract advocated by Rousseau spread widely, so that the era became saturated with the spirit of individual freedom and independence of the individual's will, considering it the source of all aspects of social life. This was a burst and drive to escape the oppression of the dark ages in Europe. These ideas of extreme freedom gave birth to a legal theory in Chapter Six: Theory of Contracts and Related Matters in Jurisprudence: The Principle of the Authority of Will is based on the idea that the abstract will of a person is the sole source of all rights and duties; to it they return, and by it all contractual and other obligations are determined and interpreted, even obligations originating from law in family matters and public interests, because the individual is considered to have accepted these obligations by choice when he voluntarily joined a legal community that imposes duties and responsibilities on its members. Among the consequences of this is that the pure will of the contracting parties alone creates the contract without dependence on anything else, and it must determine its effects and rulings. 2/44 - Then this theory was attacked with strong, solid refutations that demolished it at the hands of supporters of the socialist school, the solidarity school, and other schools opposing extreme individualism. Thus the principle of absolute contractual will retreated among proponents of social and legal schools, especially since the theory of absolute will prevents the establishment of the principle of responsibility of children and the insane and the principle of objective responsibility (2), which modern laws have tended to consider a fundamental aspect of the justice system. (1) The word ضوى means to join a group or place seeking refuge. (2) The theory of objective responsibility makes a person responsible for any harm to others arising from things in his ownership or under his custody, even if there is no fault or negligence on his part. It is a modern theory driven by socialist factors, whose proponents exaggerated it to protect workers from the dangers of modern machinery. It contradicts the theory of subjective responsibility upon which Article 1382 et seq. of the French Civil Code is based, which requires proof of fault or negligence on the part of the owner or custodian. Modern laws tend to adopt objective responsibility within a limited scope, such as labor legislation, with protective restrictions.
Source pages p. 554

Commentary
This passage is a comparative legal study presenting the Western theory of contractual will (سلطان الإرادة العقدية). The author, Dr. Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, outlines the historical evolution of this principle in European law, from its early restrictions to its extreme dominance during and after the French Revolution, influenced by Rousseau's social contract theory. The theory holds that the will of the contracting parties is the exclusive source of contract creation and obligation determination, even extending to obligations imposed by law. The author then presents the powerful critiques from socialist and solidarity schools, which condemned the absolute will theory for preventing legal responsibility for children, the insane, and objective liability (responsabilité objective). The text shows that modern legal systems have moved away from absolute contractual freedom toward more balanced approaches. This sets the stage for comparing with Islamic jurisprudence, which will be discussed in subsequent sections. The passage demonstrates al-Zarqa's methodology of presenting foreign legal theories before analyzing them from an Islamic perspective.

Key Terms
سلطان الإرادة العقدية (sulṭān al-irādah al-ʿaqdiyyah): المبدأ القانوني الذي يجعل إرادة المتعاقدين هي المصدر الأساسي لإنشاء العقد وتحديد التزاماته، ضمن حدود يضعها التشريع.
نظرية العقد الاجتماعي (naẓariyyat al-ʿaqd al-ijtimāʿī): نظرية فلسفية غربية تزعم أن الأفراد اختاروا طواعية الانضمام إلى المجتمع القانوني، مما يلزمهم بجميع أحكامه.
المسؤولية المادية (al-masʾūliyyah al-mādiyyah): نظرية قانونية حديثة تجعل الإنسان مسؤولاً عن الأضرار الناشئة عن الأشياء تحت يده ولو بغير خطأ.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What historical event is credited with the rise of the absolute contractual will theory?
A) The Industrial Revolution
B) The French Revolution
C) The Napoleonic Code
D) The Reformation

Key Takeaway
The Western theory of contractual will made individual will the source of all obligations, but it was later refuted for preventing essential legal responsibilities.

Legal Philosophy and Theory
intermediate
8 min
Critique of Absolute Contractual Will and the Hanafi Alternative
Presentation of critiques and implications for justice

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the critiques of absolute contractual will and explain why Hanafi jurisprudence restricts contractual freedom with Shariah and custom.

Core Idea
Absolute contractual will was demolished for blocking justice; Hanafi law balances will with divine guidance.

The previous lesson introduced the Western theory of contractual will. Here we examine the powerful critiques that destroyed its absolute version, and we begin to see the outline of the Hanafi alternative. You will learn how socialist and solidarity schools attacked the theory for preventing responsibility for the vulnerable, and how modern laws have adopted objective responsibility. This sets the stage for understanding the Hanafi approach, which gives will a role but always within the bounds of Shariah and public interest.

Guided Path
1
Identify the attacking schools
Read paragraph 2/44. Name the two main schools that attacked the theory: the socialist school (المذهب الاشتراكي) and the solidarity school (مذهب التضامن الاجتماعي).
These schools are described as opposing extreme individualism (الفردية المتطرف).
2
Understand why absolute will is problematic
The text says absolute will 'prevents the establishment of the principle of responsibility of children and the insane and the principle of objective responsibility'. Explain why: if only will creates obligations, those who cannot consent (children, insane) cannot be held responsible; also, harm from objects without fault cannot lead to liability.
This is a practical consequence that modern justice considers unacceptable.
3
Define objective responsibility
From the footnote, objective responsibility (المسؤولية المادية) holds a person liable for harm caused by things under his control, even without fault. Contrast with subjective responsibility requiring fault.
Modern laws adopt this in limited contexts like labor accidents.
4
Anticipate the Hanafi position
Using the key terms and your knowledge, state how Hanafi jurisprudence would limit contractual will: by Shariah (e.g., prohibitions of riba, gharar) and by custom (urf). Also, Hanafi law already has principles of liability (daman) that cover objective-like cases (e.g., ghasp).
This is not directly in the text but is implied by the comparative context.

Autonomy of Will in Western Law – A Comparative Islamic Perspective
Comparative legal philosophy explaining a Western principle and its critique

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the principle of autonomy of will, explain its historical trajectory, and contrast it with the Hanafi view on contractual freedom subject to sharīʿah.

Core Idea
Autonomy of will claims the individual's will creates obligations; Islamic law subjects that will to divine limits.

This passage from Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa's introduction explains a key Western legal principle that is often implicitly contrasted with Islamic jurisprudence. Understanding the autonomy of will helps you appreciate why Islamic contract law, while recognizing freedom, imposes binding ethical and revelatory constraints. The passage traces the principle from its extreme form, through criticism, to its moderate modern version, and shows how different areas of law restrict it. As a Hanafi student, this background equips you to articulate the unique features of Islamic contract theory.

Guided Path
1
Define the principle
Identify the key claim: the abstract will is the sole source of rights and obligations. Note that in Hanafi fiqh, the source is the sharīʿah, though the will is recognized in forming contracts (ījāb and qabūl).
2
Examine the critique
The passage states that absolute autonomy prevents doctrines like responsibility of the child/insane and objective responsibility. Understand why this critique is powerful: without limits, the weak are unprotected.
In Hanafi law, the insane and minors have limited capacity; their contracts are void or voidable.
3
Trace the moderation
The French Civil Code articles show the tension: contract as law vs. contract subject to justice and custom. How does this parallel the Hanafi concept that contracts must not violate divine law (e.g., prohibiting ribā)?
4
Categorize the limits
Public rights: no will. Marriage: will only for entry. Property: broader but still restricted. Compare with Hanafi classifications: nikāḥ has prescribed effects, bayʻ has detailed shurūṭ.
5
Synthesize with Hanafi view
Conclude that both systems limit will, but Islamic law derives limits from revelation, while Western law uses justice, custom, and public policy.

الباب السادس : نظرية العقود وما إليها في الفقه

الفصل (٤٤) سلطان الإرادة في الفقه الأجنبي

المصدر
مبدأ سلطان الإرادة تقوم على أساس أن الإرادة المجردة للشخص هي الوحيد لكل الحقوق والواجبات فإليها ترجع وبها تحدد وتفسّر جميع الالتزامات العقدية ،وغيرها حتى الالتزامات التي مصدرها القانون في شؤون الأسرة والمصالح العامة لأن الفرد يعتبر قد قبل هذه الالتزامات باختياره حينما ضوى (۱) طوعاً إلى مجتمع قانوني يفرض على أفراده واجبات وتبعات .
ومن فروع ذلك أن إرادة العاقدين المحضة هي التي توجد العقد دون توقف على شيء آخر وهي التي يجب أن تحدد آثاره وأحكامه.

٢/٤٤ - ثم هوجمت هذه النظرية بردود قوية متينة فندتها تفنيداً على أيدي رجال المذهب الاشتراكي، ورجال مذهب التضامن الاجتماعي، وغيرهما من المذاهب المناهضة لمذهب الفردية» المتطرف. فنكص على عقبيه مبدأ سلطان الإرادة التعاقدية بين رجال المذاهب الاجتماعية والحقوقية ؛ ولا سيما أن نظرية سلطان الإرادة المطلق تحول دون تقرير مبدأ مسؤولية الطفل والمجنون ومبدأ المسؤولية المادية (۲)، مما اتجهت القوانين الحديثة إلى اعتباره ناحية أساسية في نظام العدالة .
(1) يقال ضوى يضوي ضُوِياً (بضم الضاد في المصدر وكسر الواو وتشديد الياء) وزان مضى يمضي مُضِيّاً إذا انضم إلى جماعة أو مكان ملتجناً (ر : القاموس المحيط). (۲) نظرية المسؤولية المادية responsabilite objective تجعل الإنسان مسؤولاً عن كل ضرر لغيره ينشأ مما في ملكه أو تحت حراسته من الأشياء، ولو لم يكن عن خطأ منه أو تقصير وهي نظرية حديثة تحدوها عوامل اشتراكية غلا فيها أصحابها لحماية العمال من أخطار المعامل الآلية العصرية. وقد جاءت معاكسة لنظرية المسؤولية الشخصية respond subjective التي بنيت عليها المادة / ۱۳۸۲/ وما بعدها من القانون المدني الفرنسي، وهي تشترط لمسؤولية المالك أو الحارس ثبوت الخطأ أو التقصير منه . والقوانين الحديثة تتجه إلى الأخذ بنظرية المسؤولية المادية في نطاق محدود كتشريع العمل، وبقيود تحفظية ..

ثم ما لبث مبدأ سلطان الإرادة أن استعاد اعتباره على أسس جديدة. وهكذا تردَّد مبدأ سلطان الإرادة بين جزر ،ومد، وأخذ ورد، وانتكاس وانتعاش، حتى استقر أخيراً على تلك النظرية المعتدلة التي رسمناها آنفاً صدر الكلام.

في
٣/٤٤ - وكانت المادتان / ۱۱۳٤ و ۱۱۳۵ من القانون المدني الفرنسي متمسكاً قانونياً للجميع :
- وقد تمسك فريق المتطرفين في مبدأ سلطان الإرادة بأولاهما التي تنص على أنَّ العقد هو قانون في حق المتعاقدين.
- وتمسك الفريق الآخر بعد ذلك بالمادة التالية التي تصرّح بأن إلزام المتعاقدين بما تضمنه عقدهما مقيّد بما تقتضيه العدالة والعرف، وبما يقرره القانون من أحكام، وفقاً لطبيعة الالتزام.
فلا مجال إذن للقول بأنّ إرادة المتعاقدين وحدها هي التي تنشىء الالتزامات وتحدّد ،آثارها بل يجب أن تخضع لحدود يتولى الشارع تعيينها.

٤/٤٤ - وهذه الحدود والقيود التي يقيد بها التشريع مبدأ سلطان الإرادة تختلف باختلاف أنواع الحقوق والعقود، وموضوعاتها :
-
ففي الحقوق العامة والالتزامات الناشئة عنها لا سلطان لإرادة الملتزم أصلاً، بل للقانون وحده.
ب - وفي عقد الزواج ونتائجه ينحصر سلطان إرادة العاقدين في أصل العقد أي في قبول التعاقد أو رفضه بمحض حريتهما.
أما القيود الشكلية في عقد الزواج، وكذا آثاره والتزاماته بين الزوجين فيرتبها القانون على وفق ما يقتضيه تنظيم شؤون الأسرة والمصالح الاجتماعية التي مرجع تقديرها إلى الشارع.
ج - وفي الحقوق العينية العقارية يتّسع مجال سلطان إرادة العاقدين
Chapter Six: Theory of Contracts and What Pertains to It in Jurisprudence.

Chapter (44): Autonomy of Will in Foreign Jurisprudence.

Source:
The principle of autonomy of will is based on the idea that the abstract will of a person is the sole source of all rights and obligations; to it they revert, and by it all contractual obligations and others are determined and interpreted, even obligations that originate from law in matters of family and public interests, because the individual is considered to have accepted these obligations voluntarily when he willingly joined a legal community that imposes duties and burdens on its members.
Among its branches is that the mere will of the contracting parties creates the contract without depending on anything else, and it must determine its effects and rulings.

2/44 - Then this theory was attacked with strong, solid responses that refuted it thoroughly at the hands of proponents of the socialist school, the social solidarity school, and other schools opposing the extremist individualism school. Thus the principle of contractual autonomy of will retreated among proponents of social and legal schools; especially since the theory of absolute autonomy of will prevents the establishment of the principle of responsibility of the child and the insane, and the principle of objective responsibility (2), which modern laws have tended to consider a fundamental aspect of the justice system.
(1) It is said: ḍawā yaḍwī ḍuwwiyan (with ḍammah on the ḍād in the infinitive, kasrah on the wāw, and shaddah on the yā'), like maḍā yamḍī muḍiyyan, when one joins a group or a place seeking refuge. (2) The theory of objective responsibility makes a person responsible for any harm to others arising from things in his ownership or under his custody, even if there is no fault or negligence on his part. It is a modern theory driven by socialist factors exaggerated by its proponents to protect workers from the dangers of modern machine operations. It came in opposition to the theory of subjective responsibility on which Article 1382 and following of the French Civil Code were based, which requires proof of fault or negligence on the part of the owner or custodian for their responsibility. Modern laws tend to adopt the theory of objective responsibility within a limited scope, such as labor legislation, and with protective restrictions.

Then the principle of autonomy of will soon regained its standing on new foundations. Thus the principle of autonomy of will oscillated between ebb and flow, acceptance and rejection, relapse and revival, until it finally settled on that moderate theory that we outlined earlier at the beginning of the discussion.

3/44 - Articles 1134 and 1135 of the French Civil Code were a legal support for all:
- The extremist group regarding the principle of autonomy of will clung to the first one, which states that the contract is law for the contracting parties.
- The other group thereafter clung to the following article, which declares that the obligation of the contracting parties to what their contract contains is restricted by what justice and custom require, and by the provisions determined by law, according to the nature of the obligation.
Thus there is no room to say that the will of the contracting parties alone creates obligations and determines their effects; rather, it must submit to limits that the Lawgiver sets.

4/44 - These limits and restrictions with which legislation restricts the principle of autonomy of will vary according to the types of rights, contracts, and their subjects:
- In public rights and the obligations arising from them, there is no authority of the will of the obligated person at all; rather, the law alone has authority.
B - In the marriage contract and its consequences, the authority of the will of the contracting parties is limited to the contract itself, i.e., accepting or rejecting the contract freely.
As for the formal restrictions in the marriage contract, as well as its effects and obligations between the spouses, the law arranges them according to what is required by organizing family affairs and social interests, the evaluation of which is referred to the Lawgiver.
C - In real property rights, the scope of the authority of the will of the contracting parties expands.
Source pages p. 555

Commentary
This passage is a comparative legal philosophy discussion from Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa's introduction to Islamic jurisprudence. It opens by explaining the Western principle of 'autonomy of will' (سلطان الإرادة), which posits that an individual's abstract will is the sole source of rights and obligations. The text then describes the historical attack on this principle by socialist and social solidarity schools, who argued that absolute autonomy prevents necessary doctrines like the responsibility of children and the insane, and objective responsibility. The text then notes the principle's eventual moderation, referencing the French Civil Code articles 1134 and 1135 to show the tension between absolute will and limits imposed by justice, custom, and law. Finally, the text categorizes different areas of law where the autonomy of will is restricted: in public rights, law alone governs; in marriage, the parties' will is limited to entering the contract while its effects are regulated by law; in real property, the parties' will has a broader scope. This discussion serves as a backdrop to contrast with Islamic jurisprudence, which has its own principles of contract and obligation.

Key Terms
سلطان الإرادة (sulṭān al-irādah): The principle that an individual's abstract will is the sole source of rights and obligations, and that the mere will of contracting parties creates and determines the contract's effects without external interference.
العقد (al-ʿaqd): A contract, defined here as an agreement arising from the will of the parties that creates legal obligations.
المسؤولية المادية (al-masʾūliyyah al-mādiyyah): Objective responsibility: making a person liable for harm caused by things in his possession even without fault, as opposed to subjective responsibility requiring fault.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what was the outcome of the historical debate on autonomy of will?
A) It was completely rejected and replaced by objective responsibility.
B) It was adopted moderately with limits based on justice, custom, and law.
C) It remained extreme and unchanged.
D) It was replaced by socialist legal theory.

Key Takeaway
The autonomy of will is not absolute in any mature legal system; Islamic jurisprudence provides a coherent framework for limiting contractual freedom in light of divine law.

Legal Philosophy and Theory

Limits of Party Autonomy in Contract Law: The Hanafi Position
Comparative analysis of autonomy of will in Western and Islamic legal traditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the boundaries of party autonomy in Hanafi contract law and apply them to distinguish permissible contractual freedom from impermissible agreements.

Core Idea
Party autonomy is wide but bounded by Shari'ah: the contract cannot override explicit prohibitions or mandatory obligations.

This passage compares the Western legal principle of 'autonomy of will' with its limitations as seen in French law. As a Hanafi jurist, al-Zarqa uses this comparison to underscore that Islamic law also grants broad contractual freedom, but always within the boundaries set by divine legislation. In this lesson, we extract the Hanafi stance on when party will is sovereign and when it must yield to higher legal norms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principle of autonomy of will
Understand that 'autonomy of will' means parties can create legal obligations at will. In Western law, this is the principle that contracts are law for the parties.
This principle is accepted in Hanafi fiqh but with important qualifications.
2
Recognize the limits in French law
Al-Zarqa shows that French law itself restricts autonomy via public order, good morals, and mandatory legal texts (e.g., Articles 1134-1135).
These categories parallel Islamic prohibitions and mandatory rules.
3
Translate to Hanafi fiqh
In Hanafi fiqh, the same boundaries exist: contracts must not involve haram (prohibited), must not violate public interest (maslahah), and must not contradict explicit nass (Qur'an and Sunnah).
The Hanafi maxim 'al-aqd qanun al-muta'aqidayn' is conditioned by these limits.
4
Apply to a concrete example
Consider a contract to sell alcohol: despite mutual agreement, it is void (batil) because the subject is haram. Party will cannot override explicit prohibition.
This illustrates that autonomy is not absolute; it is bounded by Shari'ah.

الباب السادس : نظرية العقود وما إليها في الفقه
في آثار العقد. أما تكوينه فيخضع لمراسم شكلية في التسجيل يفرضها القانون، لارتباطها بسياسة التشريع في تنظيم الملكية العقارية، وفي الضرائب الحكومية عليها، وغير ذلك من الاعتبارات .
د - أما الحقوق الشخصية والالتزامات فهي الميدان الأوسع لمبدأ سلطان الإرادة العقدية ضمن حدود الآداب العامة، والنظام العام، والنصوص القانونية الخاصة عندما تكون آمرة بحكم إلزامي. فلا يصح الاستئجار على ارتكاب جريمة، أو على فعل ما ينافي الآداب الاجتماعية .
مثلاً عقد
ففي هذا الميدان وهذه الحدود يعقد الإنسان ما يشاء من العقود، ويحدد آثار عقده بإرادته المحضة.
- وللعاقدين أن يخالفا باتفاقهما ما رتبه القانون من أحكام العقود المسماة، لأن الأصل فيها أنها غير إلزامية وإنما يفرضها القانون عند عدم الاتفاق على خلافها إلا ما كان من النصوص الأمرة التي ينص القانون أو يقوم فيه دليل على عدم جواز اتفاق المتعاقدين على خلافها (۱).
وفي نطاق هذه المجموعة من القيود والحدود التشريعية يطبق المبدأ القانوني العام القائل : إنَّ العقد قانون المتعاقدين؛ أي إنّ العقد يلزم عاقديه في كل ما اتفقا عليه كما يلزم القانون سائر الناس. (ر: «نظرية العقد» للأستاذ العلامة السنهوري، ف/ ٩٤ - ١١٣).
