(۱) مثال ذلك أن القانون المدني الجديد لدينا في بحث ضمان العيب من عقد البيع يجيز للطرفين أن يتفقا على عدم ضمان البائع للعيوب الخفية إذا ظهر منها شيء في المبيع، فيكون المشتري حينئذ مشترياً على مسؤوليته فضمان العيب حكم مقرر قانونياً على كل بائع ما لم يتفق الطرفان على خلافه. لكن القانون ينص أيضاً على أن الاتفاق على خلافه إنما هو جائز إذا كان البائع حسن النية. أما إذا كان البائع سيىء النية بأن دلس العيب على المشتري تدليساً أي كتمه عنه وهو عالم به فإن اشتراطه عدم ضمان العيب يكون باطلاً، ويبقى البائع خاضعاً للضمان القانوني، كي لا يتخذ هذا الشرط ذريعة للاحتيال والغش ( ر ) ق م ٤١٣ و (٤٢١ وانظر أيضاً ما تقدم في بحث عيوب الرضا (ف ٥/٣٥).
Chapter (44): Autonomy of Will in Foreign Jurisprudence. Then the principle of autonomy of will soon regained its standing on new foundations. Thus the principle of autonomy of will fluctuated between ebb and flow, taking and giving, relapse and recovery, until it finally settled on that moderate theory we outlined earlier in the introduction.

3/44 - And Articles 1134 and 1135 of the French Civil Code were a legal hold for all:
- An extremist faction of the principle of autonomy of will adhered to the first of them, which states that the contract is law for the contracting parties.
- The other faction then adhered to the following article, which declares that the binding of the contracting parties to what their contract entails is restricted by what justice and custom require, and by the provisions established by law, according to the nature of the obligation.
There is thus no room to say that the will of the contracting parties alone creates obligations and determines their effects; rather, it must be subject to limits that the Lawgiver (the legislator) determines. 4/44 - These limits and restrictions with which legislation restricts the principle of autonomy of will vary according to the types of rights, contracts, and their subjects:

a) In public rights and obligations arising from them, there is no authority of the obligor's will at all; rather, it is solely for the law.
b) In the marriage contract and its consequences, the authority of the will of the two contracting parties is confined to the basic contract, i.e., accepting or rejecting the contract by their mere free will. As for the formal requirements in the marriage contract, as well as its effects and obligations between the spouses, they are arranged by the law according to what is required by the organization of family affairs and social interests, whose assessment is referred to the legislator.
c) In real property rights, the scope of the authority of the will of the contracting parties expands concerning the effects of the contract. As for its formation, it is subject to formal procedures in registration imposed by law, due to their connection with legislative policy in organizing real property ownership, government taxes on it, and other considerations.
d) As for personal rights and obligations, they are the broadest field for the principle of autonomy of contractual will within the limits of public morals, public order, and specific legal texts when they are mandatory by binding provision. For example, it is not permissible to hire someone to commit a crime or to do something contrary to social morals. In this field and within these limits, a person may make whatever contracts he wishes and determine the effects of his contract by his pure will.
- And the contracting parties may, by their agreement, deviate from the provisions arranged by law for named contracts, because in principle these provisions are non-mandatory and are only imposed by law in the absence of agreement to the contrary, except for mandatory texts that the law stipulates or for which there is evidence that agreement to the contrary is not permissible.
Within the scope of this set of legislative limits and restrictions, the general legal principle is applied that the contract is the law of the contracting parties; i.e., the contract binds its parties in everything they have agreed upon just as the law binds all people. (See: 'Theory of Contract' by Professor al-Sanhuri, pp. 94-113).

(1) An example of that is that our new civil code, in the discussion of warranty of defects in a sales contract, permits the two parties to agree that the seller is not liable for hidden defects if any appear in the sold item; then the buyer is a buyer at his own risk. Thus, warranty of a defect is a legally established rule upon every seller unless the two parties agree otherwise. But the law also states that agreement to the contrary is only permissible if the seller is in good faith. However, if the seller is in bad faith by fraudulently concealing the defect from the buyer, i.e., he knows about it and hides it, then his stipulation of no warranty for the defect is void, and the seller remains subject to legal warranty, so that this condition does not become a means for fraud and deception (see Civil Code articles 413 and 421; also see what preceded in the discussion of defects of consent, section 5/35).
Source pages p. 556

Commentary
This passage is a comparative legal discussion on the principle of autonomy of will (سلطان الإرادة). The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, a Hanafi jurist, explains how Western legal systems—specifically French law—have grappled with the tension between party autonomy and mandatory legal provisions. He notes that the French Civil Code Articles 1134 and 1135 were interpreted differently: one extremist view held that the contract is absolute law for the parties, while a more moderate view emphasized limits of justice, custom, and law. The author then classifies different types of rights and their respective degrees of party autonomy: public rights (no autonomy), marriage (autonomy only to enter), real property (autonomy in effects but not form), and personal obligations (broadest field within public order and morals). He also explains that in named contracts, the default rules are non-mandatory, allowing parties to agree otherwise, except for prohibitory texts. The passage culminates in the famous maxim 'the contract is the law of the contracting parties,' interpreted within Islamic legal limits. This comparative overview serves to contextualize the Islamic legal position on contractual freedom, which the author discusses elsewhere in the book.

Key Terms
سلطان الإرادة (sulṭān al-irādah): The principle that parties are free to create legal obligations by their own will in contracts, subject to legal limits.
العقد قانون المتعاقدين (al-ʿaqd qānūn al-mutaʿāqidayn): The contract is the law of the contracting parties, meaning it binds them as law binds others.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Party autonomy in contract is wide but always bounded by Shari'ah; the contract cannot override explicit prohibitions or mandatory obligations.

Autonomy of Will in Western Contract Law
Concluding summary of autonomy of will in Western law

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the principle of autonomy of will in Western contract law and identify the three categories of limits (public morals, public order, mandatory legal texts).

Core Idea
The contract is the law of the parties, but within the bounds of public order and mandatory rules.

This lesson examines the Western principle of autonomy of will as summarized by Mustafa al-Zarqa. You will learn how contractual freedom operates within defined legislative boundaries, including public morals, public order, and mandatory rules. The passage also illustrates with an example from civil code regarding warranty of defects.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main principle
Find the sentence that states the contract is the law of the contracting parties and note the scope of this freedom.
2
List the three categories of limits
From the text, extract the three types of restrictions: public morals (آداب عامة), public order (نظام عام), and mandatory legal texts (نصوص آمرة).
3
Analyze the example on warranty
Read the example from the civil code regarding hidden defects. Understand how the default rule (non‑mandatory) can be overridden by agreement, except when the seller is in bad faith (fraud).
This illustrates the boundary of mandatory rules.
4
Recognize the qawida
The passage implicitly relies on the legal maxim 'العقد شريعة المتعاقدين' (the contract is the law of the parties). Confirm this and note its scope.

الباب السادس : نظرية العقود وما إليها في الفقه
في آثار العقد. أما تكوينه فيخضع لمراسم شكلية في التسجيل يفرضها القانون، لارتباطها بسياسة التشريع في تنظيم الملكية العقارية، وفي الضرائب الحكومية عليها، وغير ذلك من الاعتبارات .
د - أما الحقوق الشخصية والالتزامات فهي الميدان الأوسع لمبدأ سلطان الإرادة العقدية ضمن حدود الآداب العامة، والنظام العام، والنصوص القانونية الخاصة عندما تكون آمرة بحكم إلزامي. فلا يصح الاستئجار على ارتكاب جريمة، أو على فعل ما ينافي الآداب الاجتماعية .
مثلاً عقد
ففي هذا الميدان وهذه الحدود يعقد الإنسان ما يشاء من العقود، ويحدد آثار عقده بإرادته المحضة.
- وللعاقدين أن يخالفا باتفاقهما ما رتبه القانون من أحكام العقود المسماة، لأن الأصل فيها أنها غير إلزامية وإنما يفرضها القانون عند عدم الاتفاق على خلافها إلا ما كان من النصوص الأمرة التي ينص القانون أو يقوم فيه دليل على عدم جواز اتفاق المتعاقدين على خلافها (۱).
وفي نطاق هذه المجموعة من القيود والحدود التشريعية يطبق المبدأ القانوني العام القائل : إنَّ العقد قانون المتعاقدين؛ أي إنّ العقد يلزم عاقديه في كل ما اتفقا عليه كما يلزم القانون سائر الناس. (ر: «نظرية العقد» للأستاذ العلامة السنهوري، ف/ ٩٤ - ١١٣).
.
(۱) مثال ذلك أن القانون المدني الجديد لدينا في بحث ضمان العيب من عقد البيع يجيز للطرفين أن يتفقا على عدم ضمان البائع للعيوب الخفية إذا ظهر منها شيء في المبيع، فيكون المشتري حينئذ مشترياً على مسؤوليته فضمان العيب حكم مقرر قانونياً على كل بائع ما لم يتفق الطرفان على خلافه. لكن القانون ينص أيضاً على أن الاتفاق على خلافه إنما هو جائز إذا كان البائع حسن النية. أما إذا كان البائع سيىء النية بأن دلس العيب على المشتري تدليساً أي كتمه عنه وهو عالم به فإن اشتراطه عدم ضمان العيب يكون باطلاً، ويبقى البائع خاضعاً للضمان القانوني، كي لا يتخذ هذا الشرط ذريعة للاحتيال والغش ( ر ) ق م ٤١٣ و (٤٢١ وانظر أيضاً ما تقدم في بحث عيوب الرضا (ف ٥/٣٥).
۵۸۸

الفصل (٤٤) سلطان الإرادة في الفقه الأجنبي
هذه خلاصة موجزة جداً عن مبدأ سلطان الإرادة في التشريع الأوروبي في مراحله التاريخية، وما استقر عليه المبدأ أخيراً في الفقه القانوني والتشريع الوضعي الحديث.
ومما تقدم بيانه يرى أن ما استقر عليه مبدأ سلطان الإرادة في نهاية تطوافه في الحقوق الحديثة هو الأسس التي قام عليها اجتهاد عمر بن الخطاب رضي الله عنه، والقاضي شريح، وابن شبرمة، والاجتهاد الحنبلي، وما جرى هذا المجرى من الاجتهادات في الفقه الإسلامي، مما سلف بيانه في البحث السابق.
Chapter Six: Theory of Contracts and Related Matters in Jurisprudence. On the effects of the contract. As for its formation, it is subject to formal registration procedures imposed by law, due to its connection with legislative policy in organizing real estate ownership, governmental taxes on it, and other considerations. D. Personal rights and obligations are the widest field for the principle of the supremacy of contractual will within the limits of public morals, public order, and specific legal texts when they are mandatory with binding force. It is not permissible to hire for committing a crime or for doing something contrary to social morals. For example, a contract. In this field and within these limits, a person may make whatever contracts he wishes and determine the effects of his contract by his own will. The contracting parties may, by their agreement, deviate from the legal provisions governing named contracts, because the default is that these provisions are non-mandatory and are only imposed by law in the absence of agreement to the contrary, except for mandatory texts that explicitly state or demonstrate that the parties cannot agree to the contrary (1). Within the scope of this set of legislative restrictions and limits, the general legal principle applies: The contract is the law of the contracting parties; i.e., the contract binds the parties in everything they have agreed upon just as the law binds all people. (See: 'Theory of Contract' by Professor al-Sanhuri, vol. 94-113). (1) An example of this is that the new civil code in our country, in the discussion of warranty of defects in a sales contract, allows the parties to agree that the seller is not liable for hidden defects that may appear in the sold item, in which case the buyer purchases at his own risk. Thus, warranty of defect is a legally established rule upon every seller unless the parties agree otherwise. However, the law also states that such an agreement is only permissible if the seller is in good faith. But if the seller is in bad faith, i.e., he fraudulently conceals the defect from the buyer while knowing about it, then his stipulation of non-liability is void, and the seller remains subject to legal warranty, so that this condition is not used as a means for fraud and deception. (See Civil Code articles 413 and 421, and also refer to the earlier discussion on defects of consent (vol. 5/35). 588 Chapter (44): Supremacy of Will in Foreign Jurisprudence. This is a very brief summary of the principle of supremacy of will in European legislation in its historical stages, and what the principle has finally settled upon in legal jurisprudence and modern positive legislation. From the foregoing explanation, it can be seen that what the principle of supremacy of will has settled upon at the end of its journey in modern legal systems is the foundations upon which the ijtihad of `Umar ibn al-Khattab (may Allah be pleased with him), Judge Shurayh, Ibn Shubruma, the Hanbali ijtihad, and similar ijtihads in Islamic jurisprudence were based, as explained in the previous discussion.
Source pages p. 557

Commentary
This passage concludes the discussion of the principle of autonomy of will in Western law and compares it with Islamic jurisprudence. The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, first reiterates the general rule that contracts are binding according to the parties' agreement within limits of public order and mandatory laws. He provides an example from civil code regarding warranty of defects, showing that parties can agree to exclude liability unless the seller acted fraudulently. Then in a new chapter, he summarizes that the Western principle of autonomy of will, after historical development, has converged with the ijtihad of early Islamic scholars like `Umar ibn al-Khattab, Shurayh, Ibn Shubruma, and the Hanbali school. This highlights that Islamic jurisprudence had already articulated balanced principles of contractual freedom with necessary restrictions.

Key Terms
سلطان الإرادة (sultan al-irada): The principle that contractual parties have the power to determine the effects of their contract by their own will, within legal limits.
النظام العام (al-nizam al-'amm): Public order; the set of fundamental principles and rules that cannot be contravened by private agreements.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the default nature of legal provisions governing named contracts?
A) They are always mandatory and cannot be changed.
B) They are non-mandatory and apply only if parties do not agree otherwise.
C) They are only advisory recommendations.
D) They apply only to commercial contracts.

Key Takeaway
The Western principle of autonomy of will is not absolute; it operates within the boundaries of public morals, public order, and mandatory legal texts.

Legal Philosophy and Theory
intermediate

Islamic Parallels to Autonomy of Will
Concluding summary of autonomy of will in Western law and its Islamic counterpart

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the Western principle of autonomy of will aligns with the ijtihad of early Islamic jurists such as Umar ibn al-Khattab, Shurayh, and Ibn Shubruma, and the Hanbali school.

Core Idea
Islamic jurisprudence anticipated balanced contractual freedom long before modern Western law.

In this lesson, we explore the concluding insight of Mustafa al-Zarqa: the Western principle of autonomy of will, after centuries of evolution, has arrived at a position remarkably similar to the ijtihad of early Islamic scholars. This comparison highlights the sophistication of Islamic legal thought and its relevance to modern contract theory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Islamic scholars mentioned
List the names: Umar ibn al-Khattab, Shurayh, Ibn Shubruma, and the Hanbali school. Note that these are early authorities.
2
Recall the substance of their ijtihad
The passage refers to 'الأسس التي قام عليها اجتهاد عمر...' (the foundations upon which their ijtihad was based). From earlier study, recall that they upheld contractual freedom unless it violated Sharia principles.
3
Compare with Western development
The Western principle started with near-absolute freedom, then became restricted by public order and mandatory rules. The Islamic approach always had these restrictions built-in from revealed sources.
4
Derive the fatwa implication
For a mufti, this comparison shows that Hanafi fiqh can engage with modern contractual issues without borrowing from Western law, because the principles are already present.

Definition of Condition (Shart) in Western Law – A Comparative Introduction
Introduction to Western legal definition of condition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Western legal definition of condition and identify its correspondence to the Hanafi concept of al-shart al-ta'liqi.

Core Idea
Western and Islamic law both define a condition as linking an obligation to a future uncertain event.

This lesson introduces the Western legal definition of a condition (shart) as understood in foreign jurisprudence. The passage highlights that although terminology may differ, the core concept of a suspensive condition (ta'liq) is shared between Western law and Islamic fiqh. Understanding this parallel helps a mufti analyse contracts from different legal traditions.

Guided Path
1
Locate the Western definition
Find the exact sentence where Western jurists define condition. Note the key elements: 'creation or extinction of an obligation' and 'future contingent event'.
The definition is given in the passage: tying the birth or disappearance of an obligation to a future possible event.
2
Deconstruct the definition components
Break down the definition into its essential parts: (a) what is suspended (the obligation), (b) how it is suspended (creation or extinction), (c) the trigger (a future event), and (d) the nature of the event (possible, not certain or impossible).
Contingent means neither certain nor impossible.
3
Compare with Islamic jurisprudence
The author states this corresponds to the 'suspensive condition' (al-shart al-ta'liqi) in Islamic fiqh. Identify what the Islamic equivalent would be and note any slight differences in classification or terminology.
In Hanafi fiqh, ta'liq is used in contracts and divorces, but invalid conditions can nullify the contract.
4
Recognise the ambiguity in Western division
The passage mentions that Western law divides conditions into two main types (suspensive and resolutive), which will be explained later. Recognise that the current excerpt only gives the definition, not the full taxonomy.
The text ends abruptly at 'and they are:' indicating the division is on the next page.

Second Demand: Contractual Conditions in Foreign Jurisprudence. Definition of condition according to law, and its division - suspensive condition and resolutive condition - critique of the foreign theory. 5/44 - Definition of condition legally: On the division of conditions. The legal scholars in foreign jurisprudence have a different division of contractual conditions from what we have explained in Islamic jurisprudence. They define condition as: linking the creation or extinction of an obligation to a future contingent event. The meaning of it being contingent is that it is neither certain to occur in the future nor impossible. And this - as you see - is the suspensive condition (al-shart al-ta'liqi) in the terminology of Islamic jurisprudence (see vol. 3/43 - 5/43). 6/44 - Division of condition legally: Then they divide this condition into two basic types from which subsidiary forms branch out, and they are:

Commentary
This passage introduces the Western legal concept of condition (shart). The author notes that Western jurists have a different division than Islamic fiqh. They define a condition as linking the birth or extinction of an obligation to a future uncertain event. This corresponds to what Islamic jurisprudence calls the 'suspensive condition' (shart ta'liqi). The passage then indicates that Western law divides conditions into two main categories, which will be explained subsequently. The text ends before the enumeration of the two types.

Key Terms
الشرط التعليقي (al-shart al-ta'liqi): A condition that suspends the existence or extinction of an obligation upon a future uncertain event.
حادث مستقبل محتمل (hadith mustaqbal muhtamal): A future contingent event that may or may not occur.

Key Takeaway
The Western legal definition of condition (linking obligation to a future uncertain event) directly parallels the Hanafi concept of shart ta'liqi, underscoring a shared legal logic across traditions.

The Western Classification of Contractual Conditions and the Hanafi Critique
Comparative critique of Western condition theory

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between suspensive and resolutive conditions in Western law and critique them using the Hanafi distinction between ta'liq (suspension) and taqyid (restriction).

Core Idea
Western law conflates suspending an obligation with restricting it; Hanafi usul separates them precisely.

This passage examines how Western legal theory classifies conditions in contracts into suspensive and resolutive types. While this resembles the Islamic concept of ta'liq, the author argues that Western jurists overlooked a critical distinction that Hanafi jurists made: the difference between suspending the existence of an obligation (ta'liq) and restricting its description (taqyid). Understanding this distinction is essential for a Hanafi mufti analyzing modern contracts with conditional clauses.

Guided Path
1
Define the Western condition
Understand the Western definition: tying the creation or extinction of an obligation to a future uncertain event. Note that this corresponds to the Islamic suspensive condition (al-shart al-ta'liqi).
The term 'possible' means uncertain but not impossible.
2
Identify the two types
Distinguish suspensive condition (obligation does not exist until condition) from resolutive condition (obligation exists but may dissolve upon condition).
Examples from lease contracts are given in the text.
3
Map to Islamic terminology
Recognize that Western suspensive condition matches al-shart al-ta'liqi in Islamic fiqh, but the author warns against confusing terms like 'al-shart al-waqif' with waqf terminology.
The author prefers 'shart muwaqqaf' for clarity.
4
Locate the critique
The author criticizes Western theory for failing to distinguish between suspension (ta'liq) and restriction (taqyid) by a condition.
This distinction is foundational in Hanafi contract theory.
5
Apply the Hanafi lens
Analyze a conditional clause as either ta'liq (suspending the contract itself) or taqyid (restricting its effect). The ruling changes accordingly.
For example, a condition that 'the lease continues if the tenant stays' may be taqyid, not ta'liq.

الفصل (٤٤) سلطان الإرادة في الفقه الأجنبي
هذه خلاصة موجزة جداً عن مبدأ سلطان الإرادة في التشريع الأوروبي في مراحله التاريخية، وما استقر عليه المبدأ أخيراً في الفقه القانوني والتشريع الوضعي الحديث.
ومما تقدم بيانه يرى أن ما استقر عليه مبدأ سلطان الإرادة في نهاية تطوافه في الحقوق الحديثة هو الأسس التي قام عليها اجتهاد عمر بن الخطاب رضي الله عنه، والقاضي شريح، وابن شبرمة، والاجتهاد الحنبلي، وما جرى هذا المجرى من الاجتهادات في الفقه الإسلامي، مما سلف بيانه في البحث السابق.
المطلب الثاني :
الشروط العقدية في الفقه الأجنبي
تعريف الشرط قانوناً، وتقسيمه - الشرط الموقف والشرط الفاسخ - نقد النظرية الأجنبية
٥/٤٤ - تعريف الشرط قانوناً :
في تقسيم الشروط
إن علماء القانون في الفقه الأجنبي لهم في الشروط العقدية تقسيم آخر غير ما أوضحناه في الفقه الإسلامي.
فهم يعرفون الشرط بأنه : ربط نشوء الالتزام أو زواله بحادث مستقبل محتمل .
ومعنى كونه محتملاً أنه غير مؤكد الحصول في المستقبل ولا ممتنع. وهذا - كما ترى - هو الشرط التعليقي في اصطلاح الفقه الإسلامي ر ف ٣/٤٣ - ٥/٤٣) .
٦/٤٤ - تقسيم الشرط قانوناً :
ثم هم يقسمون هذا الشرط إلى نوعين أساسيين تتفرع منهما صور فرعية، وهما:
١- الشرط الموقف : condition suspensive وهو الذي يعلق نشوء الالتزام ويجعله متوقفاً على أمر مستقبل محتمل ؛ مثل قول الموظف : آجرتك داري هذه سنة بكذا إن نقلت وظيفتي إلى بلد آخر. فتكون الإجارة بين الطرفين معدومة قبل وقوع شرط النقل.
۲ - والشرط الفاسخ : condition résolutoire وهو الذي يرتب على وقوعه زوال الالتزام القائم؛ مثل : استأجرت دارك على شرط أنني إذا نقلت وظيفتي إلى بلد آخر انفسخت الإجارة. فتكون الإجارة قائمة بين الطرفين ولكنها تفسخ بوقوع النقل.
والفارقُ الأساسي عندهم بين النوعين في الحكم أنَّ هو الالتزام في الشرط الموقف معدوم محتمل الوجود ؛ وفي الشرط الفاسخ موجود محتمل الزوال.
ويتفرع عن ذلك تفاصيل مبسوطة في مظانها من الكتب القانونية الفرنسية وما نقل عنها علماء القانون المصريون (۱) .
ر دالوز [؟] precis de droit civil) ج ف / ٤١٥ - ٤٢٦ ، و «الموجز في نظرية الالتزامات للأستاذ العلامة السنهوري ف / ٣٦٦ - ٤٨٠) .
٧/٤٤ - نقد النظرية الأجنبية في الشروط :
إن شرّاح نظرية الالتزامات في الفقه الأجنبي لم يميزوا بين التعليق على الشرط والتقييد بالشرط كما ميّز فقهاؤنا ذلك التمييز الدقيق المستند إلى
(۱) ترجم علماء القانون المصريون كلمة : La condition suspensive في القانون الفرنسي بالشرط الواقف. وعلى ذلك درج الاصطلاح القضائي في مصر غير أني أرجح هنا أن يقال : شرط موقف كما يسميه بعضهم اشتقاقاً من (أوقف) بزيادة الهمزة ولو أنه خلاف الفصيح لغة في هذه المادة، إذ الفصيح فيها الثلاثي المجرد (ر: المصباح والقاموس)؛ وذلك كيلاً يشتبه على الطالب اسم الشرط الواقف باسم «شرط الواقف» المستعمل لمعنى آخر بعيد عن هذا الموضوع في أحكام الأوقاف، وإن كان التركيب الأول وصفياً والثاني إضافياً.
Chapter (44) Autonomy of Will in Western Jurisprudence. This is a very brief summary of the principle of autonomy of will in European legislation in its historical stages, and what the principle has finally settled upon in modern legal jurisprudence and positive legislation. From what has been explained above, it is seen that what the principle of autonomy of will settled upon at the end of its tour in modern rights are the foundations upon which the ijtihad of Umar ibn al-Khattab (may Allah be pleased with him), Judge Shurayh, Ibn Shubruma, Hanbali ijtihad, and similar ijtihads in Islamic jurisprudence were based, as previously explained in the earlier discussion. Second Subsection: Contractual Conditions in Western Jurisprudence. Definition of condition legally, and its division - Suspensive condition and Resolutory condition - Critique of the Western theory. 5/44 - Definition of condition legally: On the division of conditions. Indeed, legal scholars in Western jurisprudence have another division of contractual conditions different from what we have explained in Islamic jurisprudence. They define a condition as: tying the creation or extinction of an obligation to a future possible event. The meaning of 'possible' is that it is neither certain to occur in the future nor impossible. And this - as you see - is the suspensive condition (al-shart al-ta'liqi) in Islamic jurisprudence terminology (see 3/43 - 5/43). 6/44 - Division of condition legally: Then they divide this condition into two basic types from which subsidiary forms branch, and they are: 1- Suspensive condition: which suspends the creation of the obligation and makes it dependent on a future possible matter; such as an employee saying: I lease you my house for one year for such amount if I transfer my job to another country. So the lease between the two parties is nonexistent before the occurrence of the condition of transfer. 2- Resolutory condition: which entails upon its occurrence the extinction of an existing obligation; such as: I lease your house on condition that if I transfer my job to another country, the lease is dissolved. So the lease is existing between the two parties but it dissolves upon the occurrence of transfer. The basic difference between the two types in ruling according to them is that the obligation in the suspensive condition is non-existent, possible to exist; and in the resolutory condition, it is existing, possible to cease. Derived from that are detailed explanations found in their places in French legal books and what Egyptian legal scholars have transmitted from them (1). (Reference: Dalloz précis de droit civil, vol. ?, pp. 415-426; and al-Mujaz fi Nazariyyat al-Iltizamat by Professor al-Sanhuri, vol. ?, pp. 366-480). 7/44 - Critique of the Western theory on conditions: Indeed, the commentators on the theory of obligations in Western jurisprudence did not distinguish between suspending upon a condition and restricting by a condition as our jurists made that precise distinction based upon (1) Egyptian legal scholars translated the word: La condition suspensive in French law as al-shart al-waqif. And the judicial terminology in Egypt followed that. However, I prefer here to say: shart muwaqqaf as some call it, derived from 'awqafa' with the addition of the hamza, even if it is linguistically less eloquent in this root, since the eloquent form is the simple triliteral (see al-Misbah and al-Qamus); so that the student does not confuse the term 'al-shart al-waqif' with the term 'shart al-waqif' used for another meaning far from this subject in the rulings of endowments, although the first construction is attributive and the second is genitive.
Source pages p. 558

Commentary
This passage is a comparative legal study. The author begins by summarizing the Western principle of 'autonomy of will' and notes that its final form in modern law aligns with certain ijtihadat from early Islamic jurisprudence, specifically those of Umar ibn al-Khattab, Shurayh, Ibn Shubruma, and the Hanbali school. This sets the stage for the second subsection on contractual conditions. In Western law, a condition is defined as linking the creation or extinction of an obligation to a future uncertain event. This corresponds exactly to the 'suspensive condition' (al-shart al-ta'liqi) in Islamic fiqh. Western law divides such conditions into two types: (1) suspensive condition (condition suspensive), where the obligation does not exist until the condition occurs; and (2) resolutory condition (condition résolutoire), where the obligation exists but is liable to be dissolved upon the occurrence of the condition. The author provides examples from lease contracts to illustrate. The key difference is that under a suspensive condition, the obligation is currently non-existent but may come into existence; under a resolutory condition, the obligation currently exists but may cease. The author then critiques Western legal theory for failing to distinguish between 'suspension upon a condition' (ta'liq 'ala al-shart) and 'restriction by a condition' (taqyid bi al-shart), a distinction that Islamic jurists made with precision. This distinction is fundamental in Hanafi fiqh, where suspending a contract on a condition (ta'liq) affects the existence of the obligation, while restricting a contract with a condition (taqyid) affects the description of the obligation. The author also notes a terminological issue: Egyptian legal scholars translate 'condition suspensive' as 'al-shart al-waqif', but he prefers 'shart muwaqqaf' to avoid confusion with the term 'shart al-waqif' used in waqf (endowment) law.

Key Terms
الشرط الموقف (al-shart al-muwaqqaf): A suspensive condition that suspends the creation of an obligation upon a future uncertain event; the obligation does not exist until the condition occurs.
الشرط الفاسخ (al-shart al-fasikh): A resolutory condition that causes the dissolution of an existing obligation upon the occurrence of a future uncertain event.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basic difference between a suspensive condition and a resolutory condition in Western law?
A) The suspensive condition extinguishes an existing obligation, while the resolutory condition creates a new one.
B) Under a suspensive condition, the obligation does not exist until the condition occurs; under a resolutory condition, the obligation exists but may be dissolved.
C) Both conditions are identical; the difference is only in terminology.
D) The suspensive condition applies only to sales, while the resolutory condition applies only to leases.

Key Takeaway
Western law's suspensive/resolutive binary overlooks the Hanafi distinction between suspending existence (ta'liq) and restricting description (taqyid), which is essential for correct contract analysis in fatwa.

Legal Philosophy and Theory

Critique of the Western Classification of Conditions: A Hanafi Perspective
Critiquing the foreign dichotomy of suspensive and resolutory conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify why the Hanafi school rejects the dichotomy of suspensive and resolutory conditions and explain the correct classification based on the object of suspension.

Core Idea
The condition is one type; the difference lies in what is conditioned: creation or extinction of the obligation.

Western legal systems often divide conditions into suspensive and resolutory categories. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi critique of that division, as articulated by Mustafa al-Zarqa. You will learn that the condition itself is always a suspension—the real distinction is whether the condition triggers the birth or the death of an obligation. This insight affects where conditions are discussed in legal theory: within the theory of contract, not as a separate topic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Western dichotomy
Recall the two types: suspensive (condition suspensive) delays the existence of the obligation until the condition occurs; resolutory (condition résolutoire) extinguishes an already existing obligation upon the condition's fulfilment.
The condition is always a future uncertain event.
2
Recognize the Hanafi objection
The Hanafi position is that the condition itself is the same—a suspension (ta'liq). The difference is not in the condition but in the 'conditioned matter' (al-amr al-mashrūṭ): either the creation of the obligation (nash'u) or its extinction (zawāl).
This distinction is based on the precise Hanafi separation between ta'liq and taqyīd.
3
Analyze the source of confusion
Western jurists were misled by the different results (creation vs. extinction) into thinking the condition itself changes. In reality, the condition remains a pure suspension; the effect differs because what is suspended differs.
The same condition can be used for either purpose.
4
Evaluate the proper placement
The author criticizes the Western practice of discussing conditions in a separate chapter, as if they were an independent feature of obligations. Hanafi theory insists that conditions are contractual elements (unṣur ʿaqdī) and must be studied within the theory of contract.
The German Civil Code aligns with this view by treating conditions under 'juridical acts'.
5
Apply the critique
When analyzing a contract with a condition, determine whether the condition suspends the creation or the extinction of the obligation. Then apply the same ta'liq framework regardless of the outcome.
The ruling on the condition (e.g., validity, effect) does not change; only the timing of the obligation changes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نقد النظرية الأجنبية في الشروط :

إن شرّاح نظرية الالتزامات في الفقه الأجنبي لم يميزوا بين التعليق على الشرط والتقييد بالشرط كما ميّز فقهاؤنا ذلك التمييز الدقيق المستند إلى اختلاف ماهية هذين النوعين من الشروط على ما سبق بيانه بل خلط علماء القانون بينهما .
وأما تقسيمهم الشرط إلى نوعين : موقف، وفاسخ، فليس في الحقيقة تقسيماً للشرط نفسه إلى نوعين، بل هو تقسيم للأمر المشروط : فإن كان المربوط بالشرط هو أصل الالتزام سمي الشرط عندهم موقفاً؛ وإن كان المربوط به زوال الالتزام سمي الشرط فاسخاً. فالشرط في كلا الحالين نوع واحد من قبيل التعليق؛ ولكن تعليق نشوء الالتزام يقتضي عدم وجود الالتزام بين الطرفين قبل تحقق شرطه المعلق عليه ؛ وتعليق زوال الالتزام يقتضي قيام الالتزام واستمراره بينهما إلى حين وجود ما يفسخه.
وهذا ما أوهمهم انقسام الشرط نفسه إلى نوعين مختلفي النتائج، بينما اختلاف النتائج ناشيء من اختلاف نوع الأمر المشروط: هل هو نشوء الالتزام أو هو زواله لا من اختلاف في طبيعة الشرط ونوعيته.
ومما يلحظ عليهم أيضاً من حيث الترتيب أنهم لا يبحثون عن الشرط في ضمن نظرية العقد من مصادر الالتزام بل يبحثون عنه في فصل على حدة، كما لو كان الشرط حالة مستقلة من الأحوال العامة للالتزام نفسه، بينما أنَّ الشرط عنصر عقدي ولا يتصور له وجود إلا تبعاً لعقد. فالموضع الخاص المناسب إنما هو في نظرية العقد. وعلى هذا جرى القانون المدني الألماني حيث تكلّم عن أحكام الشرط في بحث التصرف القانوني (أي العقد وتصرف الإرادة المنفردة) خلافاً للترتيب المألوف في نظرية الالتزام العامة في القانون.
Critique of the foreign theory of conditions:
Indeed, the commentators on the theory of obligations in foreign fiqh did not distinguish between suspension by a condition and restriction by a condition as our jurists made that precise distinction based on the difference in the essence of these two types of conditions as previously explained; rather, the legal scholars confused them.
As for their division of the condition into two types: suspensive and resolutory, it is not in reality a division of the condition itself into two types, but rather a division of the conditioned matter: for if what is linked by the condition is the origin of the obligation, they call the condition suspensive; and if what is linked by it is the extinction of the obligation, they call the condition resolutory. Thus the condition in both cases is one type, of the category of suspension; but suspending the creation of the obligation necessitates the non-existence of the obligation between the parties before the occurrence of the condition on which it is suspended; and suspending the extinction of the obligation necessitates the existence and continuance of the obligation between them until the time of the occurrence of what rescinds it.
This is what deluded them into thinking the condition itself is divided into two types with different results, while the difference in results arises from the difference in the type of the conditioned matter: whether it is the creation of the obligation or its extinction, not from a difference in the nature and type of the condition.
Also observed of them in terms of arrangement is that they do not discuss the condition within the theory of contract from the sources of obligation, but rather they discuss it in a separate chapter, as if the condition were an independent state among the general states of the obligation itself, while the condition is a contractual element and its existence cannot be conceived except as subsidiary to a contract. The proper specific place is within the theory of contract. The German Civil Code proceeded on this, speaking about the rulings of the condition in the discussion of juridical act (i.e., contract and unilateral act of will) contrary to the familiar arrangement in the general theory of obligation in law.
Source pages p. 559

Commentary
This passage is a critical analysis of the Western (primarily French) classification of conditions in contract law, as presented by the author. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, a prominent contemporary Hanafi jurist, begins by asserting that foreign commentators on the theory of obligations failed to make the precise distinction that Muslim jurists (fuqaha) made between suspension by a condition (ta'liq) and restriction by a condition (taqyeed). They confused the two concepts.

He then explains that the foreign division of conditions into suspensive (condition suspensive) and resolutory (condition résolutoire) is not actually a division of the condition itself, but a division of the thing that is conditioned (al-amr al-mashroot). If what is linked to the condition is the creation (nash'u) of the obligation, they call it suspensive; if what is linked is the extinction (zawal) of the obligation, they call it resolutory. In both cases, the condition is one type: a suspension. The only difference is what is suspended: the coming into existence or the termination of the obligation.

This misunderstanding led them to think that the condition itself has two types with different results, whereas the difference in results stems from the different nature of the conditioned matter—whether it is the creation or extinction of the obligation—not from any inherent difference in the condition.

Furthermore, the author criticizes the arrangement of legal discussions in foreign systems: they discuss conditions in a separate chapter apart from the theory of contract, treating conditions as an independent state of obligations. The author insists that conditions are inherently contractual elements (unṣur 'aqdi) and cannot exist except as part of a contract. Thus, the proper place for conditions is within the theory of contract. He notes that German civil law correctly includes conditions in the discussion of juridical acts (contracts and unilateral acts), unlike the French approach.

This analysis aligns with the Hanafi fiqh perspective, where conditions are always considered within the context of contracts and are not abstracted into a separate general theory.

Key Terms
التعليق على الشرط (al-ta'liq 'ala al-shart): ربط وجود الالتزام أو زواله بشرط مستقبل غير محقق
التقييد بالشرط (al-taqyeed bi al-shart): ربط التزام بوصف أو شرط في نفس العقد، لا تعليق وجوده
الشرط الموقوف (al-shart al-mawqūf): الشرط المعلق عليه نشوء الالتزام (suspensive condition في المصطلح الأجنبي)
الشرط الفاسخ (al-shart al-fāsikh): الشرط المعلق عليه زوال الالتزام (resolutory condition في المصطلح الأجنبي)
الأمر المشروط (al-amr al-mashrūṭ): ما يربط بالشرط: إما نشوء الالتزام أو زواله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Zarqa's critique, what is the true basis for the difference between a suspensive condition and a resolutory condition?
A) The nature of the condition itself (suspending vs. rescinding).
B) The type of obligation being created or extinguished.
C) The presence of a time clause in the contract.
D) The intention of the parties.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi critique reveals that conditions are a single legal category (ta'liq); the distinction between suspensive and resolutory is only about what is conditioned, not the condition itself.

Critique of the Suspensive/Resolutive Dichotomy: The Unity of Shart in Hanafi Usul
Critique of dividing conditions into suspensive and resolutive

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the fallacy in dividing conditions into suspensive and resolutive and apply the Hanafi principle that shart is a single type of ta'liq.

Core Idea
The condition is one; only the object conditioned differs—creation or termination of obligation.

In this passage, the author critiques the Western legal classification of conditions (shart) into suspensive (muwaqqif) and resolutive (fashiq). He argues that this is not a division of the condition itself but of the thing conditioned. The condition remains a single type—a suspension (ta'liq)—whether it conditions the emergence or the termination of an obligation. He also criticizes the systematic placement of conditions outside the theory of contract. This lesson will help you parse this critique and understand the Hanafi view on the nature of conditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Claim
The author begins by noting that foreign legal scholars divide conditions into suspensive and resolutive. Identify this claim and note that it is the target of critique.
The claim appears in lines 2-3 of the passage.
2
Understand the Refutation
The author refutes that this is a true division of the condition itself. Instead, it is a division of the thing conditioned (al-amr al-mashrut). Understand the reasoning: the condition in both cases is a single type of suspension.
Key phrase: 'فالشرط في كلا الحالين نوع واحد من قبيل التعليق'.
3
Recognize the Terminological Confusion
The author points out that the difference in results (effects) led to the mistaken belief that conditions are different. In reality, the difference arises from whether the obligation is being created or terminated.
The confusion is traced to the object conditioned, not the condition's nature.
4
Note the Structural Criticism
The author criticizes foreign legal systems for discussing conditions outside the theory of contract, as if they are independent states of obligation. Contrast this with the Hanafi approach where conditions are integral to the contract.
German Civil Code is mentioned as an exception that places conditions within legal dispositions.

الفصل (٤٤) سلطان الإرادة في الفقه الأجنبي
اختلاف ماهية هذين النوعين من الشروط على ما سبق بيانه بل خلط علماء القانون بينهما .

وهذا ما أوهمهم انقسام الشرط نفسه إلى نوعين مختلفي النتائج، بينما اختلاف النتائج ناشيء من اختلاف نوع الأمر المشروط: هل هو نشوء الالتزام أو هو زواله لا من اختلاف في طبيعة الشرط ونوعيته.
فمحله
ومما يلحظ عليهم أيضاً من حيث الترتيب أنهم لا يبحثون عن الشرط في ضمن نظرية العقد من مصادر الالتزام بل يبحثون عنه في فصل على حدة، كما لو كان الشرط حالة مستقلة من الأحوال العامة للالتزام نفسه، بينما أنَّ الشرط عنصر عقدي ولا يتصور له وجود إلا تبعاً لعقد. الخاص المناسب إنما هو في نظرية العقد. في نظرية العقد. وعلى هذا جرى القانون المدني الألماني حيث تكلّم عن أحكام الشرط في بحث التصرف القانوني (أي العقد وتصرف الإرادة المنفردة خلافاً للترتيب المألوف في نظرية الالتزام
العامة في القانون.
Chapter (44): The Authority of Will in Foreign Jurisprudence. The difference in the nature of these two types of conditions as previously explained, rather legal scholars confused them. As for their division of the condition into two types: suspensive and resolutive, it is not in reality a division of the condition itself into two types, but rather a division of the thing conditioned: if what is connected to the condition is the origin of the obligation, they call the condition suspensive; and if what is connected to it is the termination of the obligation, they call the condition resolutive. So the condition in both cases is one type, of the category of suspension; but suspension of the emergence of the obligation necessitates the non-existence of the obligation between the parties before the fulfillment of its contingent condition; and suspension of the termination of the obligation necessitates the existence and continuity of the obligation between them until the occurrence of what rescinds it. This is what led them to imagine the division of the condition itself into two types with different results, while the difference in results arises from the difference in the type of the thing conditioned: whether it is the emergence of the obligation or its termination, not from a difference in the nature and quality of the condition. So its place. Also observed from them in terms of arrangement is that they do not research the condition within the theory of contract from the sources of obligation, but rather they research it in a separate chapter, as if the condition were an independent state from the general states of the obligation itself, while the condition is a contractual element and cannot be conceived except following a contract. The appropriate special [place] is in the theory of contract. In the theory of contract. And the German Civil Code followed this, speaking about the rulings of the condition in the discussion of legal disposition (i.e., contract and unilateral declaration of will) contrary to the usual arrangement in the general theory of obligation in law.
Source pages p. 560

Commentary
This passage is a legal philosophy analysis by a Hanafi jurist, criticizing the Western classification of conditions (shart) into suspensive (muwaqqif) and resolutive (fashiq). The author argues that this is not a true division of the condition itself but of the object conditioned (al-amr al-mashrut). The condition remains a single type—a form of suspension (ta'liq). The difference in legal effect stems from whether it conditions the creation or the termination of an obligation. He also criticizes the placement of conditions outside the theory of contract, noting that in Islamic jurisprudence, conditions are integral to the contract and should be discussed within the theory of 'aqd, as exemplified by the German Civil Code which discusses conditions under legal dispositions.

Key Terms
شرط (shart): A condition, here defined as a single type of suspension (ta'liq) that attaches to an obligation.
موقف (muwaqqif): Suspensive condition: a condition that suspends the origin of an obligation.
فاسخ (fashiq): Resolutive condition: a condition that terminates an existing obligation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the Western division of conditions into suspensive and resolutive not a true division of the condition itself?
A) Because both types are actually the same in nature, merely differing in what they condition.
B) Because the condition is always resolutive.
C) Because Western scholars do not recognize conditions.
D) Because conditions are always suspensive in Islamic law.

Key Takeaway
The condition (shart) is a single type of suspension; any apparent division is due to the object conditioned, not the condition itself.

Understanding Contract Dissolution: Inhilal vs. Butlan
Defines contract dissolution and its types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between contract dissolution (inhilal) and nullity (butlan), and identify the two types of dissolution: rescission (faskh) and automatic dissolution (infisakh), with reference to Hanafi legal philosophy.

Core Idea
Dissolution ends a valid contract; nullity means it never legally existed.

In Hanafi fiqh, the distinction between a contract that never took legal effect and one that ends after being valid is fundamental for issuing accurate fatwas. This lesson provides the conceptual framework to distinguish nullity from dissolution, and voluntary rescission from automatic termination.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core definition
Read the first sentence: 'يراد من انحلال العقد زوال الرابطة الحقوقية...' What is being removed? The legal bond. Note that dissolution presupposes a valid prior formation.
The word 'زوال' indicates removal.
2
Contrast with nullity using the analogy
The author compares nullity to a stillborn child and dissolution to the death of a living person. This illustrates that nullity means the contract never had legal existence, while dissolution ends an existing one.
Stillborn = batil (void from start).
3
Identify the condition for dissolution
Dissolution only occurs after valid formation (in'aqd). This is a key distinguishing condition.
Shart: prior valid formation.
4
Differentiate between voluntary and involuntary causes
Voluntary removal is called faskh (rescission); involuntary removal is infisakh (automatic dissolution). This taxonomy is used in Hanafi fatwa.
Faskh can be by mutual consent or court order.
5
Understand iqala as a specific type of faskh
For binding contracts like sale, lease, and sulh, rescission by mutual agreement is called iqala. This is the default method in Hanafi fiqh.
Iqala is not a new contract; it reverses the original.

Chapter Forty-Five: Dissolution of the Contract. 1/45 - What is meant by dissolution of the contract is the removal of the legal bond that connected the contracting parties to the subject matter of the contract. So dissolution only occurs after prior formation. Hence the difference between dissolution and nullity of the contract becomes clear. For nullity is the state in which the contract has only sensory existence, without acquiring its legal existence in the view of the Law, i.e., the contract in the state of nullity is not contracted from its origin, like a child born dead. As for dissolution, it is the state in which we encounter a contract that is formed and producing its effects between its parties, but after existence it ceases and becomes non-existent due to a non-voluntary cause or a voluntary cause. So it is like a living person to whom death occurs. Then if the contract is removed by a voluntary cause, as will be explained soon, it is called rescission (faskh). And if the contract ceases due to a non-voluntary cause, it is called automatic dissolution (infisakh). 2/45 - (First): Rescission: Rescission is the dissolution of the contract bond... [reference]. Commentary
This passage defines contract dissolution (inhilal al-aqd) as the removal of the legal bond after a valid contract has been formed. It distinguishes dissolution from nullity (butlan): nullity means the contract never attained legal existence (like a stillbirth), while dissolution ends a living contract (like death). Two types are introduced: rescission (faskh) by voluntary cause, and automatic dissolution (infisakh) by non-voluntary cause. This aligns with Hanafi fiqh where iqala is a form of faskh by mutual consent.

Key Terms
انحلال العقد (inhilal al-aqd): Dissolution of contract: removal of the legal bond after valid formation.
بطلان (butlan): Nullity: state where a contract lacks legal existence from the outset.
فسخ (faskh): Rescission: voluntary dissolution of a contract, here by mutual agreement (iqala).
إقالة (iqala): Mutual rescission: rescission of a binding contract by agreement of both parties.

Dissolution of Contracts: The Hanafi Conceptual Framework
Defining contract dissolution and distinguishing rescission from cancellation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between dissolution (inhilal) and invalidity (butlan) of contracts, and apply the Hanafi rule that iqalah is treated as a new contract for third parties.

Core Idea
A contract's dissolution presupposes its valid existence; invalidity means it never legally existed.

This lesson breaks down the fundamental Hanafi distinction between a contract that never had legal existence (invalid) and one that was valid but has ceased (dissolved). You will also learn how mutual rescission (iqalah) operates: it is a pure rescission between parties but a new contract vis-à-vis third parties. Understanding these categories is essential for applying contract law correctly in fatwa and legal analysis.

Guided Path
1
Define dissolution and invalidity
Read the passage's definitions: dissolution (انحلال) is the removal of the legal tie after a valid contract; invalidity (بطلان) means the contract never acquired legal existence despite sensory existence.
Note the key phrase: 'like a child born dead' vs 'like a living person who dies'.
2
Distinguish by analogy
Internalise the two analogies: invalidity is a stillborn child (never lived), dissolution is a living person who later dies (had life).
This analogy is a powerful memorisation tool.
3
Differentiate rescission from termination
Rescission (فسخ) is voluntary dissolution; termination without consent (انفساخ) is involuntary. For binding contracts, rescission requires mutual agreement (إقالة). For non-binding contracts, it may be unilateral.
Example: sale is binding; agency is non-binding.
4
Analyse iqalah's dual nature
Between the parties, iqalah is a pure rescission returning them to pre-contract status. For third parties, it is a new contract. This protects third-party rights from collusion.
Study the shufa and defect examples in the passage.

Chapter Forty-Five: Dissolution of the Contract. 1/45 - By dissolution of the contract is meant the removal of the legal tie that bound the contracting parties to the subject matter of the contract. Dissolution only occurs after prior formation. Hence the difference between dissolution of the contract and its invalidity becomes clear. Invalidity is a state in which the contract has only a sensory existence, without acquiring its legal existence in the view of the Law, i.e., the contract in a state of invalidity is not concluded from its origin, like a child born dead. As for dissolution, it is the state in which we encounter a contract that is concluded and producing its effects between its two parties, but after existence it disappears and ceases due to an involuntary cause or a voluntary cause. It is like a living person who suddenly dies. If the contract is removed by a voluntary cause, as will be explained shortly, that is called 'rescission' (faskh). If the contract ceases due to an involuntary cause, that is called 'dissolution without consent' (infisakh). 2/45 - (First): Rescission: Rescission is the untying of the contract's bond (al-Ashbah, first of the rulings on rescissions, 195/2). Its general theory derives the right of rescission from the idea of bindingness: (a) As for contracts binding on both parties, such as sale, lease, and settlement, their rescission, like their formation, can only be by agreement of the two contracting parties, as we will see in the classification of contracts. In that case, it is called 'cancellation by mutual agreement' (iqalah). The Sixth Chapter: Theory of Contracts and Related Matters in Fiqh. This iqalah has two considerations in juristic view: - With respect to the two canceling parties, it is considered a pure rescission by which the canceled contract is dissolved, and the two parties return to their original state before the contract. - With respect to third parties, it is considered a new contract, to protect their acquired rights from being destroyed by collusion of the two parties through iqalah. For example, if real estate is sold and the right of pre-emption has been established for the pre-emptor, and the two parties cancel the sale and ownership returns to the seller, the seller is considered a new buyer through iqalah, and a new right of pre-emption is established for the pre-emptor. Similarly, if the buyer sells the sold item and then cancels with the second buyer and recovers the item, and then discovers a pre-existing defect, he cannot return it to the first seller due to the defect, because iqalah is like a new purchase with respect to the first seller. It is established in the rulings on fraud in defects that after the sold item leaves the ownership of its purchaser, if it returns to him through a new purchase, he does not return it due to a pre-existing defect to his seller, because a change in the cause of ownership is considered like a change in the essence of the owned thing, thus necessitating purification of the thing from previous rights. (R 76/12, al-Majalla 98). This is a rule in which modern legal thought agrees with Islamic fiqh. And every contract accepts iqalah except the marriage contract. This, and iqalah has legal conditions detailed in its chapter in fiqh books. (b) As for contracts not binding on each of the parties, such as partnership and agency, they are rescinded by the will of each party as long as there is no right of a third party attached to their continuation, as already explained in 6/40. (1) The jurists usually mention iqalah in a sub-chapter of the book of sales, considering that most iqalah occurs in sales, but it is appropriate to have a separate book for it after other contracts, because as we have shown, it can occur in every contract except marriage.
Source pages p. 561

Commentary
This passage is a conceptual exposition from Mustafa al-Zarqa's introduction to fiqh, explaining the dissolution of contracts. The author first defines dissolution (انحلال) as the removal of the legal tie between parties, emphasizing that it presupposes a validly concluded contract. He sharply distinguishes it from invalidity (بطلان): an invalid contract never had legal existence, like a stillborn child, while a dissolved contract had full legal effect but then ceases, like a living person who dies. This distinction is fundamental in Hanafi contract theory.

The author then divides dissolution into rescission (فسخ) when caused voluntarily, and termination without consent (انفساخ) when caused involuntarily. He next discusses rescission in detail. For binding contracts (لازمة) like sale, lease, and settlement, rescission requires mutual agreement of both parties; this mutual rescission is called iqalah (إقالة). For non-binding contracts like partnership and agency, each party can unilaterally rescind unless a third-party right is involved.

The passage then elaborates on the dual nature of iqalah: between the parties it is a pure rescission restoring them to pre-contract status; but regarding third parties, it is treated as a new contract. This protects third-party rights from collusive cancellation. The author gives examples from shufa (pre-emption) and defects. If a pre-emptor had a right in a property sale, and the parties cancel, the returning seller is considered a new buyer, so the pre-emptor gets a new right. Similarly, if a buyer reacquires an item via iqalah after selling it, he cannot claim a pre-existing defect against his original seller, because the change in ownership cause (from sale to iqalah) is like a change in the thing itself, wiping out previous defects. This aligns with modern legal thought. The only contract that cannot be iqalah is marriage.

The author notes that iqalah has detailed conditions in fiqh books, and that it is commonly discussed in the sale chapter but applies to most contracts except marriage.

Key Terms
انحلال العقد (inhilal al-'aqd): The removal of the legal tie connecting the parties after the contract was validly concluded.
بطلان (butlan): A state where the contract has sensory existence but no legal existence; it is void ab initio.
فسخ (faskh): Voluntary dissolution of a contract, either by mutual agreement (iqalah) in binding contracts or unilateral in non-binding contracts.
إقالة (iqalah): Mutual rescission of a binding contract, considered as a pure rescission between parties and a new contract regarding third parties.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the key difference between dissolution (انحلال) and invalidity (بطلان) of a contract?
A) Dissolution is permanent, invalidity is temporary.
B) Dissolution occurs only in marriage contracts, invalidity in all others.
C) Dissolution applies to a contract that was valid and then ceases; invalidity means the contract never had legal existence.
D) Dissolution is by mutual agreement, invalidity by court order.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi framework of contract dissolution hinges on the distinction between ab initio invalidity and post-formation cessation, with iqalah uniquely creating new rights for third parties.

Faskh and Infisakh: The Two Forms of Contract Dissolution
Explains the two main categories of contract dissolution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between faskh (rescission) and infisakh (automatic dissolution) and state examples of each from Hanafi fiqh.

Core Idea
Faskh requires a party's will; infisakh occurs by operation of law.

Contracts can end in two ways: by the will of an affected party (faskh) or automatically when performance becomes impossible (infisakh). This lesson unpacks these categories with examples from the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Define faskh and its triggers
Faskh occurs when a contract is binding on one party only (e.g., pledge) or when a defect of consent arises (e.g., option of defect). It requires a declaration by the affected party.
2
Define infisakh and its causes
Infisakh happens automatically when performance becomes impossible (e.g., sale of a perished object) or when a continuous contract's basis ceases (e.g., death in partnership). No party action needed.
3
Apply to specific contracts
In a sale, destruction of goods before delivery causes infisakh. In a pledge, the creditor-pledgee can rescind (faskh). In partnership, death of a partner causes infisakh (Hanafi view).
Note the difference: sale is instantaneous, partnership is continuous.
4
Recognize the general effect
Both types of dissolution return the parties to their pre-contractual position (إعادة العاقدين إلى سابق وضعهما).
This general principle may be modified by retroactivity (istinad) or confinement (iqtisar), which will be covered in a later lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

ومثل ذلك يقال في العقود اللازمة في الأصل بحق الطرفين، إذا طَرَأ عليها من عيوب الرضا ما يسلخ عنها صفة اللزوم في حق أحد الطرفين، كما في خيار العيب وغيره من الخيارات مما سَلَف بيانه، فإنها تفسخ بإرادة الطرف الذي شيبت إرادته، وعيب رضاه (۱) . (ر: ف ٦/٤٠ - ٩/٤٠) .
د - وتصرفات الإرادة المنفردة إذا كانت غير لازمة تفسخ بإرادة منشئها؛ كالوصية لجهة خيرية، فإنها تفسخ بإرادة الموصي نفسه

ينفسخ كل عقد من نفسه إذا استحال تنفيذه: وينفسخ العقد المستمر إذا فقد ما يعتمد عليه بقاؤه :
أ - فينفسخ البيع بهلاك المبيع قبل تسليمه لاستحالة تنفيذ العقد بالتسليم بعد هلاك محله .
أما موت البائع قبل التسليم فلا ينفسخ به البيع، بل يصبح الوارث مسؤولاً بالتسليم ما دام المبيع قائماً في التركة، لأن العقد الفوري كالبيع ونحوه، تثبت جميع آثاره جملة واحدة بمجرد انعقاده، فبقاء العاقد بعد ذلك وموته سواء.
ب - وتنفسخ الشركة والمضاربة (۱) والمزارعة والمساقاة بموت أحد العاقدين، لأنَّ هذه العقود تنشىء التزامات عملية ذات آثار متجددة فيها انسحاب واستمرار يعتمد بقاؤه بقاء العاقد علاوة على بقاء المحل ..
والإجارة على الراجح في الاجتهاد الحنفي كذلك تنفسخ بموت العاقدين.
أحد
وقوانينا اليوم على عدم انفساخ الإجارة بالموت، وهو الاجتهاد الشافعي ؛ والمصلحة اليوم تقتضيه
/٤/٤٥ - نتيجة انحلال العقد، والاستناد والاقتصار فيه
والمبدأ العام في نتائج انحلال العقد فسخاً أو انفساخاً أنه إعادة العاقدين إلى سابق وضعهما قبل التعاقد.
(۲) في الاصطلاح القانوني الشائع اليوم في عصرنا يسمى انسحاب الأحكام على الماضي : أثراً رجعياً. ويستعمل هذا التعبير في رجعية أحكام القوانين نفسها كما في آثار العقود على السواء. فيقال: هذا القانون له أثر رجعي وذاك ليس له، كما يقال: إن ملك الغير بدون إذنه إذا أجازه المالك يكون لإجازته أثر رجعي، فيعتبر حكم العقد سارياً منذ انعقاده لا منذ إجازته.
وليس في لغة القانون اسم لعدم الأثر الرجعي.
أما الفقه الإسلامي الغني بلغته واصطلاحاته التي تتجلى فيها عبقرية فقهائه خلال العصور فيسمي عدم رجعية الآثار اقتصاراً»، بمعنى أن الحكم يثبت مقتصراً على الحال لا منسحباً على الماضي. ويسمى رجعية الأثر: استناداً»، وهو اصطلاح المذهب الحنفي. ويسميه المالكية : انعطافاً. وقانوننا المدني الجديد قد اقتبس واضعو أصله المصري هذا الاصطلاح من الفقه الإسلامي، فاستعملوا فيه لفظ الاستناد بمعنى الأثر الرجعي.
Chapter (45): Dissolution of Contracts
C - A contract that is binding on one party only, such as a pledge (rahn), is rescinded by the will of the party for whom it is not binding, namely the creditor-pledgee. The same applies to contracts originally binding on both parties, when defects of consent arise that strip the contract of its binding character for one party, such as in the option of defect and other options that have been explained; they are rescinded by the will of the party whose consent was flawed. (See: F 40/6 - 40/9).
D - Unilateral acts of will, if non-binding, are rescinded by the will of their originator; e.g., a will for a charitable purpose is rescinded by the testator's own will.
3/45 - (Second): Automatic Dissolution (Infisakh):
Every contract dissolves by itself if its performance becomes impossible; a continuous contract dissolves if its basis for continuation ceases to exist.
A - A sale dissolves by the destruction of the sold object before delivery, due to the impossibility of performing the contract by delivery after the subject matter has perished. However, the death of the seller before delivery does not dissolve the sale; rather, the heir becomes responsible for delivery as long as the sold object remains in the estate, because an instantaneous contract like a sale establishes all its effects at once upon its conclusion, so the continued existence or death of the contracting party thereafter is the same.
B - Partnership, mudarabah, muzara'ah, and musaqah dissolve upon the death of one of the contracting parties, because these contracts create ongoing obligations with recurring effects, whose continuation depends on the survival of the contracting party in addition to the survival of the subject matter.
According to the preferred Hanafi opinion, ijarah (lease) also dissolves upon the death of one of the contracting parties. However, our current laws are on the basis that lease does not dissolve upon death, which is the Shafi'i opinion; expediency today requires it.
4/45 - Result of Contract Dissolution, and Retroactivity and Confinement Therein:
The general principle regarding the results of contract dissolution, whether by rescission or automatic dissolution, is that it returns the contracting parties to their pre-contractual position.
(Footnote 2): In modern legal terminology, the application of rules to the past is called retroactive effect. This expression is used for the retroactivity of laws themselves as well as for the effects of contracts. It is said: This law has retroactive effect and that does not. Similarly, it is said: If the owner ratifies a contract made without his permission, his ratification has retroactive effect, so the contract is considered effective from its conclusion, not from the ratification. There is no term in legal language for the absence of retroactive effect. However, Islamic jurisprudence, rich in its language and terminology that reveal the genius of its jurists over the centuries, calls the non-retroactivity of effects 'iqtisar' (confinement), meaning that the ruling is established confined to the present moment, not extending to the past. And it calls retroactive effect 'istinad' (reference back), which is the terminology of the Hanafi school. The Malikis call it 'in'itaf' (turning back). The new civil code, whose Egyptian originators borrowed this term from Islamic jurisprudence, uses the word 'istinad' to mean retroactive effect.
Source pages p. 563

Commentary
This passage from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Aam by Mustafa al-Zarqa discusses the dissolution of contracts in Islamic jurisprudence. It opens by classifying types of dissolution: rescission (faskh) and automatic dissolution (infisakh). The author first addresses contracts binding on one side, like pledge (rahn), which can be rescinded by the party not bound (the creditor-pledgee). Similarly, when a defect of consent appears (e.g., option of defect), the affected party can rescind. Unilateral non-binding acts like a charitable will can be rescinded by the originator. Then the passage covers automatic dissolution: a contract dissolves if performance becomes impossible (e.g., sale of a destroyed object). Death of a party affects continuous contracts (like partnership, lease in Hanafi) differently from instantaneous contracts (like sale, where death does not dissolve). There is an inter-madhhab dispute: Hanafis hold that lease dissolves upon death of a party, while Shafi'is and modern laws (taking expediency) hold it does not. Finally, the general result of dissolution is restoration of pre-contract status, with an explanation of Hanafi terminology: 'iqtisar' (non-retroactive) and 'istinad' (retroactive effect), which modern civil law has borrowed.

Key Terms
فسخ (faskh): إزالة العقد بإرادة أحد الطرفين بسبب عيب في الرضا أو لعدم اللزوم في حق الفاسخ
انفساخ (infisakh): انحلال العقد من نفسه باستحالة تنفيذه أو بفقد ما يعتمد عليه بقاؤه
استناد (istinad): رجعية الأثر، أي سريان حكم الفسخ أو الإجازة على الماضي
اقتصار (iqtisar): عدم رجعية الأثر، أي أن الحكم يثبت مقتصراً على الحال لا منسحباً على الماضي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the term for the automatic dissolution of a contract when performance becomes impossible?
A) Faskh
B) Infisakh
C) Iqtisar
D) Istinad

Key Takeaway
Faskh is rescission by a party's will; infisakh is automatic dissolution by operation of law.

Legal Philosophy and Theory
intermediate

Contract Dissolution upon Death: The Ijarah Dispute
Discusses the inter-madhhab dispute on lease dissolution upon death.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi and Shafi'i positions on whether a lease dissolves upon the death of a party and explain the modern tarjih.

Core Idea
Hanafis hold lease dissolves with death; Shafi'is and modern laws hold it continues — expediency today favors the Shafi'i view.

The Hanafi school traditionally held that a lease contract (ijarah) dissolves automatically upon the death of either party, similar to partnership. However, the Shafi'i school argues it is a binding contract that passes to heirs. This lesson examines the debate and its contemporary resolution.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
According to the preferred Hanafi opinion (الراجح), ijarah dissolves upon the death of either contracting party because it is a continuous contract depending on the survival of the parties.
The text states: 'والإجارة على الراجح في الاجتهاد الحنفي كذلك تنفسخ بموت أحد العاقدين.'
2
State the Shafi'i position
The Shafi'i school holds that ijarah does not dissolve upon death; it remains binding and passes to the heirs, who must fulfill the obligations.
This is the view adopted by modern civil codes.
3
Identify the basis of the difference
The Hanafi view emphasizes the continuous nature of lease and the need for the party's ongoing capacity. The Shafi'i view treats lease as a binding contract (عقد لازم) that is inherited.
4
Note the modern tarjih
The text says: 'وقوانينا اليوم على عدم انفساخ الإجارة بالموت، وهو الاجتهاد الشافعي؛ والمصلحة اليوم تقتضيه.' Thus for fatwa today, the Shafi'i view is preferred for expediency (maslahah).
This shows that tarjih can change based on time and circumstance.

Source Text

Legal Philosophy and Theory

Istinad and Iqtisar: Retroactivity in Contract Dissolution
Explains the Hanafi terminology for retroactive and non-retroactive effects of dissolution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define istinad and iqtisar and explain their application in Hanafi contract law.

Core Idea
Istinad means retroactive effect; iqtisar means confinement to the present.

When a contract is dissolved, does the dissolution cancel the contract from its inception or only from the moment of dissolution? The Hanafi school uses the terms istinad (retroactive effect) and iqtisar (confinement) to describe these two possibilities. This lesson clarifies these concepts as introduced by al-Zarqa.

Guided Path
1
Define istinad
Istinad is the retroactive effect of a legal act, meaning that the ruling extends to the past. For example, if a contract is rescinded due to a defect, the rescission is effective from the contract's inception, as if it never existed.
The text says: 'يسمى رجعية الأثر: استناداً'
2
Define iqtisar
Iqtisar is the confinement of effect to the present, meaning the dissolution only applies from now onward. For example, automatic dissolution due to impossibility from a certain point does not retroactively invalidate past performance.
The text says: 'عدم رجعية الآثار اقتصاراً'
3
Apply to examples from the passage
In faskh (rescission for defect), the rescission has retroactive effect (istinad), so the parties are restored to their pre-contract positions from the beginning. In infisakh (e.g., death in partnership), the dissolution operates only from the time of death (iqtisar), so past transactions remain valid.
The passage does not explicitly assign which type has which effect, but the general principle of restoration is modified by these concepts.
4
Note modern usage
The modern civil code has borrowed the term 'istinad' to mean retroactive effect, showing the influence of Hanafi terminology.
The text mentions that the Egyptian civil code uses 'istinad'.

Source Text
