/٤/٤٥ - نتيجة انحلال العقد، والاستناد والاقتصار فيه
والمبدأ العام في نتائج انحلال العقد فسخاً أو انفساخاً أنه إعادة العاقدين إلى سابق وضعهما قبل التعاقد.
(۲) في الاصطلاح القانوني الشائع اليوم في عصرنا يسمى انسحاب الأحكام على الماضي : أثراً رجعياً. ويستعمل هذا التعبير في رجعية أحكام القوانين نفسها كما في آثار العقود على السواء. فيقال: هذا القانون له أثر رجعي وذاك ليس له، كما يقال: إن ملك الغير بدون إذنه إذا أجازه المالك يكون لإجازته أثر رجعي، فيعتبر حكم العقد سارياً منذ انعقاده لا منذ إجازته.
وليس في لغة القانون اسم لعدم الأثر الرجعي.
أما الفقه الإسلامي الغني بلغته واصطلاحاته التي تتجلى فيها عبقرية فقهائه خلال العصور فيسمي عدم رجعية الآثار اقتصاراً»، بمعنى أن الحكم يثبت مقتصراً على الحال لا منسحباً على الماضي. ويسمى رجعية الأثر: استناداً»، وهو اصطلاح المذهب الحنفي. ويسميه المالكية : انعطافاً. وقانوننا المدني الجديد قد اقتبس واضعو أصله المصري هذا الاصطلاح من الفقه الإسلامي، فاستعملوا فيه لفظ الاستناد بمعنى الأثر الرجعي.
Chapter (45): Dissolution of Contracts
C - A contract that is binding on one party only, such as a pledge (rahn), is rescinded by the will of the party for whom it is not binding, namely the creditor-pledgee. The same applies to contracts originally binding on both parties, when defects of consent arise that strip the contract of its binding character for one party, such as in the option of defect and other options that have been explained; they are rescinded by the will of the party whose consent was flawed. (See: F 40/6 - 40/9).
D - Unilateral acts of will, if non-binding, are rescinded by the will of their originator; e.g., a will for a charitable purpose is rescinded by the testator's own will.
3/45 - (Second): Automatic Dissolution (Infisakh):
Every contract dissolves by itself if its performance becomes impossible; a continuous contract dissolves if its basis for continuation ceases to exist.
A - A sale dissolves by the destruction of the sold object before delivery, due to the impossibility of performing the contract by delivery after the subject matter has perished. However, the death of the seller before delivery does not dissolve the sale; rather, the heir becomes responsible for delivery as long as the sold object remains in the estate, because an instantaneous contract like a sale establishes all its effects at once upon its conclusion, so the continued existence or death of the contracting party thereafter is the same.
B - Partnership, mudarabah, muzara'ah, and musaqah dissolve upon the death of one of the contracting parties, because these contracts create ongoing obligations with recurring effects, whose continuation depends on the survival of the contracting party in addition to the survival of the subject matter.
According to the preferred Hanafi opinion, ijarah (lease) also dissolves upon the death of one of the contracting parties. However, our current laws are on the basis that lease does not dissolve upon death, which is the Shafi'i opinion; expediency today requires it.
4/45 - Result of Contract Dissolution, and Retroactivity and Confinement Therein:
The general principle regarding the results of contract dissolution, whether by rescission or automatic dissolution, is that it returns the contracting parties to their pre-contractual position.
(Footnote 2): In modern legal terminology, the application of rules to the past is called retroactive effect. This expression is used for the retroactivity of laws themselves as well as for the effects of contracts. It is said: This law has retroactive effect and that does not. Similarly, it is said: If the owner ratifies a contract made without his permission, his ratification has retroactive effect, so the contract is considered effective from its conclusion, not from the ratification. There is no term in legal language for the absence of retroactive effect. However, Islamic jurisprudence, rich in its language and terminology that reveal the genius of its jurists over the centuries, calls the non-retroactivity of effects 'iqtisar' (confinement), meaning that the ruling is established confined to the present moment, not extending to the past. And it calls retroactive effect 'istinad' (reference back), which is the terminology of the Hanafi school. The Malikis call it 'in'itaf' (turning back). The new civil code, whose Egyptian originators borrowed this term from Islamic jurisprudence, uses the word 'istinad' to mean retroactive effect.
Source pages p. 563

Commentary
This passage from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Aam by Mustafa al-Zarqa discusses the dissolution of contracts in Islamic jurisprudence. It opens by classifying types of dissolution: rescission (faskh) and automatic dissolution (infisakh). The author first addresses contracts binding on one side, like pledge (rahn), which can be rescinded by the party not bound (the creditor-pledgee). Similarly, when a defect of consent appears (e.g., option of defect), the affected party can rescind. Unilateral non-binding acts like a charitable will can be rescinded by the originator. Then the passage covers automatic dissolution: a contract dissolves if performance becomes impossible (e.g., sale of a destroyed object). Death of a party affects continuous contracts (like partnership, lease in Hanafi) differently from instantaneous contracts (like sale, where death does not dissolve). There is an inter-madhhab dispute: Hanafis hold that lease dissolves upon death of a party, while Shafi'is and modern laws (taking expediency) hold it does not. Finally, the general result of dissolution is restoration of pre-contract status, with an explanation of Hanafi terminology: 'iqtisar' (non-retroactive) and 'istinad' (retroactive effect), which modern civil law has borrowed.

Key Terms
فسخ (faskh): إزالة العقد بإرادة أحد الطرفين بسبب عيب في الرضا أو لعدم اللزوم في حق الفاسخ
انفساخ (infisakh): انحلال العقد من نفسه باستحالة تنفيذه أو بفقد ما يعتمد عليه بقاؤه
استناد (istinad): رجعية الأثر، أي سريان حكم الفسخ أو الإجازة على الماضي
اقتصار (iqtisar): عدم رجعية الأثر، أي أن الحكم يثبت مقتصراً على الحال لا منسحباً على الماضي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi term for the retroactive effect of contract dissolution?
A) Iqtisar
B) Istinad
C) Faskh
D) Infisakh

Key Takeaway
Istinad refers to retroactive effect, iqtisar to confinement to the present — essential terms for precise legal reasoning.

Rescission vs. Specific Performance: The Hanafi Position and Its Critical Refinement
Critical refinement of the Hanafi rule on rescission versus specific performance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the classical Hanafi position on rescission for breach of contract and evaluate the argument for granting the non-breaching party the option to rescind instead of only specific performance.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, specific performance is the primary remedy; rescission is only allowed if specific performance is impossible.

This lesson examines a classical Hanafi rule regarding breach of contract: when one party fails to perform, the other can only demand specific performance, not rescission. The passage critiques this limitation and argues that Islamic law can accommodate a more flexible approach that grants the non-breaching party a choice. We will explore the traditional ruling, the reasoning behind it, and the modern critique.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Classical Hanafi Position
State the ruling as described in the passage: most Hanafi jurists do not give the non-breaching party the right to rescind if the defaulter can be judicially compelled to perform specifically.
This is the zahir al-riwayah position.
2
Understand the Rationale
Explain why: the judge's role is to deliver rights to their owners, so compelling performance achieves the contract's purpose; rescission is unnecessary.
This reasoning is based on the principle that the contract should be fulfilled as agreed.
3
Examine the Alternative View
The passage presents a modern legal view: the non-breaching party may choose between demanding specific performance or seeking rescission, release from obligations, and damages.
This view is said to be accepted by some of the four schools in certain contracts.
4
Analyze the Author's Endorsement
The author argues that Sharia principles are broad enough to accommodate this alternative and finds the modern legal perspective sound.
This is a critical refinement: the author does not reject the classical view but expands it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٦/٤٥ - حق العاقد في الفسخ عند تخلف العاقد الآخر عن التنفيذ: إذا كان العقد ملزماً لجانبين كالبيع والإجارة والصلح وسائر عقود المعاوضات ونَكَلَ أحد عاقديه أو تخلف عن تنفيذ التزامه الذي أوجبه عليه العقد، كما لو امتنع أو تخلف البائع عن تسليم المبيع، أو تخلف المشتري عن تسليم الثمن في حينه، فإنَّ معظم فقهاء الشريعة الإسلامية في المذهب الحنفي وغيره لا يعطون العاقد غير المتخلف حقاً في فسخ العقد ما دام يمكن إجبار المتخلف على تنفيذ التزامه عيناً بقوة القضاء لأن مهمة القاضي هي إيصال ذوي الحقوق إلى حقوقهم فلا موجب للفسخ .
ولكن المبادىء والنظريات القانونية تعطي في هذه الحال العاقد غير المتخلف حقاً في طلب فسخ العقد بطريق القضاء إلى جانب حقه في طلب التنفيذ العيني. فهو بالخيار : إن شاء طَلَب من القاضي إجبار العاقد الآخر على التنفيذ، وإن شاء طَلَب فسخ العقد والتحلل من التزاماته ورد ما نفذه طلب تعويض الضرر الذي لحقه من جَرَّاء ذلك .
منها
وحجة علماء القانون في ذلك أن تنفيذ الالتزامات العقدية تختلف فيه مصالح العاقدين بحسب الوقت، فقد تفوت مصلحة العاقد في التنفيذ العيني المتأخر عن وقته، بسبب تخلّف العاقد الآخر أو امتناعه عن التنفيذ في حينه، فيجب أن يسوغ لرفيقه المتعاقد معه حق التحلل من الرابطة العقدية إذا وجد مصلحته في ذلك ويتضمن ذلك معنى الجزاء بالنسبة للطرف المتخلف.
ونحن نرى أن النظر القانوني وجيه وأنَّ قواعد الشريعة ومبادئها الفقهية تتسع له ،وتتقبله، كما أن بعض المذاهب الأربعة نفسها قد أقره في بعض العقود صراحة ولم ينفه في سواها.
6/45 - The right of the contracting party to rescind when the other contracting party defaults in execution: If the contract is binding on both sides, such as sale, lease, compromise, and all commutative contracts, and one of the two contractors reneges or defaults in executing the obligation that the contract imposed on him, such as if the seller refuses or defaults in delivering the sold item, or the buyer defaults in paying the price at its time, then most jurists of Islamic law, in the Hanafi school and others, do not give the non-defaulting party a right to rescind the contract as long as it is possible to compel the defaulter to execute his obligation specifically by judicial force, because the role of the judge is to deliver rights to their owners, so there is no cause for rescission.
However, legal principles and theories give in this case the non-defaulting party a right to request rescission of the contract through the judiciary alongside his right to request specific performance. He has a choice: if he wishes, he asks the judge to compel the other party to perform; and if he wishes, he asks for rescission of the contract, release from his obligations, return of what he executed, and claim compensation for the damage he suffered as a result.
Some of them
The argument of legal scholars for that is that the execution of contractual obligations varies according to time in the interests of the contracting parties. The interest of the party in specific performance delayed from its time may be lost due to the other party's default or refusal to execute at its time. Therefore, it should be permissible for his co-contractor to have the right to dissolve the contractual bond if he finds his interest in that, and this includes the meaning of penalty with respect to the defaulting party.
We see that the legal perspective is sound and that the rules of Sharia and its jurisprudential principles accommodate and accept it, as some of the four schools themselves have explicitly approved it in some contracts and have not denied it in others.
Source pages p. 567

Commentary
This passage discusses a key legal issue: whether the non-breaching party can demand rescission instead of specific performance when the other party fails to fulfill their contractual obligation. The author states that the majority of Islamic jurists, including the Hanafi school, do not grant this right if specific performance can be compelled judicially. Their rationale is that the judge's role is to enforce the contract as agreed, so rescission is unnecessary. However, modern legal theory gives the non-breaching party an option to either demand specific performance or seek rescission and damages. The author finds this view persuasive and argues that Islamic legal principles can accommodate it, noting that some of the four Sunni schools already explicitly grant the right of rescission for certain contracts. This suggests an openness to adapting classical fiqh to contemporary needs without violating fundamental Sharia principles.

Key Terms
فسخ (faskh): إنهاء العقد بأثر رجعي بناء على طلب أحد المتعاقدين
تنفيذ عيني (tanfidh 'ayni): إلزام المتخلف بتنفيذ الالتزام نفسه كما هو في العقد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority of Hanafi jurists as stated in this passage, what is the primary remedy when a party breaches a bilateral contract like sale?
A) The non-breaching party can immediately rescind the contract.
B) The judge must force specific performance, and rescission is not allowed if performance is possible.
C) The non-breaching party can choose between specific performance and rescission with damages.
D) The contract is automatically void.

Key Takeaway
The classical Hanafi rule prioritizes specific performance over rescission, but modern legal reasoning and flexibility in the madhhab allow for rescission as an option when it serves justice and the parties' interests.

Right of Rescission upon Non-Performance: Hanafi Principles and Broader Accommodation
Iftaa-usul analysis of rescission right

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'la darar wa la dirar' to justify rescission in a bilateral contract and evaluate the Hanafi position against the Hanbali alternative.

Core Idea
The harm maxim can override the classical Hanafi preference for specific performance, allowing rescission.

This passage from Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa tackles a critical tension in Hanafi contract law: should a party who suffers from the other's default be allowed to rescind the contract, or must they only demand specific performance? The author presents the traditional Hanafi view that forbids rescission if specific performance is possible, then argues that the Prophetic maxim 'No harm and no reciprocating harm' (la darar wa la dirar) can justify rescission, especially in light of Hanbali rulings. We will walk through the terminology, the positions, and the evidence to see how a mufti can apply these rules today.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish faskh from inha
Identify the two types of contract dissolution: faskh (rescission due to a contingency like breach) and inha (natural termination). Understand that the classical Hanafi view restricts faskh to cases where specific performance is impossible.
Faskh terminates retroactively; inha ends normally.
2
State the classical Hanafi position
In binding bilateral contracts (sale, lease, etc.), the non-defaulting party cannot rescind as long as the court can force the defaulting party to perform specifically. The judge's duty is to deliver rights, not to dissolve contracts.
This is the majority view in Hanafi fiqh.
3
Present the opposing view: the right to rescind
Modern legal theories and the Hanbali school grant an option: demand specific performance OR rescind with restitution and damages. The author supports this view using the harm maxim.
Opposing school: Hanbali (and contemporary law).
4
Examine the evidence from hadith and Hanbali fiqh
The hadith 'la darar wa la dirar' negates harm. Delayed performance may cause harm, and the court cannot turn back time. Hanbali examples: wife's right to rescind marriage for breach of valid condition; seller's right to rescind if buyer is absent or insolvent.
These show that rescission is recognized even in less flexible contracts like marriage.
5
Synthesize the author's conclusion
Al-Zarqa argues that Sharia principles accommodate rescission. The Hanafi school can adopt this view through the harm maxim and by analogy to Hanbali rulings, harmonizing with positive law.
This is an expansion probe: reconciling classical rules with modern needs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل (٤٥) انحلال العقد
هذا ونرى من المستحسن التمييز في تسمية انحلال العقد بين حالتي الاستناد والاقتصار فيسمى الحل والانحلال في حالة الاستناد فسخاً وانفساخاً، وفي حالة الاقتصار: إنهاء وانتهاء.
٦/٤٥ - حق العاقد في الفسخ عند تخلف العاقد الآخر عن التنفيذ: إذا كان العقد ملزماً لجانبين كالبيع والإجارة والصلح وسائر عقود المعاوضات ونَكَلَ أحد عاقديه أو تخلف عن تنفيذ التزامه الذي أوجبه عليه العقد، كما لو امتنع أو تخلف البائع عن تسليم المبيع، أو تخلف المشتري عن تسليم الثمن في حينه، فإنَّ معظم فقهاء الشريعة الإسلامية في المذهب الحنفي وغيره لا يعطون العاقد غير المتخلف حقاً في فسخ العقد ما دام يمكن إجبار المتخلف على تنفيذ التزامه عيناً بقوة القضاء لأن مهمة القاضي هي إيصال ذوي الحقوق إلى حقوقهم فلا موجب للفسخ .
ولكن المبادىء والنظريات القانونية تعطي في هذه الحال العاقد غير المتخلف حقاً في طلب فسخ العقد بطريق القضاء إلى جانب حقه في طلب التنفيذ العيني. فهو بالخيار : إن شاء طَلَب من القاضي إجبار العاقد الآخر على التنفيذ، وإن شاء طَلَب فسخ العقد والتحلل من التزاماته ورد ما نفذه طلب تعويض الضرر الذي لحقه من جَرَّاء ذلك .
منها
وحجة علماء القانون في ذلك أن تنفيذ الالتزامات العقدية تختلف فيه مصالح العاقدين بحسب الوقت، فقد تفوت مصلحة العاقد في التنفيذ العيني المتأخر عن وقته، بسبب تخلّف العاقد الآخر أو امتناعه عن التنفيذ في حينه، فيجب أن يسوغ لرفيقه المتعاقد معه حق التحلل من الرابطة العقدية إذا وجد مصلحته في ذلك ويتضمن ذلك معنى الجزاء بالنسبة للطرف المتخلف.
ونحن نرى أن النظر القانوني وجيه وأنَّ قواعد الشريعة ومبادئها الفقهية تتسع له ،وتتقبله، كما أن بعض المذاهب الأربعة نفسها قد أقره في بعض العقود صراحة ولم ينفه في سواها.

الباب السادس : نظرية العقود وما إليها في الفقه
أ - فمن حيث القواعد والمبادىء الفقهية يشهد لحق الفسخ، الحديث النبوي القائل: (لا ضرر ولا ضرار) فإنَّ تنفيذ العقد في غير حينه المتفق عليه بسبب تخلف أحد عاقديه أو تمرده عن التنفيذ قد يلحق ضرراً كبيراً بالعاقد الآخر، والضرر منفي بالنص، والقضاء لا يستطيع إعادة الزمن.
ب - وفي المذاهب يصرح فقهاء المذهب الحنبلي في عقد النكاح أن المرأة إذا شرطت على الرجل في عقد الزواج شرطاً صحيحاً فلم ينفذه كان لها حق فسخ الزواج.
قال العلامة موفق الدين عبدالله بن قدامة المقدسي في باب الشروط في النكاح من كتابه «المقنع» ما نصه:
الشروط قسمان: شرط صحيح مثل اشتراط زيادة في المهر، أو نقد معيَّن، أو أن لا يخرجها من دارها أو بلدها، أو لا يتزوج عليها فهذا صحيح لازم، إن وفى به، وإلا فلها الفسخ».
فأنت ترى أن معظم هذه الشروط يمكن أن يجبر القاضي الزوج على احترامها وتنفيذها، ومع ذلك أعطيت المرأة حق الفسخ.
وإذا صح إعطاؤها لهذا السبب حق الفسخ في النكاح الذي هو أقل العقود قابلية للفسخ - ولذا لا تجري فيه الإقالة - ففي غير الزواج من العقود المالية المحضة يقبل الفقه هذا الحق بطريق الأولوية.
وجاء أيضاً في فصل الخيار السابع من كتاب البيع في «المقنع» لابن قدامة نفسه ما نصه :
وإن كان الثمن ديناً أجبر البائع على تسليم المبيع ثم يجبر المشتري على تسليم الثمن إن كان حاضراً. وإن كان - أي الثمن - غائباً بعيداً، أو كان المشتري معسراً، فللبائع الفسخ» إلخ.
وهناك أيضاً نصوص في المذهب المالكي يظهر منها للمتتبع أن إعطاء حق الفسخ في بعض العقود عند تخلف العاقد الآخر، وبذلك ينسجم الفقه الإسلامي مع القانون الوضعي في هذه المسألة.
Chapter (45) Dissolution of Contract. We consider it advisable to distinguish in terminology the dissolution of a contract between the cases of contingency and limit, so dissolution in the case of contingency is called 'faskh' (rescission) and 'infisakh' (dissolution), and in the case of limit: 'inha' (termination) and 'intiha' (end). 6/45 - The right of the contracting party to rescind upon the other's default: If the contract is binding on both sides, like sale, lease, compromise, and all commutative contracts, and one of the contracting parties fails or defaults in executing his obligation that the contract imposed on him, such as if the seller refuses or defaults in delivering the sold item, or the buyer defaults in paying the price at its time, then most jurists of Islamic law in the Hanafi school and others do not give the non-defaulting party a right to rescind the contract as long as the defaulting party can be forced to execute his obligation in specific performance by judicial power, because the judge's task is to deliver rights to their owners, so there is no cause for rescission. However, legal principles and theories give in this case the non-defaulting party a right to demand rescission of the contract judicially alongside his right to demand specific performance. So he has an option: if he wishes, he asks the judge to force the other party to perform, and if he wishes, he asks for rescission of the contract and release from his obligations and return of what he performed and demand compensation for the harm that befell him due to that. The argument of legal scholars in this is that the execution of contractual obligations varies in the interests of the contracting parties according to time; the benefit of specific performance delayed from its time may be lost due to the other party's default or refusal to perform at its time, so it should be permissible for his counterparty to have the right to dissolve the contractual bond if he finds his interest in that, and that entails a sense of penalty for the defaulting party. We see that the legal view is sound and that the rules of Sharia and its jurisprudential principles are broad enough to accommodate it and accept it, just as some of the four schools themselves have explicitly approved it in some contracts and did not deny it in others. Chapter Six: Theory of Contracts and Related Matters in Fiqh. A - In terms of jurisprudential rules and principles, the prophetic hadith: 'No harm and no reciprocating harm' supports the right of rescission, for executing the contract at a time other than its agreed time due to the default or rebellion of one of its parties may cause great harm to the other party, and harm is negated by the text, and the judiciary cannot turn back time. B - Among the schools, Hanbali jurists state in the marriage contract that if a woman stipulates a valid condition on the man in the marriage contract and he does not fulfill it, she has the right to dissolve the marriage. Imam Muwaffaq al-Din Abdullah ibn Qudama al-Maqdisi said in the chapter on conditions in marriage from his book 'Al-Muqni': 'Conditions are two types: a valid condition, such as stipulating an increase in the dowry, or a specific cash, or that he does not take her out of her house or town, or does not marry another, this is valid and binding; if he fulfills it, then good, otherwise she has the right of rescission.' So you see that most of these conditions can be enforced by the judge, yet the woman is given the right of rescission. If this right is given for such a reason in marriage, which is the least susceptible to rescission among contracts—hence no revocation applies—then in contracts other than marriage, which are purely financial, fiqh accepts this right by priority. Also in the seventh chapter on options from the book of sale in 'Al-Muqni' by Ibn Qudama himself, it says: 'If the price is a debt, the seller is forced to deliver the sold item, then the buyer is forced to pay the price if he is present. If the price is absent and far, or the buyer is insolvent, then the seller has the right to rescission,' etc. There are also texts in the Maliki school showing to the observer that they give the right of rescission in some contracts upon default of the other party. Thus Islamic jurisprudence harmonizes with positive law on this issue.
Source pages p. 567

Commentary
This passage is a legal philosophy analysis by Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa on the right to rescind a contract due to non-performance by the other party. The author begins by distinguishing two types of contract dissolution: faskh (rescission) when the dissolution is due to a contingency (such as breach), and inha (termination) when the contract ends by its natural limit (completion or expiry). He then presents the classical Hanafi position: in binding bilateral contracts (e.g., sale, lease), if one party defaults, the non-defaulting party cannot rescind as long as the court can force specific performance. This is because the judge's role is to deliver rights, not to dissolve contracts. However, modern legal theories grant the non-defaulting party an option: either demand specific performance or seek rescission, release from obligations, restitution, and damages. The author argues that the 'no harm' hadith (la darar wa la dirar) supports this right, as delayed performance can cause harm. He then provides evidence from the Hanbali school: in marriage, if a woman stipulates a valid condition (e.g., that her husband not take her abroad) and he violates it, she has the right to rescind (faskh) the marriage. Since marriage is the least rescindable contract (no iqala/revocation), by priority, rescission is more appropriate in purely financial contracts. He also cites a Hanbali rule in sales: if the price is a debt and the buyer is absent or insolvent, the seller can rescind. Thus, the author concludes that Islamic jurisprudence, through recognized principles and rulings in other madhhabs, can accommodate the right of rescission for non-performance, harmonizing with modern law.

Key Terms
فسخ (faskh): Rescission of contract due to a contingency (e.g., default of party); terminates the contract retroactively.
انفساخ (infisakh): Automatic dissolution of contract without act of party, e.g., death of one party.
انهاء (inha'): Termination of contract upon completion of its purpose or expiry of term.
شرط صحيح (shart sahih): Valid condition that does not contradict the essence of the contract; binding and enforceable.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Dr. al-Zarqa, what evidence from the Hanbali school supports the right of rescission upon non-performance in financial contracts?
A) The hadith 'لا ضرر ولا ضرار' alone.
B) The wife's right to rescind marriage for breach of a valid condition, and the seller's right to rescind sale when buyer is absent or insolvent.
C) The Maliki texts allowing rescission only in marriage.
D) The Hanafi ruling that no rescission is ever allowed.

Key Takeaway
The harm maxim can justify rescission in bilateral contracts even when specific performance is possible, aligning Hanafi fiqh with modern legal needs.

Contract Dissolution vs. Obligation Extinction
Defining and contrasting dissolution of contract with extinction of obligation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the precise legal difference between inhilal al-aqd and inqida' al-iltizam, and apply this distinction to examples from sale and lease contracts.

Core Idea
Performance extinguishes obligations; dissolution extinguishes the contract itself — they are not the same.

In the study of contract theory, we learn that a contract is a source of obligations, not the obligation itself. This passage sharpens a critical distinction: when parties perform their duties, the obligations end, but the contract remains. In contrast, when a contract is dissolved, the entire contractual bond is severed, which not only ends primary obligations but may also create new ones, such as restitution. Understanding this difference is essential for accurate legal analysis in mu'amalat and fatwa issuance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two legal events
The passage defines two events: 'inhilal al-aqd' (dissolution of the contract) and 'inqida' al-iltizam' (extinction of an obligation). Note that the contract is a source, the obligation is its effect.
inhilal al-aqd ends the contract; inqida' al-iltizam ends a duty under the contract.
2
Understand the analogy
The author compares the difference to 'the disappearance of the cause and the end of the effect'. The contract is the cause, obligations are its effects.
Thus, extinction of an obligation (effect) does not necessarily mean the cause (contract) is gone.
3
Apply to the sale example
Mutual delivery of price and goods extinguishes the obligation to perform (inqida'), but the contract itself is not dissolved; it has been fulfilled. Rescission (faskh) dissolves the contract, ending primary duties and creating new ones (restitution).
Faskh severs the bond; performance does not.
4
Analyze secondary obligations
Dissolution may generate new obligations (e.g., restitution). The passage stresses that extinction of prior obligations does not prevent new ones arising from dissolution.
Example: after dissolution and receipt, both parties must return what they took.
5
Extend to the ijarah example
The same principle applies to lease: dissolution after receipt of the object and rent obligates the lessor to return the prepaid rent for the remaining period, and the lessee to return the object.
The text is cut off but the pattern is clear.

إن انحلال العقد يجعل المتعاقدين في حل من الرابطة التي كانت بينهما .

7/45 – The distinction between dissolution of the contract and extinction of the obligation: We saw at the beginning of the theory of contracts that the contract is one of the sources creating the obligation, and it is not the obligation itself; rather, the obligation is its effect (see: 5/27). From that it becomes clear that the extinction of the obligation is not the same as the dissolution of the contract. The difference between them is like the difference between the disappearance of the cause and the end of the effect. Thus, the mutual delivery of the sold item and the price by the two parties to a sale is an extinction of the obligation to execute the contract, and it is not a dissolution of the contract, and so on. For clarification, we say: The dissolution of the contract frees the two contracting parties from the bond that existed between them. This dissolution entails the extinction of the obligations that arose from the dissolved contract. So, for example, the rescission of a sale terminates the buyer's obligation to pay the price and also terminates the seller's obligation to deliver the sold item. Similarly, every contract by its dissolution extinguishes the obligations arising from its formation. However, this extinction of the prior obligations generated from the contract before its dissolution does not prevent the emergence of new obligations arising from the dissolution: For example, if the dissolution of a sale occurs after the mutual receipt of the two considerations, it necessitates the obligation of restitution. The seller becomes obligated to return the price, and the buyer becomes obligated to return the sold item. And the dissolution of a lease after mutual receipt of the leased object and rent obligates the lessor to return the prepaid rent for the remaining period of the lease, and also obligates the lessee to...
Source pages p. 569

Commentary
This passage provides a precise conceptual distinction between two related but different legal events: the dissolution of the contract (inhilal al-aqd) and the extinction of an obligation (inqida' al-iltizam). The author reminds us that a contract is a source of obligations, not the obligation itself; the obligation is the effect of the contract. Therefore, the extinction of the obligation (e.g., by performing the contract) is not the same as dissolving the contract. For example, when a buyer pays the price and the seller delivers the goods, the obligation to perform is extinguished, but the contract itself remains; it has been fulfilled, not dissolved. In contrast, dissolution (like faskh) terminates the contractual bond itself, freeing the parties from the relationship. Dissolution automatically extinguishes the primary obligations of the contract (e.g., to deliver and pay), but it may also create new, secondary obligations, such as restitution of what was already exchanged. The author illustrates this with a sale: if the contract is rescinded after the parties have already exchanged the price and goods, each must return what they received. Similarly, in a lease rescinded after possession and payment, the lessor must return the unused portion of the rent. Thus, the passage clarifies that dissolution is a deeper legal act than mere extinction of obligation, and it often generates its own regime of obligations.

Key Terms
انحلال العقد (inhilal al-aqd): Dissolution of the contract; termination of the contractual bond freeing the parties from the relationship.
انقضاء الالتزام (inqida al-iltizam): Extinction of an obligation; fulfillment or satisfaction of a duty arising from a contract, without necessarily dissolving the contract itself.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when the buyer and seller mutually deliver the price and goods, what happens legally?
A) The contract is dissolved
B) The obligations are extinguished but the contract is not dissolved
C) The contract becomes void
D) Both the contract and obligations are terminated

Key Takeaway
Dissolution of a contract ends the contractual bond and may create new obligations (e.g., restitution), whereas extinction of an obligation merely fulfills a duty under an existing contract.

The Distinction Between Dissolution and Extinguishment
Conceptual explanation of dissolution vs extinguishment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between dissolution of a contract (inḥilāl al-ʿaqd) and extinguishment of an obligation (inqiḍāʾ al-iltizām), and apply the rule that dissolution extinguishes prior obligations but also creates new ones.

Core Idea
Dissolution ends the contract; extinguishment ends an obligation—performance does not dissolve.

This lesson addresses a foundational conceptual distinction in Islamic contract law: the difference between dissolving the contract itself and merely fulfilling its obligations. Understanding this distinction is critical for a mufti when analyzing the lifecycle of a contract, especially when obligations change or new ones arise after rescission. The passage from Dr. al-Zarqa clarifies that extinguishment by performance is not dissolution; rather, dissolution is a separate event that both ends prior obligations and creates new reciprocal duties.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two concepts
Read the passage and locate the definitions of انحلال العقد (dissolution of the contract) and انقضاء الالتزام (extinguishment of the obligation). Understand that the contract is the source, the obligation is the effect.
The passage explicitly compares them to 'removal of the cause' vs 'termination of the effect'.
2
Understand the relationship
Note the key rule: 'كل انحلال في العقد تنقضي به التزاماته السابقة، ولا عكس.' Dissolution necessarily extinguishes prior obligations, but extinguishment (e.g., by performance) does not necessarily dissolve the contract.
This is the central legal maxim of this passage.
3
Analyze the consequences
After dissolution, new obligations arise, such as the obligation to return countervalues (taradd). Trace the examples of sale and lease rescission after mutual receipt.
The passage emphasizes that the legal status after dissolution becomes without a cause, requiring restoration of the prior state.
4
Recognize modes of extinguishment
Identify the two fundamental methods: real performance (e.g., payment, delivery) and constructive performance (e.g., set-off, merger of debts). Understand the conditions for set-off: same category and simultaneous maturity.
This part introduces practical tools for the mufti to determine when an obligation is considered fulfilled.

Chapter 45: Dissolution of the Contract
Right of rescission for one of the two contracting parties when the other party defaults in performing his obligation at its time — if some schools do not accept it, then in others there is room for it in the rules of Islamic law.
7/45 - The difference between dissolution of the contract and extinguishment of the obligation:
We saw at the beginning of the theory of contracts that the contract is one of the sources creating the obligation, and it is not the obligation itself, rather the obligation is an effect of it (see: 5/27).
From this it becomes clear that extinguishment of the obligation is not dissolution of the contract. The difference between them is like the difference between the removal of the cause and the termination of the effect. Thus, the mutual receipt of the seller and buyer in the sold item and price is an extinguishment of the obligation to execute the contract, and it is not a dissolution in the contract, and so on.
By way of clarification we say:
Dissolution of the contract releases the contracting parties from the bond that was between them.
So this dissolution entails the extinguishment of the obligations that had arisen from that dissolved contract. For example, rescission of a sale terminates the buyer's obligation to pay the price, just as it terminates the seller's obligation to deliver the sold item. And so every contract: its dissolution extinguishes the obligations arising from its conclusion.
However, this extinguishment of the prior obligations that were generated from the contract before its dissolution does not prevent the emergence of new obligations generated from the dissolution.
For example, if rescission of a sale occurs after mutual receipt of the countervalues, it necessitates the obligation of return. So the seller becomes obligated to return the price, and the buyer becomes obligated to return the sold item.
And rescission of a lease after mutual receipt of the leased property and rent obligates the lessor to return the rent advanced for the remaining period of the lease, just as it obligates the lessee to return the leased property to the lessor after recovering the remaining rent; because the continuation of the legal status established by the contract between the contracting parties has become without a legal cause after the rescission of the contract, so it became necessary to return the contracting parties to their prior state before contracting.
8/45 - Based on what has been explained above, we say: The extinguishment of the obligation occurs through one of two fundamental methods to which all its forms refer. Thus, this extinguishment occurs: either by performing the obligation, when every right-holder obtains his right; or by dropping the liability for performance due to the removal of what necessitated it, as in the dissolution of the contract with respect to contractual obligations.
So every dissolution in the contract extinguishes its prior obligations, but not vice versa, i.e., extinguishment of the obligation does not necessarily entail dissolution of the contract that created it, because the extinguishment may be by performing the obligation that the contract imposed, not by the contract itself falling away as stated.
Real and Constructive Performance:
9/45 - Moreover, the performance that extinguishes the obligation may be: either real performance, such as paying a debt, and such as mutual receipt of countervalues in commutative contracts like sale and the like; or constructive performance, i.e., deemed, by the occurrence of what legally takes the place of performance, as in set-off (muqassa) and merger of estates (ittihad al-dhimmah).
(a) Set-off (taqas or muqassa, with shadd on the sad) is that the debtor has a claim against the creditor similar to what the creditor has against him, so that claims between them cease judicially due to their futility, because if one were sued, he would have the right to sue the other in return. It is as if each of the two debts cut off (qassa) the other, i.e., eliminated it (see: al-Misbah al-Munir).
Among its conditions are that the two debts are of the same category, and due at the same time. So if one's debt is dinars and the other's debt is wheat, for example, or if...
Source pages p. 569

Commentary
This passage from Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa's General Introduction to Fiqh deals with the conceptual distinction between dissolution of a contract (انحلال العقد) and extinguishment of an obligation (انقضاء الالتزام). The author first notes that a contract is a source of obligations, not the obligation itself. Therefore, extinguishing the obligation (e.g., by fulfilling it) does not dissolve the contract; dissolution is a separate event that terminates the contractual bond. The key point is that dissolution necessarily extinguishes all obligations that arose from the contract (past obligations), but it also gives rise to new reciprocal obligations (such as returning the price and sold item after rescission with mutual receipt). Then the passage discusses the two modes of extinguishment: by actual performance (real) and by constructive performance (such as set-off or merger of estates). Set-off occurs when two parties owe each other similar debts; the debts are considered mutually extinguished. Conditions include same type of debt and simultaneous maturity. This discussion is foundational for understanding the lifecycle of a contract in Islamic law.

Key Terms
انحلال العقد (inḥilāl al-ʿaqd): زوال الرابطة التعاقدية بين المتعاقدين، مما يستلزم انقضاء الالتزامات السابقة وقد ينشئ التزامات جديدة كالتراد
انقضاء الالتزام (inqiḍāʾ al-iltizām): زوال ما في الذمة من حق بإحدى طريقتين: التنفيذ الحقيقي أو الحكمي
التقاص (al-taqāṣṣ): مقابلة دين بمثله بين شخصين يوجب سقوط المطالبة قضاءً

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Dissolution of a contract extinguishes prior obligations and creates new ones (like return), while extinguishment by performance does not dissolve the contract.

The Two Fundamental Ways Obligations Terminate
Explains two basic ways obligations end

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between termination by execution and termination by dissolution, and explain why termination does not necessarily imply contract voidance.

Core Idea
Obligations end either by performing them or by the duty falling away, but performance does not void the contract.

This passage from al-Zarqa's legal theory explains the two fundamental mechanisms by which any obligation (iltizam) comes to an end. Understanding this classification is essential for analyzing contractual obligations and their extinction in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two modes
From the text, pick out the two ways obligations terminate: execution (tanfidh) and dissolution (suqut 'uhdat al-tanfidh).
The first occurs when the right-holder obtains his due; the second when the reason for the obligation vanishes.
2
Understand dissolution
Dissolution occurs when the cause of obligation is removed, e.g., contract dissolution.
The text gives the example of dissolution of a contract with respect to contractual obligations.
3
Note the non-reversal
The author stresses that termination of an obligation does not mean the contract is dissolved; it could be due to performance.
The Arabic says: 'لا عكس أي لا يلزم من انقضاء الالتزام انحلال العقد'.
4
Apply to contracts
In contractual obligations, dissolution of the contract terminates its obligations, but performance of obligations does not dissolve the contract.
This is key for muftis: a fulfilled contract remains valid, only unfulfilled obligations vanish.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب السادس : نظرية العقود وما إليها في الفقه

٨/٤٥ بناء على ما تقدم بيانه نقول : إن انقضاء الالتزام يكون بأحد طريقين أساسيين ترجع إليهما جميع صوره، فهذا الانقضاء يحصل: - إما بتنفيذ الالتزام إذ يصل كل ذي حق إلى حقه. - وإما بسقوط عهدة التنفيذ لزوال ما أوجبها كما في انحلال العقد بالنسبة إلى الالتزامات العقدية.
فكل انحلال في العقد تنقضي به التزاماته السابقة، ولا عكس أي لا يلزم من انقضاء الالتزام انحلال العقد الذي أنشأه إذ قد يكون الانقضاء بتنفيذ الالتزام الذي أوجبه العقد، لا بسقوط العقد الموجب كما تقدم.

التنفيذ الحقيقي والحكمي:
٩/٤٥ - هذا، وإنَّ التنفيذ الذي ينقضي به الالتزام قد يكون : - إما تنفيذاً حقيقياً، كوفاء الدين، وكتقابض العوضين في المعاوضة من بيع ونحوه . - وإما تنفيذاً حكمياً، أي اعتبارياً؛ وذلك بحصول ما يقوم مقام التنفيذ شرعاً، كما في التقاص، واتحاد الذمة.

أ - فالتقاص، أو المقاصة، بتشديد الصاد فيهما)، هو أن يثبت للمدين على الدائن نظير ما للدائن عليه فتمتنع المطالبة بينهما قضاء لعدم فائدتها، إذ لو طولب أحدهما حق له أن يطالب هو الآخر بالمثل. فكأنما كل من الدينين قد قصَّ الآخر أي قطعه (ر: المصباح المنير). ومن شرائطها أن يكون الدينان من جنس واحد، ومستحقي الأداء في وقت واحد فلو كان دين أحدهما دنانير ودين الآخر قمحاً مثلاً، أو كان أحدهما حالاً ودين الآخر مؤجلاً، فلا تقاص بينهما.

ب - وأما اتحاد الذمة فهو اجتماع صفتي مدين ودائن في شخص واحد بدين واحد كما لو مات الدائن وكان المدين هو الوارث له. فإنَّ المدين يرث فيما يرثه حق الدائن في ذمته فيصبح دائناً لنفسه، فينقضي الدين باتحاد الذمة.
والفرق بين اتحاد الذمة والمقاصة أنّ الدين في حالة اتحاد الذمة هو دين واحد، وقد اجتمعت صفة المدين والدائن به في شخص واحد. أما في المقاصة فدينان متناظران لشخصين كل منهما دائن ومدين.
Chapter Six: Theory of Contracts and Related Matters in Fiqh

8/45 – Based on what has been explained, we say: The termination of an obligation occurs through one of two fundamental ways to which all its forms trace back. This termination happens: either by executing the obligation, so that every right-holder attains his right; or by the duty to perform falling away due to the removal of what necessitated it, as in the dissolution of a contract with respect to contractual obligations.
Thus every dissolution of a contract terminates its prior obligations, but not the reverse – that is, it does not necessarily follow from the termination of an obligation that the contract which created it is dissolved, since termination may be by execution of the obligation that the contract imposed, not by the abrogation of the obligating contract, as mentioned.

Real and Constructive Execution:
9/45 – Furthermore, the execution by which an obligation is terminated may be: either real execution, such as payment of a debt, or the mutual delivery of consideration in commutative contracts like sale and the like; or constructive execution, i.e., deeming something equivalent to execution legally, as in set-off (taqas) and merger of debts (ittihad al-dhimmah).

A) Set-off (taqas or muqassa, with emphasis on the sad) is that the debtor has against the creditor a counter-obligation equivalent to what the creditor has against him, so that claims between them are prevented judicially because of its uselessness – for if one of them were sued, he would have the right to sue the other for the equivalent. As if each of the two debts has cut off the other. Among its conditions are that the two debts be of the same type, and both due for payment at the same time. So if one debt is dinars and the other wheat, for example, or one is immediate and the other deferred, there is no set-off between them.

B) Merger of debts (ittihad al-dhimmah) is the combining of the qualities of debtor and creditor in one person regarding a single debt, as when the creditor dies and the debtor is his heir. For the debtor inherits, among what he inherits, the right that the creditor had against his own liability, so he becomes a creditor to himself, and the debt is extinguished through the merger of liabilities.
The difference between merger of debts and set-off is that in merger, the debt is a single debt, and the qualities of debtor and creditor have combined in one person. In set-off, there are two reciprocal debts between two persons, each of whom is both creditor and debtor.
Source pages p. 570

Commentary
This passage is a legal-philosophical exposition on the termination of obligations within contract theory. The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, explains that obligations (iltizam) end via two basic mechanisms: (1) actual execution (tanfidh haqiqi), where the debtor fulfills what is due, and (2) discharge of the duty to perform (suqut 'uhdat al-tanfidh), which occurs when the cause of the obligation is removed—most commonly through dissolution of the contract (inhilal al-'aqd). Importantly, the author clarifies that termination of an obligation does not necessarily mean the contract is voided; it may simply be that the obligation has been performed.

He then distinguishes between real execution (tanfidh haqiqi) and constructive execution (tanfidh hukmi). Constructive execution occurs when the law treats an event as equivalent to actual performance. Two key examples are given: set-off (taqas/muqassa) and merger of debts (ittihad al-dhimmah).

Set-off applies when two parties owe each other mutual debts of the same type and both are due immediately. In such a case, each debt extinguishes the other reciprocally, as if paid. The author notes the condition that the debts must be of the same genus (jins) and both due at the same time; otherwise set-off is not valid.

Merger of debts occurs when the same person becomes both creditor and debtor in respect of a single debt. The classic example is when the creditor dies and the debtor is his heir—the debtor inherits the claim against himself, so the debt is extinguished because one cannot owe oneself. The key difference from set-off is that merger involves one debt with two qualities in one person, while set-off involves two distinct debts between two persons.

This passage uses the terminology of classical Hanafi contract theory but is written in a modern analytical style typical of al-Zarqa's approach.

Key Terms
انقضاء الالتزام (inqida' al-iltizam): Termination of an obligation, either by performance or by discharge.
تنفيذ حقيقي (tanfidh haqiqi): Actual physical performance of the obligation, e.g., payment of debt.
تنفيذ حكمي (tanfidh hukmi): Legal or constructive performance where the law treats an event as equivalent to actual performance.
تقاص (taqas): Set-off between two mutual debts of the same kind and maturity.
اتحاد الذمة (ittihad al-dhimmah): Merger of the qualities of debtor and creditor in one person, extinguishing the debt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is true?
A) Termination of an obligation always dissolves the contract.
B) Dissolution of a contract terminates its obligations.
C) Execution of an obligation dissolves the contract.
D) Both B and C.

Key Takeaway
Obligations terminate either by performance or by the duty falling away; only dissolution voids the contract, not mere performance.

Legal Philosophy and Theory

Real and Constructive Execution: Set-off and Merger
Explains real vs constructive execution, with set-off and merger

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between real execution (tanfidh haqiqi) and constructive execution (tanfidh hukmi), and explain the conditions and effects of set-off (taqas) and merger of debts (ittihad al-dhimmah).

Core Idea
Constructive execution legally treats set-off and merger as if the debt was paid, without actual payment.

This passage builds on the previous classification by dividing execution into real (physical performance) and constructive (legal equivalence). Two key examples of constructive execution are set-off (taqas) and merger of debts (ittihad al-dhimmah), each with specific conditions under Hanafi law.

Guided Path
1
Define real execution
Identify real execution as actual physical performance, such as paying a debt or delivering goods in a sale.
The text gives examples: 'كوفاء الدين، وكتقابض العوضين'.
2
Define constructive execution
Constructive execution occurs when the law treats an event as equivalent to performance. The two examples are set-off and merger.
The author calls it 'تنفيذاً حكمياً، أي اعتبارياً'.
3
Analyze set-off (taqas)
Set-off applies when two parties owe each other mutual debts of the same type and both are due. The debts cancel each other out.
Conditions: same type (jins) and both due immediately. Text says: 'أن يكون الدينان من جنس واحد، ومستحقي الأداء في وقت واحد'.
4
Analyze merger (ittihad al-dhimmah)
Merger occurs when the same person becomes both debtor and creditor regarding a single debt, typically through inheritance.
Example: creditor dies and debtor inherits. The debt extinguishes because one cannot owe oneself.
5
Distinguish between the two
Merger involves one debt with both qualities in one person; set-off involves two debts between two persons.
The text explicitly states this difference: 'الدين في حالة اتحاد الذمة هو دين واحد... أما في المقاصة فدينان'.

Introduction to Named Contracts: The Contract of Sale in Hanafi Fiqh
Listing and defining major named contracts, focusing on sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the 25 named contracts in Hanafi fiqh, differentiate those covered by the Majalla, and define the contract of sale (bay‘) with its key technical terms (mabī‘, thaman).

Core Idea
The contract of sale (bay‘) is the paradigm of all financial commutative contracts in Hanafi fiqh, defined as a permanent exchange of property for property.

This passage from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-‘Amm by Mustafa al-Zarqa introduces the system of named contracts that form the backbone of Hanafi commercial law. You will learn that the contract of sale (bay‘) is not merely a transaction of goods for money but a generic exchange of property for property, serving as the basis for analogy in many other contracts. Understanding its definition and sub-types is essential for grasping the Hanafi approach to financial transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the list of 25 contracts
Scan the enumerated list and note which contracts are included in the Ottoman Majalla and which are omitted because they pertain to personal status.
The Majalla covers the first seventeen plus rescission (iqalah).
2
Distinguish the scope of the Majalla
Understand why loan (qard) and mukharaja are omitted despite being transactional, and why marriage and wills are omitted as personal status matters.
This reveals the Majalla's focus on pure financial transactions.
3
Extract the definition of bay‘
Locate the precise definition: 'a contract based on exchange of property for property, resulting in the exchange of ownerships on a permanent basis.'
Note the key elements: exchange, property, permanence.
4
Parse the terminology
Identify the terms mabī‘ (the sold commodity) and thaman (the price, usually money) and understand how they are allocated to the seller and buyer respectively.
Custom assigns 'seller' to the one offering the goods, though linguistically both parties can be called seller/buyer.
5
Recognize sub-types of sale
List the branches under bay‘: salam, barter, currency exchange, istisna‘, trust sales (murabahah, tawliyah, wadi‘ah), and sale with right of redemption.
These are all treated as types of bay‘ in Hanafi fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب السادس: نظرية العقود وما إليها في الفقه
وهذه العقود المسماة مما بحثت فيه المجلة أو لم تبحث هي: (۱) البيع (۲) الإجارة (٣) الكفالة (٤) الحوالة (٥) الرهن (٦) بيع الوفاء (۷) الإيداع (۸) الإعارة (۹) الهبة (۱۰) القسمة (۱۱) الشركة (۱۲) المضاربة (۱۳) المزارعة (١٤) المساقاة (١٥) الوكالة (١٦) الصلح (١٧) التحكيم (۱۸) المخارجة (۱۹) القرض (۲۰) العُمْرى (۱) (۲۱) الموالاة (۲۲) الإقالة (۲۳) الزواج (٢٤) الوصية (٢٥) الإيصاء.
والذي ورد منها في المجلة هو العقود السبعة عشر الأولى وعقد الإقالة فقط. وأهملت المجلة البحث عن المخارجة والقرض إهمالاً، مع أنهما من عقود المعاملات التي تشملها غاية المجلة؛ أما العقود الخمسة الباقية فلم تبحث عنها لأنها من الأحوال الشخصية.
وليلاحظ أن بعض هذه العقود يشتمل على عقود كثيرة تعد من تفاريعه وتذكر في بحثه ؛ كالبيع والشركة مثلاً، فإنَّ تحتهما أنواعاً عديدة من العقود المختلفة الأسماء تعد فروعاً منهما .
وسنعرض فيما يلي هذه العقود المسماة المعروفة في الفقه الإسلامي عرضاً مقتصراً على التعريف بكل منها، وبيان الأسماء الاصطلاحية فيه، وذكر أهم خصائصه الأساسية التي تعطي الطالب فكرة إجمالية عنه، متبعين في عرضها هذا الترتيب الذي جَرَت عليه المجلة فيما ذكرته منها - إلا في عقدين اثنين بيع الوفاء والإقالة، كما سننبه عليه، ومشيرين في جانب كل عقد إلى مواد المجلة التي يقع بحثه فيها.
٣/٤٦ - (١) البيع: (المجلة / ١٠١ - ٤٠٣ ) .
هو عقد يقوم على أساس مبادلة المال بالمال، فيفيد تبادل الملكيات على وجه الدوام أي بلا توقيت في انتقال الملكيات بهذا التبادل.
(1) (العُمرى) بضم فسكون وبالألف المقصورة في آخرها وزان «كبرى وصغرى وسيأتي تفسيرها في محله (ف ٢٢/٤٦).
٦٠٦
الفصل (٤٦) عرض لأهم العقود المسماة
وهو رأس عقود المعاوضات المالية وأحكامه أساس للقياس في كثير من أحكامها .
وقد استهلت به المجلة العقود المسماة وبحثت عنه في ثلاث وثلثمائة مادة بعد القواعد الكلية مباشرة من المادة ۱۰۱ إلى ٤٠٣ . وهذا العقد يسمى في الأصل بيعاً و «شراء» بالنسبة إلى كل واحد من طرفيه (1)
لكن عرف الناس والفقهاء على تخصيص لفظي «البيع، والبائع بجانب باذل السلعة التي تتعلق بها حاجة الانتفاع والاستعمال، وتخصيص لفظي «الشراء، والشاري بجانب باذل العوض المالي الآخر الذي يسمى أيضاً: «مشترياً ومبتاعاً».
والسلعة أو الشيء الذي هو محل البيع يسمى: «مبيعاً». والعوض المالي الآخر المتفق عليه ويبذله المشتري في مقابل امتلاك المبيع يسمى: ثَمَناً» (بفتحتين). وهذا يكون في الغالب من النقود.
وتحت عقد البيع أنواع فرعية كثيرة كالسَّلَم أو السَّلَف (بفتحتين فيهما، وكالمقايضة، والصَّرْف والاستصناع، وبيوع الأمانة (المرابحة، والتولية، والوضيعة)(۲)، وبيع الوفاء.
فكل هذه العقود يعدونها فروعاً خاصة لعقد البيع، ويذكر كل منها غالباً في فصل خاص ضمن كتاب البيوع تعالج فيه أحكامه .
(۱) بما أن كلا الطرفين في وضعين متماثلين من حيث المبادلة والمعاوضة، لذلك كان لفظ البيع معدوداً من أسماء الأضداد؛ وكذا لفظ الشراء، فيسمى كل من طرفي عقده في أصل اللغة بائعاً وشارياً، فيقال باع البضاعة أو شراها إذا عقد على تمليكها أو تملكها بعوض (ر: المصباح المنير). وفي القرآن العظيم حكاية عن يوسف عليه السلام: ﴿ وَشَرَوْهُ بِثَمَن بَخسٍ دَرَاهِمَ مَعْدُودَةٍ وَكَانُوا فِيهِ مِنَ الزَّاهِدِينَ ﴾ أي باعوه.
(۲) انظر ما تقدم عن بيوع الأمانة في بحث عيوب الرضا (ف ٢/٣٥ الحاشية).
٦٠٧
Chapter Six: The Theory of Contracts and Related Matters in Fiqh. These named contracts, whether discussed in the Majalla or not, are: (1) Sale (2) Lease (3) Suretyship (4) Transfer of Debt (5) Pledge (6) Sale with Right of Redemption (7) Deposit (8) Loan of an Object (9) Gift (10) Partition (11) Partnership (12) Mudarabah (13) Sharecropping (14) Irrigation Contract (15) Agency (16) Compromise (17) Arbitration (18) Mukharaja (19) Loan (20) 'Umra (21) Clientage (22) Rescission (23) Marriage (24) Will (25) Bequest. What appears in the Majalla is only the first seventeen contracts and the contract of rescission. The Majalla completely neglected researching Mukharaja and Loan, although they are among contracts of transactions that the Majalla's purpose encompasses; as for the remaining five contracts, it did not discuss them because they are matters of personal status. Note that some of these contracts include many contracts that are considered its branches and are mentioned in its discussion; for example, sale and partnership, for under them are many types of contracts with different names that are considered branches of them. We will present below these well-known named contracts in Islamic fiqh in a summary presentation limited to defining each, explaining its technical terminology, and stating its most important basic characteristics that give the student a general idea about it, following in this presentation the order that the Majalla followed in what it mentioned – except for two contracts, sale with right of redemption and rescission, as we will alert, and indicating alongside each contract the Majalla articles where its discussion falls. 3/46 – (1) Sale: (Majalla / 101-403). It is a contract based on the exchange of property for property, resulting in the exchange of ownerships on a permanent basis, i.e., without a time limit in the transfer of ownerships through this exchange. (1) ('Umra: with a dammah then sukun and with a short alif at the end, on the measure of 'kubra' and 'sughra', and its explanation will come in its place (F 22/46). 606 Chapter (46) Presentation of the Most Important Named Contracts. It is the head of financial commutative contracts, and its rulings are the basis for analogy in many of their rulings. The Majalla began with it the named contracts and discussed it in three hundred and three articles directly after the general maxims from Article 101 to 403. This contract is originally called 'sale' and 'purchase' with respect to each of its two parties (1). However, the custom of people and jurists is to allocate the terms 'sale' and 'seller' to the side of the one offering the commodity that is needed for benefit and use, and to allocate the terms 'purchase' and 'buyer' to the side of the one offering the other financial consideration, who is also called 'purchaser' and 'buyer'. The commodity or thing that is the subject matter of sale is called 'mabi''. The other agreed-upon financial consideration that the buyer pays in exchange for ownership of the mabi' is called 'thaman' (with two fat'ahs). This is usually money. Under the contract of sale are many subsidiary types such as salam or salaf (both with two fat'ahs), barter, currency exchange, istisna', and trust sales (murabahah, tawliyah, wadi'ah) (2), and sale with right of redemption. All these contracts are considered special branches of the contract of sale, and each is usually mentioned in a separate section within the book of sales where its rulings are discussed. (1) Since both parties are in similar positions in terms of exchange and compensation, the word 'sale' is counted among words with opposite meanings; similarly, the word 'purchase', so each party to the contract is called in the original language a seller and a buyer, so one says 'he sold the goods' or 'he purchased them' when he contracts to transfer ownership of them or to acquire them in exchange for consideration (see: al-Misbah al-Munir). And in the Noble Quran in the story of Yusuf, peace be upon him: {And they sold him for a low price, a few dirhams, and they were of him indifferent} meaning they sold him. (2) See what preceded about trust sales in the discussion of defects of consent (F 2/35 footnote). 607
Source pages p. 573

Commentary
This passage is an introductory overview of the major named contracts in Hanafi fiqh, as covered in the classical work 'al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm' by Mustafa al-Zarqa. The author first enumerates twenty-five named contracts, distinguishing those included in the Ottoman Majalla (the codification of Hanafi law) from those omitted. The Majalla covers the first seventeen plus rescission; loan and mukharaja are omitted despite being transactional, while the last five are personal status matters. The author then announces his methodology: he will define each contract, explain its technical terms, and note its key characteristics, following the Majalla's order except for two contracts. He begins with the contract of sale (bay'), which is the foremost financial commutative contract and the basis for analogy in many others. The definition: sale is an exchange of property for property, permanently transferring ownership. The text explains the terminology: the transaction is called 'sale' from the perspective of the party providing the commodity and 'purchase' from the perspective of the party providing the price, though custom assigns 'seller' to the one offering the goods and 'buyer' to the one offering money. The commodity is the 'mabi'' and the price is 'thaman', usually currency. Under sale fall many sub-types: salam (forward sale), barter (muqayadah), currency exchange (sarf), istisna' (manufacturing contract), and trust-based sales (murabahah, tawliyah, wadi'ah), as well as sale with right of redemption (bay' al-wafa'). These are all treated as branches of the sale contract in Hanafi fiqh. The passage also includes a linguistic note that 'sale' and 'purchase' are words with opposite meanings (addad), as each party can be described by either term, and gives the Quranic example of Yusuf being 'sold' (shara) for a paltry price.

Key Terms
بيع (bay‘): A contract based on the exchange of property for property, resulting in permanent transfer of ownership.
مبيع (mabī‘): The commodity or thing that is the subject matter of sale.
ثمن (thaman): The agreed-upon financial consideration paid by the buyer in exchange for the mabī‘, usually money.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how does the Hanafi fiqh define the contract of sale (bay‘)?
A) A contract for the transfer of ownership without consideration
B) A contract based on exchange of property for property, permanently transferring ownership
C) A contract limited to monetary transactions only
D) A contract that must be executed immediately upon agreement

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi contract of sale (bay‘) is defined as a permanent exchange of property for property, covering both monetary sales and barter, and serves as the foundation for many other financial contracts.

Definition of Suretyship (Kafalah) as a Contract of Secondary Liability
Defines suretyship (kafalah) and its parties.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of kafalah, identify its four parties, and distinguish it from a transfer of debt (hawalah).

Core Idea
Kafalah joins liability, it does not transfer it — the original debtor remains bound.

This passage from the Majalla defines the contract of suretyship (kafalah) in classical Hanafi terms. The definition centers on the concept of 'joining a liability to a liability' – the surety’s obligation is added to, not substituted for, the original debtor’s. Understanding this definition and the precise terminology for each party is essential for correctly classifying guarantees in fatwa practice. Let’s parse the definition step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core definition
Find the key phrase 'ضم ذمة إلى ذمة في المطالبة' – this is the essence of kafalah.
The phrase emphasizes additive liability.
2
Break down the contract elements
The definition implies a contract where a person assumes an existing obligation of another, including himself in responsibility.
The obligation must already be due.
3
Map the four parties
Identify the surety (kafil), principal debtor (makful 'anhu), creditor (makful lahu), and subject matter (makful bihi) as stated in the passage.
Each term has a specific role.
4
Distinguish from hawalah
Note that kafalah does not release the original debtor; it adds a secondary liability. This distinguishes it from hawalah (transfer of debt).
This distinction is crucial for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٥/٤٦ - (٣) الكفالة : (المجلة / ٦١٢ - ٦٧٢). ويعرفونها بأنها : «ضم ذمة إلى ذمة في المطالبة». أي: إنها عقد يتضمن التزام شخص بحق واجب على غيره، وإشراك نفسه معه في المسؤولية به تجاه الطالب. فالمُلتزم الجديد كفيل والمُلتَزم الأصلي : مكفول، أو مكفول عنه. والطالب الملتزم له مكفول له والمُلتزم به أو محل الطلب: مكفول به.
5/46 - (3) Suretyship: (Majalla 612-672). They define it as: 'Joining a liability to a liability in a claim.' That is, it is a contract that entails a person assuming a right that is due on another, and including himself with him in responsibility for it towards the claimant. So the new obligor is the surety (kafil), and the original obligor is the guaranteed (makful) or the guaranteed about (makful 'anhu). The claimant to whom it is obligated is the guaranteed for (makful lahu), and the obliged thing or subject of the claim is the guaranteed by (makful bihi).
Source pages p. 575

Commentary
This passage defines the contract of suretyship (kafalah) as per the Majalla and Hanafi jurists. The core definition is 'joining a liability to a liability in a claim', meaning a contract where a person (surety) assumes responsibility for an existing obligation of another, thereby adding his own liability to the original. The passage then breaks down the parties: the surety (kafil), the principal debtor (makful or makful 'anhu), the creditor (makful lahu), and the debt or subject matter (makful bihi). This definition emphasizes the accessory nature of suretyship — it does not replace the original liability but supplements it. In Hanafi fiqh, kafalah is a binding contract of guarantee, valid only if the underlying debt is known and existing. The Majalla covers it in articles 612-672.

Key Terms
الكفالة (kafalah): A contract joining a liability to another liability in a claim, i.e., guarantee.
كفيل (kafil): The surety who assumes the liability.
مكفول/مكفول عنه (makful / makful 'anhu): The original debtor on whose behalf the guarantee is made.
مكفول له (makful lahu): The creditor or claimant to whom the obligation is owed.
مكفول به (makful bihi): The subject matter of the claim, i.e., the debt or obligation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How is kafalah defined in the passage?
A) Transfer of debt from one person to another
B) Joining a liability to a liability in a claim
C) A sale of usufruct
D) A contract of exchange

Key Takeaway
Kafalah is an accessory guarantee that joins liability, not a transfer of debt.

Bai al-Wafa: Independent Contract or Subtype of Sale?
Author critiques the Majallah's classification of Bai al-Wafa.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the classification of Bai al-Wafa as an independent contract in Hanafi law and apply the principle of analyzing contracts based on their essential elements.

Core Idea
Bai al-Wafa is not a pure sale but a hybrid—its legal classification follows its nature, not its name.

The Ottoman Majallah listed Bai al-Wafa as a subsidiary type of sale, alongside Salam and Istisna'. However, many later Hanafi jurists, including the author, argue that this classification is misleading. Because Bai al-Wafa combines elements of sale and pledge, it deserves its own independent legal category. Understanding why this matters deepens your ability to classify novel contracts as a mufti.

Guided Path
1
Identify the classification issue
Note why the Majallah grouped Bai al-Wafa with sales, and why the author objects. The object is not just definitional—it has practical consequences for rulings.
The author says 'we see' (nara) indicating his preferred opinion.
2
Analyze the contract's composite nature
Break down Bai al-Wafa: it has a sale price, a condition of redemption, and the item serves as security. Which known contract does each element resemble?
Sale: transfer of ownership. Pledge: possession as security. Bai al-Wafa does both.
3
Assess which contract it is closer to
The author claims it is closer to pledge (rahn) because the dominant purpose is security, not ownership. Consider the maqasid (objectives) of the transaction.
This reflects the Hanafi principle of considering the reality (haqiqah) of the contract.
4
Apply the independent contract framework
If a contract is a genuine hybrid and does not fit neatly into existing categories, Hanafi jurists may treat it as a 'mustahdath' (newly emerged) contract. Identify what makes Bai al-Wafa a distinct legal entity.
This prepares you for dealing with modern financial contracts that combine elements.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب السادس : نظرية العقود وما إليها في الفقه وقد ذكرت المجلة ثلاثة من هذه الأنواع الفرعية في آخر كتاب البيوع، وهي: بيع السَّلَم والاستصناع، وبيع الوفاء. غير أننا نرى أن بيع الوفاء يجب أن يعد عقداً مستقلاً ولا ينبغي أن يعتبر من فروع البيع، لأنه عقد مُسْتَحدَث مزيج من بيع ورهن فسنفرده بالذكر بعد الرهن لأنه أقرب إليه في خصائصه كما سنرى.
Chapter Six: Theory of Contracts and Related Matters in Fiqh. The Majallah mentioned three of these subsidiary types at the end of the Book of Sales: Bai al-Salam, Istisna', and Bai al-Wafa. However, we are of the view that Bai al-Wafa should be considered an independent contract and should not be regarded as a branch of sale, because it is a newly emerged contract that is a mixture of sale and pledge. We will treat it separately after the discussion of pledge (rahn) because it is closer to it in its characteristics, as we will see.
Source pages p. 575

Commentary
This passage presents the author's critical commentary on the classification of contracts in the Majallah. The Majallah had listed Bai al-Wafa as a subsidiary type of sale, alongside Salam and Istisna'. The author argues that this is a misclassification. His reasoning: Bai al-Wafa is not a pure sale; it is a hybrid contract combining elements of sale (bay') and pledge (rahn). Therefore, it deserves its own independent treatment. He indicates he will discuss it after the chapter on rahn, because its characteristics are closer to rahn than to sale. This reflects the Hanafi tendency to analyze the reality of a transaction rather than just its form.

Key Terms
بيع الوفاء (Bai al-Wafa): A contract where a seller sells an item with the condition that he can redeem it upon repayment of the price; a hybrid of sale and pledge.
عقد مستحدث (aql mustahdath): A contract that emerged later and was not explicitly named in the primary texts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Because it is a newly emerged contract combining sale and pledge.
C) Because it is invalid according to the Hanafi school.
D) Because it is only used in commercial practice.

Key Takeaway
Bai al-Wafa is a hybrid contract that must be classified by its substance, not its form; treat it as an independent contract closer to rahn.

Ijarah: Definition and Key Terms
Defines ijarah and its key terms according to the Majallah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define ijarah in Hanafi terms, identify its key parties and components, and distinguish it from i'arah.

Core Idea
Ijarah is a sale of usufructs: the lessor transfers ownership of the usufruct for a known period in exchange for rent.

This lesson introduces the Hanafi definition of ijarah (lease/hire) as presented in the Majallah. The passage not only defines the contract but also establishes the precise terminology for the parties, the object, and the consideration. Understanding this foundational contract is crucial for any mufti dealing with property leases, employment contracts, or service agreements, as ijarah is a daily occurrence in Muslim societies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core definition of ijarah
Read the Arabic definition: 'عقد موضوعه المبادلة على منفعة مدة محدودة' (a contract whose subject matter is exchange for a usufruct for a limited period). Note that ijarah is an exchange (mubadala), not a gratuitous transfer.
The phrase 'بيع المنافع' (sale of usufructs) is key: Hanafis treat ijarah as a sale.
2
List the four key terms and their roles
Identify the four parties/objects: mu'jir (lessor), musta'jir (lessee), ma'jur (leased asset), ajr/ujrah (rent). Understand each: mu'jir sells the usufruct; musta'jir buys it; ma'jur is the asset whose usufruct is sold; ajr is the price.
The pronunciation 'ajr' (fatha on hamza) and 'ujrah' (damma on hamza) are both correct.
3
Recognize the legal nature: sale of usufructs
Note the parenthetical: 'تمليكها بعوض في بيع المنافع' (transfer of ownership of usufruct for compensation in the sale of usufructs). This implies that ijarah shares many rules with sale (bay').
This is a Hanafi specific: other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) treat ijarah as an independent contract.
4
Note the promise of comparison with i'arah
The passage ends with 'انظر المقارنة بين الإجارة والإعارة' (see the comparison between ijarah and i'arah). Prepare to differentiate ijarah (onerous) from i'arah (gratuitous).
This comparison is a common pedagogical tool in Hanafi texts.
5
Apply the definition to a simple scenario
Take a real case: renting an apartment for one year for $12,000. Identify each term: lessor (owner), lessee (tenant), asset (apartment), rent ($12,000). Check that the usufruct is known (use of apartment) and period is fixed.
This step bridges definition to fatwa application.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٤/٤٦ - (٢) الإجارة: (المجلة ٤٠٤ - ٦١١). وهي عقد موضوعه المبادلة على منفعة مدة محدودة أي تمليكها بعوض في بيع المنافع. فالمالك الذي يبيع منافع ملكه يسمى مؤجراً. والطرف الآخر يسمى مستأجراً. والشيء المعقود على منفعته يسمى مأجوراً. والبدل الملتزم أداؤه لقاء المنفعة يسمى أجراً وأجرة بفتح الهمزة في الأول وضمها في الثاني). وانظر المقارنة بين الإجارة والإعارة فيما سيأتي (ف ٢٠/٤٦).
4/46 - (2) Ijarah: (Majallah articles 404-611). It is a contract whose subject matter is exchange for a usufruct for a limited period, i.e., the transfer of ownership of usufruct for compensation in the sale of usufructs. The owner who sells the usufruct of his property is called the mu'jir (lessor). The other party is called the musta'jir (lessee). The thing whose usufruct is contracted is called ma'jur (leased asset). The consideration obligated to be paid for the usufruct is called ajr or ujrah (rent), with fatha on the hamza in the first and damma in the second. And see the comparison between ijarah and i'arah (lending) in what follows (paragraph 20/46).
Source pages p. 575

Commentary
This passage provides the Hanafi definition of ijarah as found in the Majallah. Ijarah is fundamentally a sale (bay') but of usufructs (manafi'), not of the corpus. The usufruct is transferred (tamlik) for a period, in exchange for a consideration called rent. The author clarifies the key terms: mu'jir (lessor), musta'jir (lessee), ma'jur (the object), and ajr/ujrah (rent). Note the pronunciation nuances: 'ajr' with fatha on hamza and 'ujrah' with damma. The passage ends by promising a comparison between ijarah and i'arah (gratuitous lending) later, which is a common Hanafi pedagogical tool to distinguish between onerous and gratuitous contracts.

Key Terms
الإجارة (al-ijarah): A contract for the exchange of a usufruct for a consideration over a limited period.
مؤجر (mu'jir): The party who sells the usufruct, i.e., the lessor.
أجر / أجرة (ajr / ujrah): The rent or consideration paid for the usufruct.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi terminology, what is the owner who sells the usufruct of his property called?
A) مستأجر (musta'jir)
B) مأجور (ma'jur)
C) مؤجر (mu'jir)
D) أجر (ajr)

Key Takeaway
Ijarah is a sale of usufructs: it requires a known usufruct, known period, known consideration, and ability to deliver.
