Kafalah: Definition and Types
Defines kafalah and its types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of kafalah, identify its four key parties, and classify the main types (financial and personal) along with the three subtypes of financial suretyship.

Core Idea
Kafalah is cumulative liability: the surety joins, not replaces.

This passage lays out the foundational definition of kafalah (suretyship) according to the Hanafi school as codified in the Majallah. Understanding this definition is crucial because it determines the legal effects — especially the creditor's right to sue either party. The passage also provides a detailed taxonomy of kafalah types, each with its own subject and rules.

Guided Path
1
Unpack the definition
Analyze the phrase 'ضم ذمة إلى ذمة في المطالبة' (joining a liability to a liability in a claim). What does 'joining' imply about the relationship between the surety (kafil) and the principal (makful 'anhu)?
The key is that the surety adds his liability; the principal's liability remains.
2
Identify the four parties
List the four parties mentioned: kafil (surety), makful 'anhu (principal debtor), makful lahu (creditor/claimant), and makful bihi (the subject of the claim). Understand each role.
These terms are standard in Hanafi suretyship contracts.
3
Distinguish main categories
Differentiate between kafalah bil mal (financial suretyship) and kafalah bil nafs (personal suretyship). What does each guarantee?
Financial focuses on property; personal focuses on producing the person.
4
Break down financial subtypes
Examine the three subtypes of kafalah bil mal: kafalah bil dayn (debt), kafalah bil 'ayn/taslim (specific asset/delivery), and kafalah bil darak (title guarantee). Note the unique scenario for each.
Kafalah bil darak is specific to sale contracts and guarantees against eviction.
5
Understand the legal effect of cumulative liability
From the statement that the claimant may demand from the principal, the surety, or both, derive the rule that kafalah does not transfer liability but adds another party.
This distinguishes kafalah from hawalah (transfer of debt) in Hanafi law.

Source Text

٥/٤٦ - (٣) الكفالة : (المجلة / ٦١٢ - ٦٧٢). ويعرفونها بأنها : «ضم ذمة إلى ذمة في المطالبة». أي: إنها عقد يتضمن التزام شخص بحق واجب على غيره، وإشراك نفسه معه في المسؤولية به تجاه الطالب. فالملتزم الجديد كفيل والملتزم الأصلي : مكفول، أو مكفول عنه. والطالب الملتزم له مكفول له والملتزم به أو محل الطلب: مكفول به. وهذه الكفالة أنواع متميزة في بعض الأحكام بحسب اختلاف موضوعها : ( أ ) فقد يكون موضوعها التزاماً مالياً ؛ وهذه تسمى: كفالة بالمال. 5/46 - (3) Kafalah: (Majallah articles 612-672). They define it as: 'Joining a liability to a liability in a claim.' That is, it is a contract that involves a person undertaking a right that is due from another, and making himself a partner with him in the responsibility towards the claimant. The new undertaker is called kafil (surety), and the original undertaker is called makful or makful 'anhu. The claimant is called makful lahu, and the thing undertaken or subject of the claim is called makful bihi. This kafalah has distinct types with different rulings depending on its subject: (a) Its subject may be a financial obligation; this is called kafalah bil mal (suretyship for property). (b) Its subject may be the surety's obligation to produce the original responsible person; this is called kafalah bil nafs (suretyship for the person). Then kafalah bil mal is of three types: (1) It may be an undertaking to pay a debt owed by another; called kafalah bil dayn. (2) It may be to deliver a specific asset in the possession of another, such as returning the usurped property to the usurper or delivering the sold item to the buyer; called kafalah bil 'ayn or kafalah bil taslim. (3) Its subject may be a guarantee that the sold property is free from any right of a third party, i.e., a guarantee of the buyer's rights against the seller if a third party claims ownership, such as if it becomes clear that the sold item belongs to someone else or is mortgaged. This last type is called kafalah bil darak, meaning the risk that befalls the sold property due to a previous cause. Since kafalah is based on the participation and joint liability of the surety with the principal, not on transferring liability from the principal to the surety, the claimant may demand his right from whichever he wishes: the principal, the surety, or both together. However, kafalah bil nafs alone has no financial consequence; its result is forcing the surety by court order to produce the original responsible person. But making the financial liability conditional on non-performance of the personal surety makes it...
Source pages p. 575

Commentary
This passage provides a comprehensive definition of kafalah (suretyship) according to Hanafi jurisprudence as codified in the Majallah. The definition 'joining a liability to a liability in a claim' captures the essence: the surety adds his liability to that of the principal without removing the principal's liability. The text then enumerates the key parties: kafil (surety), makful 'anhu (principal), makful lahu (creditor/claimant), and makful bihi (the subject). It then categorizes kafalah into two main branches: kafalah bil mal (financial suretyship) and kafalah bil nafs (personal suretyship, i.e., guaranteeing the appearance of the principal). Financial suretyship is further divided into three subtypes: (1) kafalah bil dayn (guarantee of a debt), (2) kafalah bil 'ayn or bil taslim (guarantee of delivering a specific asset), and (3) kafalah bil darak (guarantee against defects in title, like eviction). The passage also highlights a key legal effect: because kafalah is cumulative liability (i.e., the surety joins rather than replaces the principal), the creditor can choose to sue the principal, the surety, or both. This is a distinctive Hanafi principle. The last sentence is incomplete but suggests that if the personal surety is not fulfilled, it may lead to financial liability, indicating a possible hybrid situation.

Key Terms
الكفالة (al-kafalah): A contract where a person joins his liability to that of another in a claim.
كفالة بالدرك (kafalah bil darak): Suretyship against defects in title, guaranteeing that the sold property is free from third-party claims.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi definition of kafalah, which of the following is true?
A) The surety replaces the principal, so the claimant can only sue the surety.
B) The surety joins his liability to the principal, and the claimant may sue either or both.
C) The surety guarantees only the personal appearance of the principal, not financial obligations.
D) Kafalah bil darak is a guarantee of delivery of a specific asset.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, kafalah is a cumulative guarantee where the surety joins his liability to the principal's, enabling the creditor to claim from either or both.

Bay' al-Wafa' as an Independent Contract
Juridical nature and classification of Bay' al-Wafa'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify Bay' al-Wafa' as an independent Hanafi contract and evaluate the misapplication in positive law that conflates it with mortgage.

Core Idea
Bay' al-Wafa' is sui generis: buyer gets usufruct by default, unlike mortgage.

This passage from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi establishes that Bay' al-Wafa' is not a subtype of sale but an independent contract. Understanding it requires mastering both sale and mortgage rules. The author critiques legal codifications that mistakenly reduced it to a mere repetition of mortgage, ignoring its distinctive feature: the buyer's right to usufruct. This lesson unpacks the juridical nature of Bay' al-Wafa' and the fatwa implications of its classification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core claim
What is the author's main thesis about Bay' al-Wafa'? State the claim that it is an independent contract.
The author says it is 'عقداً مستقلاً' – not a branch of sale.
2
Distinguish from sale and mortgage
How does Bay' al-Wafa' differ from a regular sale? How does it differ from rahn (mortgage)? Focus on the usufruct entitlement.
In a sale, buyer owns everything; in rahn, creditor gets no usufruct. Bay' al-Wafa' buyer gets usufruct by default.
3
Analyze the proof from the Majalla
The author cites Majalla articles 398 and 118. Explain how these articles support the independent nature of Bay' al-Wafa'.
Article 118 states that buyer owns usufruct by default – distinguishing it from rahn.
4
Critique the legal misapplication
What error did the drafters of the Ottoman Law No. 3339 commit? How did they conflate Bay' al-Wafa' with rahn?
They made usufruct default for seller, contradicting the contract's essence.
5
Identify authoritative references
List the classical Hanafi sources where the correct ruling is verified.
Al-Durar, Radd al-Muhtar, al-Fatawa al-Bazzaziyya, Jami' al-Fusulayn.

Source Text

(١) كانت النصوص القانونية المعول عليها لدينا في أحكام بيع الوفاء هي المواد / ۹۱ - ١٠٠ من قانون الملكية العقارية ذي الرقم / ۳۳۳۹/ . وقد نصت المادة / ٩٥/ منه على أن منافع العقار المبيع بالوفاء تكون بمقتضى العقد للبائع لا للمشتري، ويكون المشتري مسؤولاً وضامناً لكل ما يستفيده من ثمرات ومنافع المبيع، فتطرح قيمتها من أصل الدين إلا إذا اشترط الطرفان خلاف ذلك.
وبذلك لم يبق فرق يذكر بين رهن العقار وبيعه وفاء في أحكام هذا القانون فيصبح الوفاء فيه عبثاً وتكراراً محضاً لأحكام الرهن فيه باسم جديد. بيع
ذكر
وهذا من جهل واضعي القانون بمبنى الفرق بين الرهن وبيع الوفاء، بحسب منشأ هذا العقد. فإن الوفاء إنما استحدث إلى جانب الرهن على أساس أن يستحق المشتري بيع فيه منافع المبيع بمقتضى العقد على خلاف حكم الرهن، كما أوضحناه. ولعل خطأ واضعي هذا القانون من هذه الناحية ناشيء عما يوهمه ظاهر المادة/ ٣٩٨/ من المجلة، إذ تقول إنه : إذا اشترط في بيع الوفاء أن يكون قدر من منافع المبيع للمشتري صح ذلك». أن المشتري لا يستحق منافع المبيع وفاء إلا بالشرط.
بيع
فإن ظاهرها يوهم لكنها يجب أن تفهم بأنه إذا اشترط للمشتري بالوفاء جزء فقط من منافع المبيع ينحصر حقه في المشروط، أما إذا لم يشرط في العقد شيء فالمنافع كلها للمشتري بمقتضى العقد. وذلك بدلالة المادة // ۱۱۸ / من المجلة نفسها. فقد صرحت هذه المادة أن الوفاء إنما يشبه البيع الصحيح ويفترق عن الرهن من جهة أن المشتري وفاء يملك منافع المبيع، وهو الحكم الذي قرره الفقهاء في بيع الوفاء. وهذا ما حققه والدي الشيخ أحمد الزرقاء رحمه الله تعالى في شرحه على القواعد الكلية من المجلة. وتفصيل النظر الفقهي في بيع الوفاء وأحكامه، علاوة على ما ورد منه في المجلة، يرى في الدرر شرح الغرر»، وفي رد المحتار)، وفي الفتاوى البزازية أواخر كتاب البيوع، وفي الفصل // ۱۸ / من جامع الفصولين وهو أوفاها بحثاً. وأخيراً جاء القانون المدني سنة ١٩٤٩م، فمنع بيع الوفاء، واعتبره باطلا، استغناء عنه بالرهن (ر: ق م ٤٣٣).
Chapter 46: Presentation of the Most Important Nominate Contracts. This contract is only applicable to immovable property, and the jurists' ijtihad differed regarding its application to movable property. Hence it becomes clear that Bay' al-Wafa' must be considered an independent contract, not a branch of sale, and should be mentioned in the order of nominate contracts after mortgage, because its understanding depends on understanding both the rules of sale and mortgage together. (1) (1) The legal texts relied upon in our law regarding the rules of Bay' al-Wafa' are Articles 91-100 of the Law of Land Ownership No. 3339. Article 95 thereof states that the usufruct of the immovable sold by wafa' belongs by virtue of the contract to the seller, not the buyer, and the buyer is liable and guarantor for all fruit and benefits he derives from the sold property, and their value is deducted from the principal debt unless the parties stipulate otherwise. Thus no significant difference remained between mortgage of immovable and its sale by wafa' in the rules of this law, so wafa' became in it an absurd and pure repetition of the rules of mortgage under a new name. This stems from the ignorance of the law's drafters regarding the basis of the difference between mortgage and Bay' al-Wafa', according to the origin of this contract. Indeed, wafa' was innovated alongside mortgage on the basis that the buyer in it deserves the usufruct of the sold property by virtue of the contract, contrary to the ruling of mortgage, as we explained. Perhaps the error of the drafters of this law in this regard arises from what the apparent meaning of Article 398 of the Majalla suggests, for it says: "If it is stipulated in Bay' al-Wafa' that a portion of the usufruct of the sold property be for the buyer, that is valid." That the buyer does not deserve the usufruct of the wafa' property except by stipulation. Its apparent meaning is misleading, but it must be understood that if only a part of the usufruct is stipulated for the buyer, his right is limited to what is stipulated, whereas if nothing is stipulated in the contract, all the usufruct belongs to the buyer by virtue of the contract. This is indicated by Article 118 of the same Majalla. Indeed, this article stated that wafa' resembles a valid sale and differs from mortgage in that the wafa' buyer owns the usufruct of the sold property, which is the ruling established by the jurists regarding Bay' al-Wafa'. This is what my father Sheikh Ahmad al-Zarqa' (may Allah have mercy on him) verified in his commentary on the General Rules of the Majalla. And the detailed juristic examination of Bay' al-Wafa' and its rules, in addition to what is mentioned in the Majalla, is found in al-Durar Sharh al-Ghurar, Radd al-Muhtar, al-Fatawa al-Bazzaziyya at the end of the Book of Sales, and Chapter 18 of Jami' al-Fusulayn, which is the most comprehensive. Finally, the Civil Code of 1949 prohibited Bay' al-Wafa' and considered it void, deeming mortgage sufficient (see: Civil Code, Art. 433).
Source pages p. 580

Commentary
This passage from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Aam discusses the juridical nature of Bay' al-Wafa' (sale with right of redemption). The author argues that it is a sui generis contract, not a subtype of sale, and should be placed in the order of nominate contracts after mortgage because understanding it requires knowledge of both sale and mortgage rules. He then critiques the Ottoman Law of Property (Law No. 3339) which treated it nearly identically to mortgage, rendering Bay' al-Wafa' redundant. He references the Majalla articles 398 and 118 to show that the default in Bay' al-Wafa' is that the buyer gets the usufruct, contrary to mortgage. He concludes by noting that the Egyptian Civil Code of 1949 banned Bay' al-Wafa', considering it void, in favor of mortgage.

Key Terms
بيع الوفاء (Bay' al-Wafa'): A contract where the seller sells property with the right to redeem it upon repayment of the price; the buyer typically gets usufruct.
الرهن (al-Rahn): Mortgage; a contract where property is given as security for a debt, with the creditor not entitled to usufruct.
المجلة (al-Majalla): The Ottoman Code of Islamic Law (Majallat al-Ahkam al-'Adliyya) based on Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) As a branch of sale
B) As a branch of mortgage
C) As an independent contract placed after mortgage
D) As a type of gift

Key Takeaway
Bay' al-Wafa' is a valid, independent Hanafi contract distinct from sale and mortgage; the buyer owns the usufruct by default.

Time Specification in Ijarah vs Ariyah
Comparison of ijarah and ariyah regarding time specification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule regarding time specification in ijarah and ariyah, and explain the legal reasoning (talil) based on the nature of the contract.

Core Idea
Time specification is wajib in ijarah but not in ariyah because ijarah is commutative and ariyah is gratuitous.

This passage from Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa's legal philosophy work compares two common contracts: ijarah (hire) and ariyah (gratuitous loan of usufruct). The key difference lies in whether time must be specified. For a mufti, understanding this distinction is crucial when advising on contract validity. The reasoning (talil) ties time specification to the nature of the contract's obligation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two contracts and their natures
Recognize that ijarah is a commutative contract (mu'awada) where wage is exchanged for usufruct, while ariyah is a gratuitous contract (tabarru') where the lender can revoke at any time.
This classification is foundational for the ruling.
2
State the rule for ijarah
Ijarah requires specification of the duration of usufruct; without it, the contract is fasid (defective).
The reason: the amount of benefits is determined by time.
3
State the rule for ariyah
Ariyah does not require specification of duration; silence or general time is valid.
The lender can revoke anytime, so precise term is not needed.
4
Analyze the talil (causation)
In ijarah, the usufruct is in exchange for wage, so its quantity must be measurable, and time is the measure. In ariyah, no such exchange exists, so time specification is irrelevant.
The illa (effective cause) is the presence or absence of compensation.
5
Consider practical implication for fatwa
If a contract is ambiguous on duration, first classify it as ijarah or ariyah to determine validity.
Misclassification leads to incorrect ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل (٤٦) عرض لأهم العقود المسماة
ومن ثم وجب في الإجارة تحديد مدة الانتفاع، لأن مقدار المنافع المقابل للأجرة إنما يحدده الزمن بخلاف الإعارة، فإنها وإن كان التوقيت ملحوظاً فيها لابتنائها على أساس الرد، لا يجب تحديد المدة في بل يصح فيها إطلاق الوقت والسكوت عنه لأنها تبرع بالمنفعة يستطيع المعير الرجوع عنه متى شاء، وليس فيه التزام بعوض يحتاج إلى تحديد مقابله من المنافع بالوحدات الزمانية.
Chapter 46: Presentation of the Most Important Named Contracts. Hence, it is obligatory in ijarah to specify the duration of usufruct, because the amount of benefits corresponding to the wage is determined by time. In contrast to ariyah, although time is considered in it because it is based on return, it is not obligatory to specify the period; rather, it is valid to leave the time unspecified and remain silent about it, because it is a gratuitous transfer of benefit, and the lender can revoke it whenever he wishes, and it does not involve a commitment to a compensation that requires specifying its equivalent of benefits in temporal units.
Source pages p. 582

Commentary
This passage explains a fundamental difference between ijarah (hire) and ariyah (loan of usufruct). Ijarah is a commutative contract (mu'awada) where the wage is exchanged for usufruct. Since the usufruct is intangible, its quantity is measured by time; therefore, specifying the duration is obligatory (wajib) for the contract to be valid. Ariyah, in contrast, is a gratuitous contract (tabarru') where the lender can revoke at any time. Thus, time specification is not required; indeed, silence about duration is permissible. This distinction highlights the Hanafi classification of contracts based on consideration.

Key Terms
الإجارة (al-ijarah): A contract for hiring the use of something in exchange for a wage.
الإعارة (al-ariyah): A contract for gratuitous loan of usufruct, where the object is returned.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the amount of benefits is determined by time.
B) Because it is a gratuitous contract.
C) Because the lender can revoke at any time.
D) Because silence about duration is invalid.

Key Takeaway
The rule that time specification is wajib in ijarah but not in ariyah is rooted in the distinction between commutative and gratuitous contracts.

Definition and Nature of Hiba
Definition and characteristics of the gift contract

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define hiba (gift) according to Hanafi fiqh and explain its nature as a real contract (ʿaqd ʿaynī) that requires delivery for completion.

Core Idea
Hiba is a gratuitous transfer of ownership that becomes binding only upon delivery, distinguishing it from sale and from contracts of usufruct.

This passage from the Majalla lays out the foundational definition of hiba (gift) in Hanafi law. Understanding the precise nature of hiba is crucial for distinguishing it from other property transfers and grasping why it is considered a 'real contract' that is not binding until executed. We will parse the definition, identify its key elements, and explore the practical legal consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contract's subject matter
The passage states the subject of hiba is 'tamlik al-insān mālahu li-ghayrihi majjānan bilā ʿiwaḍ' (transfer of ownership of one's property to another gratuitously without consideration). Identify: the act (tamlik - transfer of ownership), the parties (donor, donee), the property (māl), and the gratuitous nature.
The term 'majjānan bilā ʿiwaḍ' emphasizes the absence of any exchange or consideration.
2
Distinguish hiba from similar contracts
The passage contrasts hiba with sale (transfer for consideration) and with ʿāriyah and ijārah (transfer of usufruct, either gratuitous or for consideration). Note that the key variable is whether the transfer is of ownership (tamlik al-ʿayn) or usufruct, and whether consideration exists.
This comparative approach clarifies the category boundaries.
3
Identify the legal nature as a real contract
The passage classifies hiba (and ʿāriyah) as 'ʿuqūd ʿayniyya' — real contracts that are not completed except by delivery (taslīm). This means the contract is non-binding (ghayr mulzim) until delivery takes place; it only becomes binding upon execution.
This is a distinctive Hanafi position that affects revocability.
4
Apply the definition using the legal_definition_dissector
Using the toolbox, confirm the hukm (contract type), mahall (ownership of tangible property), sabab (offer and acceptance), shart (delivery for completion), and athar (transfer of ownership). This dissector helps internalize the definition.
The design of a 'real contract' means that delivery is a condition (shart) for completion, not for existence.
5
Recognize the practical fatwa implication
From an iftāʾ perspective, the non-binding nature until delivery means the donor can revoke the offer before delivery. The mufti must advise that the gift is not consummated until actual transfer occurs. This is the operative ruling for fatwa.
This is consistent with the Majalla's codification (articles 833-880).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
١١/٤٦ - (۹) الهبة : (المجلة / ۸۳۳ - ۸۸۰). وهي عقد موضوعه تمليك الإنسان ماله لغيره مجاناً بلا عوض. فمعطي المال واهب وقابله موهوب له والمال محل العقد: موهوب. والهبة تقابل البيع من حيث ان كليهما يرد على تمليك الأعيان المالية. لكن البيع تمليك بعوض والهبة تبرع بلا عوض، كما تقابل الإعارة الإجارة في تمليك المنافع مجاناً أو بعوض. ومن ثم كانت الهبة والإعارة من العقود العينية التي لا تتم إلا بالتسليم لأنهما تبرع غير ملزم وإنما يأخذ حكمه بالتنفيذ، كما تقدم في بحث العقود العينية (ف١٣/٣٠).
11/46 - (9) Hiba (Gift): (Majalla articles 833-880). It is a contract whose subject is the transfer of ownership of one's property to another gratuitously without consideration. So the giver is called wahib, the recipient mawhub lah, and the property is mawhub. Hiba parallels sale in that both involve transfer of ownership of tangible property. But sale is transfer for consideration, while hiba is gratuitous. It also parallels ariyah and ijarah in the transfer of usufruct, either gratuitously or for consideration. Hence, hiba and ariyah are among real contracts (uqud 'ayniyya) which are not completed except by delivery, because they are non-binding gratuitous contracts and take effect only by execution, as previously discussed in the section on real contracts (vol. 1, p. 30).
Source pages p. 582

Commentary
This passage defines hiba (gift) as a contract that transfers ownership of property without any consideration. It distinguishes hiba from sale (transfer with consideration) and from ariyah/ijarah (transfer of usufruct). The key legal implication is that hiba is a 'real contract' ('aqd 'ayni) meaning it is not binding until delivery (qabd) takes place. Until delivery, the donor may revoke. This is consistent with the Hanafi view that gratuitous contracts are non-binding until executed.

Key Terms
الهبة (al-hiba): A contract transferring ownership of property without consideration.
الواهب (al-wahib): The donor in a hiba contract.
الموهوب له (al-mawhub lah): The donee in a hiba contract.
الموهوب (al-mawhub): The gifted property in a hiba contract.
عقد عيني (ʿaqd ʿaynī): A real contract that requires delivery (qabd) for completion.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When does a hiba contract become binding according to Hanafi fiqh?
A) Upon offer and acceptance.
B) Upon delivery (qabd) of the gifted property.
C) Upon registration.
D) Upon the death of the donor.

Key Takeaway
Hiba is a gratuitous contract that becomes binding only upon delivery, not upon mere offer and acceptance.

Qismah (Partition): Definition and Dual Nature
Definition and dual nature of partition contract

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi view of partition as a combination of segregation and exchange, and distinguish between consensual and judicial partition.

Core Idea
Partition is not mere segregation; it includes an implicit exchange, making it akin to a sale.

Partition (qismah) is a fundamental concept in property law. In the Hanafi school, it is not simply the physical division of joint property but a legal process that involves both segregation (ifraz) and exchange (mubadala). This dual nature has important practical consequences, such as the application of contract rules. In this lesson, we will dissect the definition, understand the two elements, and explore the two types of partition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition
Locate the definition of qismah in the text: 'إفراز الحصص الشائعة في الملك المشترك وتخصيص كل منها بجزء معين منه'.
This is the standard Hanafi definition.
2
Break down the two elements
The author states that partition contains two elements: segregation (ifraz) and exchange (mubadala/ sale). Understand why both are necessary.
The exchange element is notional but legally significant.
3
Trace the reasoning
The author explains that in joint ownership, every atom is shared. So when dividing, each partner takes part of what is already his (segregation) and part of what was his partner's (exchange).
This justifies treating partition as including a sale.
4
Distinguish between consensual and judicial
Note that partition can be consensual (express contract) or judicial (court order with implied contract).
Only consensual partition is an explicit contract.
5
Draw practical implications
Consider how the classification affects the application of contract law rules, e.g., options, delivery, risk.
The dual nature is the key to correct fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٤٦ / ١٢ - (١٠) عقد القسمة : (المجلة / ١١١٤ - ١١٩١). القسمة هي: : إفراز الحصص الشائعة في الملك المشترك، وتخصيص كل منها بجزء معين منه . وعملية القسمة فيها عنصران : عنصر إفراز من جهة، - وعنصر مبادلة وبيع من جهة أخرى، وذلك لأن الملك الشائع في الشيء المشترك تعتبر كل ذرة منه مشتركة في الأصل (ر: ف ٦/٢٤). فلو كان الشيء مشتركاً مناصفة مثلاً فقسم نصفين، يكون كل شريك إنما يملك في الواقع نصف النصف الذي عُيْن له فقط، وباقيه لشريكه الآخر. فيعتبر كل منهما آخذاً في قسمه المعين ما يخصه من الأجزاء بطريق الإفراز، وآخذا للباقي بطريق المبادلة على ما يخصه في قسم شريكه . والقسمة تكون تارة رضائية باتفاق الشركاء ؛ وتارة قضائية يجريها القضاء بطلب بعض الشركاء جبراً على باقيهم . فالقسمة التي تعد عقداً من العقود الصريحة، إنما هي الرضائية . أما القضائية فليست عقداً صريحاً، بل هي إزالة للشيوع بطريق القضاء، ولكن يعتبر فيها عقد ضمني مقدر اقتضاء في عمل السلطة القضائية، كما تقدم في نظرية الملكية (ر: ف ۷/۲۳).
12/46 - (10) Contract of Partition: (Majalla articles 1114-1191). Partition is: the segregation of undivided shares in joint ownership, and allocation of each share to a specific part thereof. The partition process has two elements: an element of segregation (ifraz) on one hand, and an element of exchange and sale on the other. This is because in joint ownership, every atom of the property is originally shared. If the property is owned equally and divided into two halves, each partner actually owns only half of the half assigned to him, and the rest belongs to his partner. Thus each is considered to take, in his specified portion, what belongs to him by segregation, and to take the remainder by way of exchange for what belongs to him in his partner's portion. Partition is sometimes consensual (rida'iyya) by agreement of the partners, and sometimes judicial (qada'iyya) carried out by the court upon the request of some partners, compelling the others. The partition that is considered an express contract is only the consensual one. As for judicial partition, it is not an express contract but rather a removal of joint ownership by judicial process, but an implicit contract is considered to exist by necessity in the work of the judicial authority, as previously explained in the theory of ownership (vol. 1, p. 23).
Source pages p. 582

Commentary
This passage explains qismah (partition) as a legal process to separate undivided shares in joint property. The Hanafi analysis reveals that partition has two components: (1) segregation (ifraz) – each partner takes what is already his, and (2) exchange – each partner receives a portion that originally belonged to the other in exchange for his own share in the other's portion. Thus, partition is not a pure segregation but includes a notional sale. The passage then distinguishes between consensual partition (which is a contract) and judicial partition (which is a court order but implies a contract). This distinction has practical effects: for example, in consensual partition, the rules of contracts (e.g., option of inspection) apply, while in judicial partition they do not.

Key Terms
القسمة (al-qismah): Segregation of undivided shares in joint property, involving both ifraz and exchange.
إفراز (ifraz): Segregation; separating the specific portion that belongs to each partner by original ownership.
مبادلة (mubadala): Exchange; the notional sale of the portion that each partner receives from the other.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which type of partition is considered an express contract?
A) Consensual partition (qismah rida'iyya).
B) Judicial partition (qismah qada'iyya).
C) Both are express contracts.
D) Neither is a contract.

Key Takeaway
Partition in Hanafi law is a hybrid act: it segregates existing shares and exchanges the remainder, making it analogous to a sale.

Sharikah: Co-ownership vs. Contractual Partnership
Definition and classification of partnership types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between shirkat al-milk and shirkat al-'aqd, and apply the Hanafi classification to identify the legal nature of a given arrangement.

Core Idea
A partnership can be mere co-ownership or a profit-seeking contract; only the latter is a true contract in law.

This lesson introduces the fundamental Hanafi distinction between two types of partnership: one that arises naturally (co-ownership) and one that is formed by mutual agreement to conduct business. Understanding this distinction is crucial because the legal rules governing each are entirely different.

Guided Path
1
Identify the genus
Recognize that 'sharikah' is a broad term covering both joint ownership (shirkat al-milk) and contractual partnership (shirkat al-'aqd).
2
Define shirkat al-milk
Shirkat al-milk is joint ownership without a business agreement, arising from causes like inheritance or joint purchase.
It is not a contract, though its cause may be a contract (e.g., joint purchase).
3
Define shirkat al-'aqd
Shirkat al-'aqd is a contract between two or more persons to cooperate in a gainful activity and share profits.
This is the type intended in named contracts under Hanafi fiqh.
4
Contrast the two types
Note the key difference: shirkat al-milk lacks the element of mutual agreement to exploit the property for profit, while shirkat al-'aqd is built on such an agreement.
The legal effects (e.g., liability, management rights) differ accordingly.
5
Apply the distinction
Given a scenario, determine whether the arrangement is shirkat al-milk or shirkat al-'aqd by checking for a profit-seeking agreement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
١٣/٤٦ ـ (١١) عقد الشركة : (المجلة / ١٣٢٩ - ١٤٠٣) .. وهو عقد بين شخصين فأكثر على التعاون في عمل اكتسابي، واقتسام أرباحه . والشركة في ذاتها قد تكون شركة في ملك مشترك بين عدة أشخاص ناشئة عن سبب طبيعي كالإرث مثلاً. وقد تكون شركة عقد بأن يتعاقد جماعة على القيام بعمل استثماري يتساعدون فيه بالمال أو بالعمل، ويشتركون في نتائجه. فشركة الملك هي من قبيل الملك الشائع وليست من العقود، وإن كان سببها قد يكون عقداً، كما لو اشترى شخصان شيئاً، فإنه يكون مشتركاً بينهما شركة ملك، ولكن ليس بينهما عقد على استغلاله واستثماره بتجارة أو إجارة ونحو ذلك من وسائل الاسترباح كما تقدم بيانه في نظرية الملكية (ر ف ٨/٢٤) وأما شركة العقد التي غايتها الاستثمار والاسترباح فهي المقصودة هنا والمعدودة من أصناف العقود المسماة
13/46 - (11) Contract of Partnership: (Majalla articles 1329-1403). It is a contract between two or more persons to cooperate in a gainful activity and share its profits. Partnership in itself may be a partnership in joint ownership (shirkat al-milk) arising from a natural cause such as inheritance, or it may be a contractual partnership (shirkat al-'aqd) where a group contracts to undertake an investment activity, assisting each other with capital or labor, and sharing the results. Shirkat al-milk is a type of joint ownership and is not a contract, though its cause may be a contract, as when two persons buy something, it becomes joint between them as shirkat al-milk, but there is no contract between them to exploit it through trade, lease, etc., as previously explained in the theory of ownership (vol. 1, p. 24). As for the contractual partnership whose purpose is investment and profit, it is the one intended here and counted among the types of named contracts.
Source pages p. 583

Commentary
This passage defines sharikah (partnership) broadly and distinguishes two main types: shirkat al-milk (co-ownership) and shirkat al-'aqd (contractual partnership). Shirkat al-milk is simply joint ownership without an agreement to run a business; it arises naturally (e.g., inheritance) or by a joint purchase. It is not a contract in the true sense because there is no mutual agreement to share profits from a business. In contrast, shirkat al-'aqd is a genuine contract where parties agree to engage in a profit-seeking enterprise, contributing capital or labor. The Hanafi fiqh categorizes various forms of shirkat al-'aqd (e.g., shirkat al-'inan, shirkat al-mufawada), but the passage only gives the general definition. This distinction is crucial because the legal rules applicable to each type differ significantly.

Key Terms
الشركة (al-sharikah): An arrangement for joint ownership or cooperation in business.
شركة الملك (shirkat al-milk): Joint ownership without an agreement to conduct business.
شركة العقد (shirkat al-'aqd): A contract to cooperate in a gainful activity and share profits.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following best describes shirkat al-milk?
A) A contract to share profits from a business.
B) Joint ownership without an agreement to exploit the property.
C) A partnership formed by mutual consent for investment.
D) A type of sale contract.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school sharply distinguishes between mere co-ownership (shirkat al-milk) and contractual partnership (shirkat al-'aqd); only the latter is a true contract with mutual intent to profit.

Definition and Legal Nature of Qard (Loan)
Explains the qard contract with its dual nature and guarantee effect

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the qard contract, explain its dual nature as both a donation and an exchange, and distinguish it from aariyah in terms of possession being a guarantee.

Core Idea
Qard transfers ownership of fungible goods; the borrower guarantees the value, not the specific item.

In this lesson, we explore the Hanafi legal definition of qard (loan). The passage reveals that qard combines two contractual meanings: it begins as a gratuitous donation but functions as an exchange because the borrower consumes the property and owes its like. This dual nature has practical implications for possession and liability. We will dissect the definition and its effects.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contract's essential elements
What are the parties, subject matter, and obligation? Note the requirement for mithli (fungible) property.
The subject must be consumable and fungible.
2
Analyze the dual nature
How does qard contain the meaning of both aariyah and sale? Consider the initial character (donation) and the operational character (exchange).
The passage says 'فيه معنى عقدين اثنين'.
3
Trace the legal effect of possession
What happens upon receipt? Why is it a guarantee (daman) and not a trust (amanah)? What are the consequences for liability?
The passage emphasizes 'قبض ضمان'.
4
Distinguish from aariyah
How does the obligation to return differ? What happens if the item remains unused?
Qard allows return of like, not the same item.
5
Note the historical omission in the Majalla
Why does the author criticize the Majalla for neglecting qard?
This highlights the development of Hanafi codification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القرض : وهو عقد موضوعه أن يأخذ أحد المتعاقدين من الآخر مالاً مثلياً استهلاكياً كالنقود والزيت والقمح، على أن يرد إليه مثله فيما بعد. فدافع المال : مُقْرِض بضم فسكون والآخذ: مُسْتَقْرض ومُقْتَرض؛ والمال المدفوع : قَرْضٌ بفتح فسكون»، ومُقرَضٌ بصيغة المفعول». والقرض فيه معنى عقدين اثنين :
- معنى الإعارة ابتداء، لأنَّ فيه تبرعاً بإعطاء مال على أساس رده إلى معطيه .
- ومعنى المعاوضة والبيع ؛ لأن المال لا يُؤخذ فيه على أساس استعماله والاحتفاظ بعينه وإعادته بذاته كما في العارية، وإنما يُؤخذ على سبيل استهلاكه في حاجة المقترض، ثم تأدية عِوَض عنه من مثله إلى المقرض. فمتى قبضه يملكه ويصبح العوض المقابل ديناً عليه ثابتاً في ذمته .
ففي القرض من هذه الجهة مبادلة ومعاوضة، وهو معنى البيع.
ولذلك لو استغنى المقترض عما اقترضه قبل استهلاكه لا يكون ملزماً بإعادته عيناً ولو أنه قائم في يده بل له أن يمسكه لديه ويرد مثله.
وعن هذا كان القبض في القرض قبض ضمان لا قبض حفظ وأمانة.
فبمجرد تسلم المقترض المال بيده مباشرة أو بيد وكيله بالقبض أو رسوله، المال المقبوض مضموناً على المقترض أي إن كل آفة تصيب هذا المال بعد ذلك، من هلاك فما دونه إنما تكون على مسؤوليته وحسابه كمسؤولية المشتري بعد تسلمه المبيع.
ومن ثم يفترق القرض عن العارية التي تبقى على ملك صاحبها المعير أمانة في يد المستعير لا يضمنها إلا بتعديه عليها أو بتقصيره في حفظها. هذا، وإن المجلة لم تبحث عن عقد القرض، وقد كان عليها أن لا تهمله كما سلفت الإشارة إليه.
Qard (loan): It is a contract whose subject is that one of the two contracting parties takes from the other fungible consumable property, such as money, oil, and wheat, on the condition that he returns its like to him later. The giver of the property is called muqrid, and the taker is mustaqrid or muqtarid; the property given is called qard or muqrad. Qard contains the meaning of two contracts: the meaning of loan (aariyah) initially, because it involves a donation of property on the basis of returning it to its giver; and the meaning of exchange and sale, because the property is not taken on the basis of using it, retaining its essence, and returning it as it is, as in aariyah, but rather is taken for consumption for the borrower's need, then paying a substitute of its like to the lender. So once he takes possession, he owns it and the corresponding substitute becomes a debt established in his liability. Thus, from this aspect, qard involves exchange and compensation, which is the meaning of sale. Therefore, if the borrower becomes independent of what he borrowed before consuming it, he is not obligated to return the same item, even if it exists in his hand, but rather may keep it and return its like. Because of this, taking possession in qard is a possession of guarantee, not possession of safekeeping and trust. As soon as the borrower receives the property directly or through his agent or messenger, the received property is guaranteed on the borrower, meaning that any calamity that befalls it thereafter, from destruction to lesser, is his responsibility and account, like the responsibility of the buyer after receiving the sold item. Hence, qard differs from aariyah, which remains the property of its owner, the lender, as a trust in the hand of the borrower and is not guaranteed except by his transgression or negligence in its preservation. This, and the Majalla did not discuss the qard contract, although it should not have neglected it, as previously indicated.
Source pages p. 588

Commentary
This passage defines the loan (qard) contract in Hanafi law. The author explains that qard involves a transfer of fungible goods (mithli) like money, oil, or wheat, with the obligation to return an equivalent amount. He highlights the dual nature of qard: initially it resembles a loan for use (aariyah) because it is a gratuitous grant, but functionally it is like a sale because the borrower consumes the property and owes a debt of the same type. Consequently, possession in qard transfers ownership and makes the borrower a guarantor (daman) — any loss is on his account, unlike aariyah where the borrower is only liable for misuse. The author notes that the Ottoman Majalla omitted qard, which he criticizes.

Key Terms
قرض (qard): A contract whereby one gives fungible property to another for consumption, with the obligation to return its like.
عارية (aariyah): A contract to give property for use without consuming it, retaining ownership and returning the same item.
مثلي (mithli): Goods that have equivalents in the market and can be replaced by similar items (e.g., money, grains).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi definition of qard, what happens to the borrowed property immediately upon possession?
A) It remains the property of the lender as a trust
B) It becomes owned by the borrower, and a debt of its like is established
C) It becomes a shared ownership between lender and borrower
D) It must be returned exactly as received, even if not consumed

Key Takeaway
Qard is a transfer of ownership of fungible goods for consumption, with the borrower guaranteeing the debt and bearing any loss, unlike aariyah where the item is returned as is.

The 'Umra Contract: Definition and Customary Basis
Definition of the 'umra contract

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the 'umra contract and identify its characteristic condition of reversion.

Core Idea
'Umra is a lifetime gift that reverts to the giver upon the recipient's death.

In pre-Islamic Arabia and early Islamic practice, a particular form of gift called 'umra was common. This lesson clarifies its definition and the condition attached to it, setting the stage for understanding its legal treatment in the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term and its linguistic root
The word 'umra is derived from 'umr (life). Recognize that the name itself hints at the lifetime nature of the gift.
2
Understand the transactional structure
The giver says 'I grant you this as 'umra' or 'it is for you as 'umra.' Identify the parties: donor (qā'il) and recipient (maqūl lahu).
3
Recognize the condition of reversion
The gift is for the recipient's lifetime; if the recipient dies, the property returns to the donor. This condition is the defining feature of 'umra.
This condition distinguishes 'umra from an absolute gift (hiba).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العُمرى (١) : وهي نوع من الهبة معتاد عند العرب بأن يقول الشخص لآخر : أعمرتك هذا الشيء أو هذه الدار أو هي لك عُمْرى أي على سبيل (العمرى ومعناه أنها للمقول له مدى عمره فإن مات عادت إلى القائل .

(١) بضم العين المهملة وبالألف المقصورة وزان صغرى وكبرى).
Al-'Umra: It is a type of gift customary among the Arabs, where one person says to another: 'I grant you this thing or this house as 'umra,' or 'It is for you as 'umra,' meaning that it is for the recipient for the duration of his life; if he dies, it returns to the giver.
Source pages p. 589

Commentary
This passage defines the 'umra contract, a pre-Islamic Arabian custom adopted into Islamic law. 'Umra is a specific form of gift (hiba) where the property is given to the recipient for his lifetime, with automatic reversion to the donor upon the recipient's death. The term derives from 'umr (life). Hanafi jurists discuss its validity: according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, 'umra is valid and the condition of reversion is void, making the gift absolute; the property passes fully to the recipient's heirs. Muhammad al-Shaybani considers the condition valid, so it reverts. The relied-upon fatwa (mufta bihi) is that the condition is null and the gift is absolute, as per hadith 'al-'umra ja'iz li ahlihi' (the 'umra is valid for its recipient).

Key Terms
العُمرى (al-'umra): A gift of property for the recipient's lifetime, with an express or implied condition of return to the donor upon the recipient's death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what triggers the return of property to the giver in an 'umra contract?
A) The expiration of a fixed term
B) The death of the recipient
C) The donor's demand
D) The sale of the property

Key Takeaway
'Umra is a lifetime gift with a condition of reversion; understanding this definition is essential for analyzing its legal status.

Nature and Legal Effect of Loan (Qard) in Hanafi Fiqh
Explains loan as an exchange contract, its binding nature, and distinction from loan for use.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish qard from 'ariyah in Hanafi fiqh and apply the rule that qabd in qard is qabd daman.

Core Idea
In Hanafi fiqh, a loan (qard) is an exchange contract; the borrower guarantees the property from the moment of possession.

This lesson explores the Hanafi conceptualization of the loan contract (qard). The source text from Mustafa al-Zarqa's work explains that qard is fundamentally an exchange (mubadala), akin to a sale, and that the borrower's possession is a possession of guarantee (qabd daman). This has practical consequences: if the borrower no longer needs the loan before consuming it, he may keep the property and return an equivalent, rather than being forced to return the exact same item. This distinguishes qard from 'ariyah (loan for use), where the borrower is a trustee and only liable for negligence.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the loan as an exchange contract
Understand that qard is considered a type of sale (bay') because the borrower compensates with an equivalent. This aligns with the Hanafi view that qard is binding once possession is taken.
The text states 'qard is an exchange and compensation, which is the meaning of sale.'
2
Analyze the nature of possession (qabd)
Distinguish between qabd daman (guarantee possession) and qabd amana (trust possession). In qard, the borrower is liable for any loss or damage from the moment of receipt, like a buyer.
This is a key difference from 'ariyah.
3
Apply the rule to a scenario
If the borrower no longer needs the loan before using it, he is not compelled to return the exact item; he may keep it and return a like item. This illustrates the exchange nature.
The text explicitly states this consequence.
4
Distinguish from 'ariyah
In 'ariyah, the item remains the property of the lender, possession is trust, and the borrower only liable for transgression or negligence. The practical effect is different risk allocation.
The text highlights that 'ariyah is a trust, not a guarantee.

ففي القرض من هذه الجهة مبادلة ومعاوضة، وهو معنى البيع. ولذلك لو استغنى المقترض عما اقترضه قبل استهلاكه لا يكون ملزماً بإعادته عيناً ولو أنه قائم في يده بل له أن يمسكه لديه ويرد مثله. وعن هذا كان القبض في القرض قبض ضمان لا قبض حفظ وأمانة. فبمجرد تسلم المقترض المال بيده مباشرة أو بيد وكيله بالقبض أو رسوله، المال المقبوض مضموناً على المقترض أي إن كل آفة تصيب هذا المال بعد ذلك، من هلاك فما دونه إنما تكون على مسؤوليته وحسابه كمسؤولية المشتري بعد تسلمه المبيع. ومن ثم يفترق القرض عن العارية التي تبقى على ملك صاحبها المعير أمانة في يد المستعير لا يضمنها إلا بتعديه عليها أو بتقصيره في حفظها (۱). هذا، وإن المجلة لم تبحث عن عقد القرض، وقد كان عليها أن لا تهمله كما سلفت الإشارة إليه. وبعض الفقهاء يذكرونه في فصل فرعي من كتاب البيوع ( ر ) فصل القرض من كتاب البيوع في الدر المختار ورد المحتار ١٧١/٤ - ١٧٦) .
Thus, in this respect, loan is an exchange and compensation, which is the meaning of sale. Therefore, if the borrower no longer needs what he borrowed before consuming it, he is not obliged to return it in kind even if it remains in his hand; rather, he may keep it and return its equivalent. For this reason, possession in a loan is a possession of guarantee, not a possession of safekeeping and trust. As soon as the borrower receives the money directly or through his agent or messenger for receipt, the received money is guaranteed by the borrower, meaning every calamity that befalls this money thereafter, from destruction to lesser, is upon his responsibility and account, like the responsibility of the buyer after receiving the sold item. Hence, loan differs from 'ariyah (loan for use), which remains the property of its owner, the lender, as a trust in the hand of the borrower; he is not liable for it except by transgression or negligence in its preservation (1). This, and the Majalla did not discuss the contract of loan, and it should not have neglected it as previously indicated. Some jurists mention it in a subsection of the book of sales (see: section on loan from the book of sales in al-Durr al-Mukhtar and Radd al-Muhtar 4/171-176).
Source pages p. 589

Commentary
This passage explains the legal nature of a loan (qard) in Hanafi fiqh. The author establishes that a loan is fundamentally an exchange contract (mubadala wa mu'awada), similar to a sale, because the borrower benefits from the property and is required to return an equivalent. A key consequence is that if the borrower no longer needs the item before consuming it, he is not forced to return the very same item; he may keep it and return a like item. This is because possession in a loan is a possession of guarantee (qabd daman), not a possession of trust (qabd amana). Once the borrower takes possession, all risks of loss or damage fall on him, just like a buyer after taking delivery. This distinguishes loan from 'ariyah (loan for use), where the item remains a trust in the borrower's hands and he is only liable for negligence or transgression. The author notes that the Ottoman Majalla (a codification of Hanafi fiqh) regrettably did not treat the loan contract, but some Hanafi jurists discuss it within the framework of sales.

Key Terms
قرض (qard): A contract in which one party gives fungible property to another with the condition that the recipient returns an equivalent amount or quality.
عارية (‘ariyah): A contract where one party gives another non-fungible property for use without consumption, to be returned as a trust.
قبض ضمان (qabd daman): Possession that carries liability for loss or damage to the property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi fiqh, if a borrower receives a loan of 100 dinars and then after one month no longer needs the money before spending it, what is the ruling?
A) He must return the exact same coins immediately.
B) He may keep the money and return an equivalent later.
C) The loan becomes a gift because he did not use it.
D) He must return the money with profit.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the loan contract (qard) is an exchange with possession as guarantee (qabd daman), distinct from 'ariyah where possession is trust.

Muwalat as a Basis for Insurance: A Hanafi Legal Analogy
Legal definition and analogical expansion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the contract of muwalat as a qiyas basis for the validity of modern insurance and evaluate the accompanying madhhab dispute.

Core Idea
Muwalat is a valid Hanafi contract that can analogically justify modern insurance.

The contract of muwalat (clientage) is a unique Hanafi institution that allows a person without blood heirs to contract with another for inheritance and blood-money liability. This passage defines muwalat and then presents it as a strong analogical basis (qiyas) for the permissibility of modern insurance contracts. We will dissect the contract, understand its legal effects, and then see how the Hanafi master Mustafa al-Zarqa uses it to bridge to contemporary insurance, all while acknowledging inter-madhhab differences.

Guided Path
1
Define the contract of muwalat
Identify the two parties, the offer, and the mutual obligations: inheritance vs. payment of blood-money for accidental offenses.
Focus on the terms 'mawla al-muwalat' and 'ta‘qil'.
2
Map the legal effects
The acceptor inherits from the declarant (after spouse's share) and must pay blood-money for accidental crimes. Note the conditions and the absence of a blood heir as the cause (sabab).
Reference the legal_definition_dissector block for a structured view.
3
Examine the madhhab dispute
Hanafis accept muwalat; other schools reject it, citing the hadith restricting wala' to emancipation. Understand the Hanafi response distinguishing muwalat from wala' of emancipation.
Use the madhhab_dispute_map block.
4
Apply the qawida 'al-darar yuzal'
Recognize that muwalat compensates harm (blood-money) – this is the same objective as insurance. See how the qawida 'harm must be removed' justifies both.
Refer to the qawida_panel block for furuat and istithna'.
5
Construct the analogy to insurance
The author uses muwalat as a solid foundation for qiyas: insurance also involves compensation for harm caused by external factors, analogous to the acceptor paying blood-money. Identify the common 'illa (need for harm removal).
Consider potential objections like gharar or riba which are exceptions.

(21) Contract of Muwalat (clientage): A contract between two persons, one of whom has no relative heir. He says to the other: 'I make you my patron (mawla)' or 'You are my heir who inherits from me when I die, and you pay blood-money on my behalf if I commit an offense.' The meaning of 'you pay blood-money on my behalf' is that you pay the legal blood-money if I commit an unintentional crime such as homicide or less. By this contract, this clientage is established between the contracting parties: the acceptor undertakes the financial obligation for accidental offenses if committed by the declarant, and he becomes entitled to all the estate as inheritance by virtue of this contract if the declarant dies, unless the declarant has a spouse, then the acceptor gets the remainder after the share of one of the spouses, depending on whether the declarant is male or female. Certain conditions are required, explained in their proper place in the books of inheritance (fara'id) and the book of clientage (wala') in fiqh works. This acceptor who inherits in return for his undertaking to guarantee accidental offenses is called 'mawla al-muwalat.' Important Note: Comparison between the contract of muwalat and today's insurance contract (soukra): 24/46 - The contract of muwalat, as is evident to the contemplative, constitutes a strong basis and analogical support for the validity of the insurance contract (soukra) prevalent today in commercial transactions, whose subject and aim is compensation for damages that befall goods and objects due to causes not caused by the affected owner, by the insurance company paying an agreed compensation for specified types of damages...
Source pages p. 590

Commentary
This passage presents the contract of muwalat (clientage) in Hanafi fiqh. It is a contract between two parties where one has no blood heirs. The offer (from the one without heirs) makes the other his patron and heir, and the acceptor undertakes to pay blood-money on his behalf for accidental crimes. In return, the acceptor inherits the estate. If there is a spouse, the acceptor gets the remainder after the spouse's share. This contract is subject to conditions detailed in fiqh works. The author then makes a significant note: muwalat can serve as a strong analogical basis (qiyas) for the validity of modern insurance (soukra). The insurance contract involves a company paying compensation for damages in exchange for premiums, similar to muwalat where the acceptor pays blood-money and inherits. This comparison shows that insurance is not a forbidden gambling but a legitimate mutual guarantee contract by qiyas.

Key Terms
الموالاة (al-muwalat): A contract by which a person without blood heirs agrees with another that the latter inherits from him and pays blood-money for his accidental crimes.
مولى الموالاة (mawla al-muwalat): The party who accepts to pay blood-money and inherits in return.
تعقل (ta‘qil): To pay blood-money (diya) on behalf of someone who commits an accidental offense.
الضرر يزال (al-darar yuzal): A major legal maxim meaning 'Harm must be removed or compensated.' It is the basis for the analogy between muwalat and insurance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The absence of heirs.
B) The mutual exchange of money.
C) The need to remove or compensate for harm (al-darar yuzal).
D) The fact that both are contracts of guarantee.

Key Takeaway
Muwalat provides a classical Hanafi model for insurance by analogizing the removal of harm through mutual guarantee.

The Contract of Muwalat as an Analogical Basis for Insurance
Defines 'aqd al-muwalat and argues for validity of insurance by qiyas jaliy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a qiyas jaliy from the contract of muwalat to justify the permissibility of modern insurance contracts in Hanafi fatwa.

Core Idea
A new contract need not have an explicit text; analogical reasoning from established contracts suffices for permissibility.

This passage from Mustafa al-Zarqa presents a contemporary fatwa-style argument: the classical Hanafi contract of muwalat (clientage) can serve as the foundational analogy (asl) for modern insurance. The author demonstrates how to identify the effective cause (illa) and apply qiyas jaliy to derive a ruling for a novel contract. This lesson walks you through the analogical reasoning, the Hanafi position on insurance, and the counter-argument from Ibn Abidin.

Guided Path
1
Identify the asl and its ruling
The asl is the contract of muwalat. Its ruling in the Hanafi school is sahih (valid). Confirm the elements: definition, parties, conditions, effect.
Muwalat is a contract between two persons: one with no blood heir (qa'il) and an acceptor (qabil). The acceptor inherits and guarantees blood-money.
2
Identify the far' (the new case)
The far' is the modern insurance contract (tamin). Its subject is compensation for damages to goods or property from covered risks, in exchange for premiums.
Insurance is a recent contract not explicitly mentioned in classical sources, so qiyas is needed.
3
Determine the illa (effective cause)
The illa in muwalat is mutual guarantee against accidental loss (daman janayat al-khata). Insurance also involves mutual guarantee against accidental harm to property. Both share the common attribute of Takaful (cooperative risk-sharing).
The author also cites another analogy: guaranteeing road risk (daman khatar al-tariq) where a person says 'take this road, I am liable'.
4
Verify no explicit prohibition and illa satisfaction
No explicit text (nass) prohibits insurance. The illa of mutual guarantee is satisfied in both contracts. Therefore, qiyas applies: the ruling of the asl (validity) transfers to the far'.
This step reflects the principle that for new contracts, analogical evidence suffices; an explicit text is not required.
5
State the ruling and address the khilaf
The author concludes: insurance is ja'iz (permissible). Ibn Abidin's objection is considered but the author prioritizes the qiyas and practical necessity.
The practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf): some muftis permit, some prohibit insurance. The preferred view here is permissibility.

The contract of al-muwalat: a contract between two persons, one of whom has no blood heir. He says to the other: 'You are my master (mawla), or you are my guardian; you inherit from me when I die, and you pay the blood-money (ta'qil) for me when I commit a crime.' The meaning of 'ta'qil 'anni' is: you pay the legal blood-money if a homicide by mistake or less occurs by me. Through this contract, this patronage (wala') is established between the two contracting parties. The acceptor (al-qabil) becomes obligated to pay the financial liability for a mistaken crime if it occurs from the sayer (al-qa'il), and he also deserves his entire estate as inheritance by virtue of this contract if he dies, unless the sayer has a spouse, in which case the acceptor gets the remainder of the estate after the share of one of the spouses, depending on whether the sayer is a man or a woman. Some conditions are required that are explained in their place in the book of fara'id and the book of wala' in fiqh works. This acceptor, who inherits in exchange for his undertaking to guarantee the mistaken crime, is called 'mawla al-muwalat'. Important note: Comparison between the contract of muwalat and today's insurance contract (al-sukrah). Section 24/46: Indeed, the contract of muwalat, as it appears to the observer, is a strong basis and analogical support for the validity of the insurance contract (al-sukrah) common today in commercial transactions, whose subject and purpose is compensation for damages that befall goods and things due to causes that the affected owner of these things had no role in causing, by the insured company paying an agreed-upon compensation for specified types of damages in the contract if they occur, in return for the owner of the property paying a small annual amount to the insurance company called the premium. Today, many Islamic scholars and muftis hesitate about the validity of this contract, or they announce its prohibition. What I see is that it is a permissible contract with no impediment in the principles of Islamic law, and it involves reparation of damages and cooperation in bearing them instead of them falling on the head of the affected person alone, especially since today's import trade, with these fast means of transportation, has connected the ends of the world with each other, and its risks have increased as much as its speed has increased, so a merchant cannot be reassured about his financial stability without this insurance against risks of transport, fire, and the like. Our jurists have stated the validity of guaranteeing the risk of the road in certain forms, as if a person says to another: 'Take this road, for it is safe, and if anything happens to you, I am responsible.' So if he takes it and is harmed, the speaker is liable (see Radd al-Muhtar, book of kafalah). And we, regarding the insurance contract, do not need to find an explicit text on its permissibility in the principles of Sharia and its fiqh, because it is a modern contract; rather, it suffices that we find analogical evidences that support it. And what we have stated is sufficient, although the late Ibn Abidin discussed this contract in his commentary Radd al-Muhtar and looked at it from another angle, concluding its impermissibility (see Radd al-Muhtar, Bab al-Musta'min from Kitab al-Jihad).
Source pages p. 590

Commentary
This passage defines the contract of al-muwalat (clientage) and then uses it as a basis to legitimize modern insurance contracts through qiyas (analogy). The author begins by explaining the contract: it is an agreement between a person with no blood heir and another who agrees to become his heir in return for undertaking liability for his accidental crimes (payment of blood-money). This contract establishes a legal relationship of clientage (wala'). The acceptor becomes liable for the blood-money of the sayer's accidental crimes and inherits his entire estate, except when the deceased leaves a spouse, then the acceptor takes the remainder. Then the author makes an important note: he sees the contract of muwalat as a strong analogical basis for the validity of modern insurance contracts. The purpose of insurance is compensation for damages to goods due to events beyond the policyholder's control, in exchange for an annual premium. The author asserts that despite hesitation from many scholars, the contract is permissible in Islamic law. He provides further evidence: Hanafi jurists have validated a form of guaranteeing road risk, as when someone says 'take this road and I guarantee any harm.' By analogy, insurance operates similarly: the community shares risks. The author acknowledges that Ibn Abidin opposed insurance, but he maintains it is valid based on qiyas. The key point is that for new contracts, explicit text is not required; analogical proofs are sufficient.

Key Terms
عقد الموالاة ('aqd al-muwalat): a contract between two persons where the one with no blood heir appoints the other as his heir in return for undertaking blood-money liability
مولى الموالاة (mawla al-muwalat): the acceptor who inherits and guarantees blood-money
تعقل (ta'qil): to pay blood-money
ضمان جناية الخطأ (daman janayat al-khata'): liability for accidental crimes

Key Takeaway
The contract of muwalat provides a valid analogical basis for the permissibility of modern insurance through qiyas jaliy on the effective cause of mutual guarantee against accidental harm.

Analogy for Modern Insurance: Qiyas on ضمان خطر الطريق
Contemporary fatwa using qiyas jaliy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a qiyas argument justifying the permissibility of a modern insurance contract using the Hanafi ruling on ضمان خطر الطريق.

Core Idea
Insurance is permissible through qiyas on the classical guarantee of road risk – necessity and analogy justify a modern contract.

The author, a prominent Hanafi jurist, argues for the permissibility of insurance despite the absence of direct textual evidence. He relies on the principle of qiyas, specifically analogizing to the established ruling on ضمان خطر الطريق. Here we examine the structure of that analogy and its implications for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original case (asl)
Locate the classical Hanafi ruling on ضمان خطر الطريق – when someone says 'Take this road, I guarantee you against harm,' the guarantor is liable.
Reference: Radd al-Muhtar, book of Kafala.
2
Identify the new case (far')
Define the modern insurance contract: the insured pays a premium, and the insurer promises compensation for specified risks (transport, fire, etc.).
This is a contemporary contract not directly mentioned in classical texts.
3
Determine the common illa (effective cause)
Both cases involve a promise of compensation for potential harm in exchange for taking a risk. In the classical case, the person takes the road; in insurance, the insured engages in risky activity.
The illa is 'promise to compensate for a specific risk undertaken at the request of the promisor'.
4
Apply qiyas to derive the ruling
Since the illa is present in insurance, the ruling of permissibility (ja'iz) from the asl transfers to the far'. The author also cites necessity (haja) as supporting evidence.
The author notes that explicit text is not required; analogical proofs suffice.
5
Note the ikhtilaf and evaluate tarjih
Ibn Abidin examined insurance from another angle and deemed it impermissible. The author acknowledges this but chooses permissibility based on qiyas and public benefit.
The thamarah al-khilaf is the operative fatwa: permissible vs impermissible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واليوم يتردد علماء الشريعة ومفتوها كثيراً في صحة هذا العقد، أو يعلنون تحريمه.
والذي أرى أنه عقد جائز لا مانع منه في أصول الشريعة الإسلامية، وفيه ترميم للأضرار وتعاون على احتمالها بدلاً من أن تنزل في رأس المصاب وحده ولا سيما أن تجارة الاستيراد اليوم، بهذه المواصلات السريعة ربطت أطراف العالم بعضها ببعض، وكثرت أخطارها بقدر ما زادت رعتها، فلا يمكن أن يطمئن التاجر فيها على كيانه المالي دون هذا التأمين ضد أخطار النقل والحريق ونحو ذلك.
وقد نص فقهاؤنا على صحة ضمان خطر الطريق في صور معينة، كما لو قال شخص لآخر: «اسلك هذا الطريق فإنه آمن، وإن أصابك شيء ضامن. فإذا سلكه فأصيب يضمن القائل . (ر ) رد المحتار كتاب الكفالة). ونحن تجاه عقد التأمين لا يجب أن نجد لجوازه نصاً صريحاً عليه في أصول الشريعة وفقهها، لأنه عقد حديث، بل يكفي أن نجد دلائل قياسية تؤيده. وفيما بيناه كفاية؛ وإن كان المرحوم ابن عابدين بحث في هذا العقد في حاشيته رد المحتار ونظر إليه من زاوية أخرى انتهى منها إلى عدم جوازه (ر ) رد المحتار باب المستأمن من كتاب الجهاد).
And today scholars of Sharia and its muftis hesitate much about the validity of this contract, or they declare its prohibition. And what I see is that it is a permissible contract, with no obstacle in the principles of Islamic Sharia, and it involves repairing damages and cooperation in bearing them instead of them falling on the head of the afflicted alone, especially since today's import trade, with these fast means of transportation, has bound the parts of the world together, and its risks have increased in proportion to its speed, so the merchant cannot be secure in his financial entity without this insurance against risks of transport, fire, and the like. And our jurists have stipulated the validity of guaranteeing the risk of the road in specific forms, such as if a person says to another: 'Take this road, for it is safe, and if anything befalls you, I am liable.' So if he takes it and is afflicted, the speaker is liable. (Refer to Radd al-Muhtar, book of Kafala). And we, regarding the insurance contract, do not need to find an explicit text for its permissibility in the principles of Sharia and its fiqh, because it is a modern contract; rather it suffices to find analogical proofs that support it. And what we have stated is sufficient; although the late Ibn Abidin discussed this contract in his hashya Radd al-Muhtar and looked at it from another angle, concluding its impermissibility (Refer to Radd al-Muhtar, chapter of al-Musta'min from the book of Jihad).
Source pages p. 591

Commentary
This passage is a contemporary legal opinion (fatwa) by the author on the permissibility of modern insurance contracts. The author begins by noting the debate among scholars. He then states his view that insurance is permissible (ja'iz) because it serves the purpose of compensating harm and mutual cooperation, and is necessary for modern commerce. He supports his argument by citing the Hanafi ruling on 'ضمان خطر الطريق' (guarantee against road risk) from Radd al-Muhtar. In that ruling, if someone encourages another to take a road by promising to compensate any harm, that promise is binding. The author argues that insurance is analogous: the insurance company promises compensation in exchange for premiums. He acknowledges that a clear textual basis is not required; analogical reasoning (qiyas) suffices. He also notes that Ibn Abidin, in his commentary, examined insurance from a different perspective and deemed it impermissible, but the author does not adopt that view.

Key Terms
عقد التأمين (‘aqd al-ta’mīn): A modern contract where the insured pays a premium (قسط) in exchange for the insurer's promise to compensate against specified risks.
ضمان خطر الطريق (ḍamān khaṭar al-ṭarīq): A specific guarantee where one person promises to compensate another if harmed while traveling on a particular road.
قياس جلي (qiyās jaliyy): A clear analogy where the effective cause (illa) is evident and undisputed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Custom of the people

Key Takeaway
The permissibility of modern contracts can be established through sound qiyas on classical rulings, especially when necessity and public benefit support the analogy.

Iqala as an Independent Contract: Definition and Scope
Theoretical discussion on defining iqala as an independent contract

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define iqala as an independent contract, identify its scope (including exceptions), and apply its definition to binding contracts beyond sale.

Core Idea
Iqala is not just rescinding a sale; it is an independent contract that annuls any binding contract except marriage.

Most students first encounter iqala in the context of sales, where it is treated as a simple dissolution. But Hanafi legal theory elevates iqala to a full contractual status. This passage from Dr. Zarqa explains why iqala deserves its own analytical treatment and how it applies across a wide range of binding contracts. Understanding this will sharpen your ability to analyze contract termination in Islamic law.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the general definition
Read the author's definition: iqala is 'a contract by which a previous contract is annulled'. Note that this is not limited to sales.
The definition emphasizes that iqala is itself a contract, not a mere dissolution.
2
Identify the scope
Which contracts can be rescinded by iqala? The author says all binding contracts (العقود اللازمة) except marriage.
Binding contracts include sale, hire, guarantee, settlement, partnership, etc.
3
Note the unique feature: iqala of iqala
The author states that iqala itself can be rescinded. This revives the original contract as if it had never been dissolved.
This demonstrates the contractual nature of iqala.
4
Apply the definition
Test with examples: can iqala be used for a hire contract? Yes. Can it be used for a marriage? No.
The exclusion of marriage is explicit; other binding contracts are included.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Chapter Six: Theory of Contracts and Related Matters in Fiqh. Likewise, the Majalla did, making iqala a branch of the book of sales, and defined it - following most jurists - as the annulment of a sales contract (Majalla article 163). This, as you see, is a definition for iqala of sale, not for iqala in general. But it is appropriate that it be considered an independent contract that concludes transactions contracts because iqala is not specific to sales but applies to all binding contracts except marriage. The verified definition in general is that it is 'a contract by which a previous contract is annulled.' Cf. the chapter on iqala from the book of sales in al-Jawharah and Radd al-Muhtar. Just as a sales contract is annulled by iqala, so too are contracts of hire, guarantee, settlement, and partnership annulled. The parties return to their state and rights before the contract. And even iqala itself accepts iqala, so by rescinding the iqala, the contract is restored between the two parties, and their connection to its rulings and effects returns as if it had not been dissolved. (See: al-Durr al-Mukhtar and its gloss 4/150).
Source pages p. 593

Commentary
This passage is a theoretical discussion on the nature of iqala (rescission of contracts). The author begins by noting that the Majalla treated iqala as a branch of sales, but he argues this is too narrow. Iqala is not limited to sales; it applies to all binding contracts (عقود لازمة) except marriage. Therefore, it should be considered an independent contract that concludes the series of transactional contracts. The author gives a general definition: iqala is a contract that lifts a previous contract. He then illustrates its broad application: it can rescind sales, hire, guarantee, settlement, and partnership. The effect is that the parties revert to their pre-contract state. Finally, he notes an interesting nuance: iqala itself can be rescinded (iqala of iqala), effectively reviving the original contract. This shows the flexibility of Islamic contract law.

Key Terms
الإقالة (al-iqālah): عقد يُرفع به عقد سابق (a contract by which a previous contract is annulled)
عقد لازم (ʿaqd lāzim): عقد ملزم للطرفين (binding contract that cannot be unilaterally dissolved)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Iqala is an independent contract that annuls any previous binding contract except marriage, and it can even be rescinded itself.
