Marriage (Nikah): Definition, Conditions and Effects
Defines marriage, its conditions, and effects

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the essential components of a valid marriage contract according to the Hanafi school and apply the legal effects that arise from it.

Core Idea
Marriage is a contract requiring mahr and witnesses; its effects include lawful intimacy, mutual obligations, and inheritance.

Marriage (nikah) is the cornerstone of family life in Islamic law. This passage from Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa lays out the Hanafi definition: a contract between a man and a woman for lawful sexual enjoyment, procreation, and cooperation. The author then explains the two essential requirements – mahr (dower) and witnesses – and the legal consequences that follow the contract, including maintenance, obedience, and inheritance. As you study this passage, you will learn to parse a legal definition and distinguish between conditions (shurut) and effects (athar) in Hanafi marriage law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of marriage
Extract the key elements from the definition: it is a contract between a man and a woman, with the purpose of lawful sexual enjoyment, reproduction, establishment of a family, and cooperation.
The Arabic term 'عقد' (contract) is central – marriage is a legal agreement, not a mere social arrangement.
2
List the essential conditions (shurut)
From the passage, note two conditions: mahr (dower) and witnesses (ishhad). Understand their role: mahr is an obligatory payment from husband to wife; witnesses are required for validity to publicize the marriage.
The Hanafi minimum mahr is ten silver dirhams (or equivalent). Witnesses are a condition of validity (shart al-sihha).
3
Recognize the mutual effects (athar)
The passage lists: lawful enjoyment, wife's maintenance, wife's obedience (in lawful matters), and inheritance between spouses. These are consequences that arise automatically from a valid marriage.
These effects are not conditions but results. They are binding upon both parties.
4
Note the dissolution and iddah
The contract ends by divorce, death, or other causes. Upon dissolution, the wife must observe iddah (waiting period) to ensure no pregnancy from the previous husband, except if divorced before consummation.
Iddah is a period during which remarriage is prohibited; its purpose is to prevent confusion of lineage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الزواج، أو النكاح
وهو عقد بين رجل وامرأة غايته استحلال المتعة الجنسية بينهما بغية التناسل وتأسيس الأسرة بالطريق المشروع والتعاون في حياة مشتركة . فكل من الرجل والمرأة بعد العقد يسمى زوجاً للآخر، وقد يقال للمرأة زوجة بعلامة التأنيث على خلاف الأفصح لغة.
وعقد الزواج لا بد فيه شرعاً من مهر (بفتح فسكون)، وهو مقدار من المال يلتزم به الرجل للمرأة ويسمى أيضاً: «صداقاً» بفتح الصاد المهملة . واختلفت الاجتهادات في الحد الأدنى للمهر، والاجتهاد الحنفي على أنه لا يقل عن عشرة دراهم من الفضة أو ما يعادل قيمتها.
ويشترط لصحته الإشهاد على عقده بغية إعلانه لإزالة الريبة من اجتماع الرجل بالمرأة في حياة مشتركة كما تقدم في مناسبته. (ف ٦/٢٧ الحاشية).
والزواج، علاوة على ما يثبت به من حلّ الاستمتاع بين الزوجين، ينشيء حقوقاً ووجائب متقابلة بينهما من مالية وأسرية : منها نفقة الزوجة على الزوج بحدود وشرائط معينة، ووجوب متابعتها له، وطاعتها إياه في غير إثم ومعصية شرعية، وإرث أحد الزوجين من الآخر .
وينحل الزواج وينتهي بالطلاق وبوفاة أحد الزوجين، وببعض أسباب أخرى.
وبانحلال الزوجية بأحد أسبابه تجب على المرأة عدة، وهي تربص مدة معينة من الزمن لا يجوز لها خلالها أن تتزوج، وذلك لمقاصد شرعية أهمها: أن يُعرف فراغ رحمها من الزوج السابق منعاً لاختلاط الأنساب واشتباهها. لكن إذا طلقت الزوجة قبل الدخول بها فلا عدة عليها .
Marriage, or nikah. It is a contract between a man and a woman whose purpose is to make lawful sexual enjoyment between them with the aim of reproduction, establishing a family in a legitimate way, and cooperation in a shared life. So each of the man and woman after the contract is called a spouse of the other, and the woman may be called 'zawjah' with the feminine marker, contrary to the more eloquent linguistically. The marriage contract necessarily requires, according to Sharia, a mahr (with a fatha and sukun), which is an amount of wealth that the man undertakes to give to the woman, also called 'sadaq' with a fatha on the important sad. There is difference of ijtihad on the minimum amount of mahr, and the Hanafi ijtihad is that it should not be less than ten silver dirhams or their equivalent in value. For its validity, it is required to have witnesses to the contract to publicize it and remove doubt about the man and woman living together in a shared life, as mentioned in its appropriateness (footnote 6/27). Marriage, besides establishing the lawfulness of enjoyment between the spouses, creates mutual rights and obligations, financial and familial: including maintenance of the wife by the husband within certain limits and conditions, the obligation of the wife to accompany him and obey him in what is not sinful or disobedient to Sharia, and inheritance between the spouses. Marriage is dissolved and ends by divorce, death of one spouse, and by some other causes. Upon dissolution of marriage by one of its causes, a woman must observe iddah, which is waiting a specific period of time during which she may not marry, for Sharia objectives the most important of which is to know that her womb is free from the previous husband to prevent mixing of lineages and their confusion. But if the wife is divorced before consummation, there is no iddah upon her.
Source pages p. 593

Commentary
This passage provides a comprehensive definition of marriage (nikah) from a Hanafi perspective. The author begins by defining marriage as a contract between a man and a woman aimed at lawful sexual enjoyment, procreation, family formation, and cooperation. He notes the linguistic point that 'zawjah' is a common feminine form though 'zawj' is more eloquent. Essential components are: (1) mahr (dower) – an obligatory payment from husband to wife; the Hanafi view sets a minimum of ten silver dirhams (or equivalent). (2) Witnesses (ishhad) – required for validity to publicize the marriage and remove suspicion. The effects of marriage include: lawfulness of sexual intimacy, husband's obligation to provide maintenance (nafaqa), wife's duty to obey in lawful matters, and mutual inheritance. The contract is dissolved by divorce, death, or other causes. Upon dissolution, the wife must observe iddah (waiting period) to ensure no pregnancy from the previous husband, unless the marriage was not consummated. This section is a concise summary of Hanafi marriage law.

Key Terms
المهر (al-mahr): مقدار من المال يلتزم به الرجل للمرأة في عقد الزواج (a sum of wealth the man undertakes to give the woman in marriage)
الإشهاد (al-ishhād): إعلان العقد بشهود لصحته (publicizing the contract with witnesses for validity)
العدة (al-ʿiddah): تربص مدة معينة لا يجوز للمرأة خلالها التزوج بعد انحلال الزواج (a waiting period during which a woman may not remarry after dissolution)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway

Wasiyya: Definition and One-Third Limit
Defines the legal contract of wasiyya and its core limitation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the wasiyya, identify its key parties, and apply the one-third limit rule.

Core Idea
A will is a suspended donation limited to one-third of the estate to balance charity with heirs' rights.

This lesson introduces the essential definition of a will (wasiyya) in Hanafi law and the crucial rule limiting it to one-third of the estate. Understanding this definition and its rationale equips you to advise on valid will drafting and to identify when heirs' consent becomes necessary.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition and its components
Extract the core definition: 'أن يوجب إنسان في حياته تبرعاً من ماله لغيره بعد وفاته' — a person obligates a donation from his wealth to another after his death.
Focus on the elements: obligation during life, donation (tabarru'), after death.
2
Distinguish the parties
Identify the three key terms: الموصي (testator), الموصى له (legatee), الموصى به (bequeathed property).
The legatee can be any legal entity capable of ownership.
3
Understand the suspended nature
The will is 'مضاف حكمه إلى ما بعد الموت' — its legal effect is attached to after death. This means ownership transfers only upon death.
Until death, the testator can revoke the will.
4
Analyze the one-third limit and its rationale
The text states the limit is 'في حدود ثلث ماله' to encourage charity while protecting heirs' rights.
The rationale is a balance between the testator's generosity and the heirs' prescribed shares.
5
Apply the rule to an excess case
If the will exceeds one-third, it executes up to one-third and the excess is suspended (requires heirs' consent).
Suspended means the excess is not automatically void but conditionally valid pending consent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٢٧/٤٦ - (٢٤) عقد الوصية :
الوصية هي أن يوجب إنسان في حياته تبرُّعاً من ماله لغيره بعد وفاته .
فالموجب صاحب المال : مُوصٍ . والطرف الآخر: موصى له. والمال المخصص لذلك : موصى به أو وصية.
فالوصيَّة تبرع من الإنسان في حياته مضاف حكمه إلى ما بعد مماته، وهو الوقت الذي تتعلق فيه حقوق الورثة بالتركة.
فالشرع الإسلامي قد أطلق للإنسان هذا الحق في حدود ثلث ماله تشجيعاً على عمل الخير في سبيل البر بجزء من ماله، وحفظاً لحقوق الورثة في الباقي.
فإذا تجاوزت الوصية قدر الثلث نفذت في الثلث، وتوقفت الزيادة
27/46 - (24) Contract of Wasiyya: The wasiyya is that a person obligates during his life a donation from his wealth to someone else after his death. So the obligator, the owner of the wealth, is the musī (testator). The other party is the mūṣā lahu (legatee). The wealth designated for that is mūṣā bihī (bequeathed property) or wasiyya. So the wasiyya is a donation by a person during his life whose legal effect is attached to after his death—the time when the rights of the heirs become attached to the estate. Islamic law has granted this right to a person within the limit of one-third of his wealth, to encourage doing good in the path of virtue with a portion of his wealth, and to preserve the rights of the heirs in the remainder. If the will exceeds the amount of one-third, it is executed within the one-third, and the increase is suspended.
Source pages p. 594

Commentary
This passage defines the contract of wasiyya (will or testament). It begins by defining the will as an obligation a person makes during his lifetime, transferring ownership by way of donation (tabarru') to another after his death. The parties are: the testator (mūṣī), the legatee (mūṣā lahu), and the bequeathed property (mūṣā bihī). The key legal feature is that the will is a suspended transfer (muḍāf ḥukmuhu ilā mā baʿda al-mamāt) – its effect takes effect after death. The author explains the wisdom behind the one-third rule: to encourage charitable giving while protecting the heirs' rights to the remaining two-thirds. If the will exceeds one-third, only one-third is executed, and the surplus is suspended (i.e., needs consent of the heirs). This is a core principle of Islamic inheritance law.

Key Terms
الوصية (al-waṣiyyah): أن يوجب إنسان في حياته تبرعاً من ماله لغيره بعد وفاته (a person obligates a donation from his wealth to another after his death)
الموصي (al-mūṣī): صاحب المال الذي يوجب التبرع (the owner of the wealth who obligates the donation)
الموصى له (al-mūṣā lahu): الطرف الآخر الذي يتلقى التبرع (the other party who receives the donation)
الموصى به (al-mūṣā bihī): المال المخصص للتبرع (the wealth designated for the donation)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Half is given to the legatee
C) One-third is given to the legatee and the remainder is suspended
D) The heirs can claim the entire estate

Key Takeaway
A will is a suspended donation that is automatically effective up to one-third of the estate; any excess requires the consent of the heirs to take effect.

The Will as a Bilateral Contract vs Unilateral Disposition
Explaining the contractual nature of a will to a specific person

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a will as a bilateral contract or a unilateral disposition based on the beneficiary and apply the Hanafi rule regarding acceptance or non-rejection.

Core Idea
A will to a specific person requires acceptance; a will to a public cause takes effect automatically.

The contractual nature of a will (wasiyya) varies depending on the beneficiary. When made to a specific person, it functions as a bilateral contract similar to a gift, but with deferred acceptance. For public causes, it becomes a unilateral disposition that does not require any response. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly applying the rules of inheritance and bequests.

Guided Path
1
Classify the will by beneficiary
Identify whether the will names a specific person or a general cause (e.g., mosque, school, poor).
The text explicitly distinguishes these two categories.
2
Determine the contractual nature
If the beneficiary is a specific person, the will is a bilateral proprietary contract (عقد مملك ذو طرفين). If it is a public cause, it is a unilateral disposition (تصرف بالإرادة المنفردة).
The nature determines the rules of acceptance.
3
Apply the acceptance rule
For bilateral contracts, acceptance or non-rejection (عدم الرد) is required after death. For unilateral dispositions, no acceptance is needed; the will takes effect immediately.
Silence (non-rejection) suffices as acceptance for specific persons.
4
Consider the consequence of rejection or death
If the legatee rejects after the testator's death, the will is void and the property returns to the estate. If the legatee dies without accepting or rejecting, the right to accept passes to the legatee's heirs.
This is a unique Hanafi rule.
5
Distinguish from gifts (hiba)
Unlike a gift, which transfers ownership immediately and requires acceptance in the testator's lifetime, a will defers ownership to after death, so acceptance is postponed.
This explains why acceptance is deferred.

الباب السادس : نظرية العقود وما إليها في الفقه فقط على إجازة الورثة : فإن أجازوها نفذت ؛ وإلا بطلت الزيادة وضمت إلى ميراثهم . فالوصية إذا كانت الشخص معيَّن كانت عقداً مُمَلكاً ذا طرفين كالهية . ولكن لكونه تمليكاً مضافاً إلى ما بعد الموت يكون القبول أو الرد فيه مؤخراً اعتباره إلى ذاك الحين. فلو رد الموصى له الوصية بعد موت الموصي بطلت الوصية، وبقيت في تركة الموصي لورثته. لكن يغني عن القبول فيها عدم الرد كما تقدم في مناسبته، حتى لو مات الموصى له بعد موت الموصي دون أن يعرف منه قبول أو رد كانت ملكاً لورثة الموصى له . (ر: ف ۸/۳۱). أما إذا لم تكن الوصية لشخص معيَّن بل كانت لمصلحة جهة من الجهات العامة كالمساجد والمدارس والجهاد والفقراء، فإنها عندئذ لا تعد من العقود ذات الطرفين بل من تصرُّفات الإرادة المنفردة، فتكون نافذة بمجرد وفاة الموصي مصراً عليها ولا يتأتى فيها قبول أو رد.
Chapter Six: Theory of Contracts and Related Matters in Jurisprudence. Only with the consent of the heirs: if they consent, it is executed; otherwise, the increase is void and is added to their inheritance. So if the will is for a specific person, it is a proprietary bilateral contract like a gift. But because it is a transfer of ownership deferred to after death, acceptance or rejection is postponed in consideration to that time. If the legatee rejects the will after the testator's death, the will is void and remains in the testator's estate for his heirs. However, non-rejection suffices for acceptance as previously mentioned in its context, such that if the legatee dies after the testator without known acceptance or rejection, it becomes the property of the legatee's heirs. (ref: F 8/31). As for when the will is not for a specific person but for the benefit of a general cause, such as mosques, schools, jihad, and the poor, then it is not considered a bilateral contract but rather a unilateral disposition, so it takes effect immediately upon the testator's death while persisting, and acceptance or rejection does not apply.
Source pages p. 595

Commentary
This passage discusses the contractual nature of a will (wasiyya) in Hanafi fiqh. The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, explains that a will to a specific person is a bilateral contract (cأqd mumallik dhu tarafayn) similar to a gift (hiba), but because ownership transfers after death, acceptance or rejection is deferred until after the testator's death. If the legatee rejects it after death, the will is void and the property remains with the heirs. However, silence (عدم الرد) suffices as acceptance; if the legatee dies without acceptance or rejection, the legatee's heirs inherit the right to accept. For wills benefiting general causes (e.g., mosques, charitable projects), these are unilateral dispositions (tasarrufat al-irada al-munfarida) that take effect immediately upon death without requiring acceptance or rejection.

Key Terms
الوصية (al-wasiyya): تمليك مضاف إلى ما بعد الموت
الموصى له (al-musa lahu): الشخص المعين الذي توجّه له الوصية
قبول (qabul): إظهار الرضا بالوصية بعد موت الموصي، أو عدم الرد

A) The will is void
B) The legatee's heirs inherit the right to accept
C) The property goes to the public treasury
D) The testator's heirs take the property

Key Takeaway
A will to a specific person is a bilateral contract requiring acceptance (or non-rejection); a will to a public cause is a unilateral disposition taking effect automatically.

The Contract of Guardianship (Wasaya) in Hanafi Fiqh
Defining the contract of guardianship and its legal implications

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the contract of guardianship (wasaya), distinguish between the chosen guardian and the court-appointed guardian, and explain the legal effects of acceptance and grounds for removal.

Core Idea
Guardianship is a contract of agency after death; once accepted, the guardian cannot resign without cause.

This passage from Dr. Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa's legal introduction explores the contract of guardianship (wasaya) as a key named contract in Hanafi fiqh. It clarifies the nature of the appointment, the distinction between contractual and judicial guardianship, and the legal consequences of acceptance. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for anyone dealing with estate planning, minors' property, or fatwa issuance in family law.

Guided Path
1
Parse the Definition
Identify the parties (testator and guardian), the subject matter (executing the will and managing minors' rights), and the temporal scope (after death). Note that it is a contract (عقد) requiring offer and acceptance.
The guardian is called 'wasi' (with shaddah on ya).
2
Examine Acceptance
The guardian may accept or reject after the testator's death, similar to a financial will. However, engaging in the work (mumarasah) serves as implied acceptance. Once accepted, the guardian becomes a legal representative.
This is analogous to the rule in contracts where performance indicates acceptance.
3
Distinguish the Two Types
The chosen guardian (wasi mukhtar) is appointed by the testator via contract; the court-appointed guardian (wasi al-qadi) is appointed by the judge as an administrative act. The former has broader authority and cannot be removed if competent and trustworthy.
This distinction affects the legal analysis of their appointment and removal.
4
Understand Removal and Liability
The chosen guardian cannot resign after acceptance, nor can the judge remove him if he is capable and honest. However, if he becomes incapacitated, betrays trust, or is negligent, the judge removes him and holds him liable.
The judge's removal is a safeguard against abuse.
5
Connect to Modern Regulations
The passage notes historical Ottoman reforms and the 1953 Syrian Personal Status Law, showing how classical Hanafi principles were codified. This illustrates the ongoing relevance of these rulings.
Understanding these roots helps in applying fiqh to contemporary contexts.

٢ / ٢٨ - (٢٥) عقد الإيصاء أو (الوصاية) : عقد وهي يوجب به الإنسان في حياته نيابة عنه بعد وفاته لشخص آخر في تنفيذ وصية المتوفى، وإدارة حقوق أولاده القاصرين. يقال: أوصى فلان إلى فلان إذا عهد إليه بالوصاية . فالموجب موص. والشخص المعهود إليه بالوصاية، رجلاً كان أو امرأة: وصي (بتشديد الياء). وللوصي قبول الوصاية وردها بعد وفاة الموصي ايضا كما ، الوصية المالية، لكن ممارسته لأعمال الوصاية تقوم مقام القبول. عقد في ومتى قبل أصبح نائباً شرعياً، فيكون هو صاحب الصلاحية في تنفيذ الوصية المالية وإدارة أموال القاصرين وحفظها، والإنفاق الضروري عليهم حتى يبلغوا رشدهم. 1 .. ٦٢٨ : الفصل (٤٦) عرض لأهم العقود المسماة وليس له أن يعزل نفسه عن الوصاية متى قبلها بعد وفاة الموصي، ولا للقاضي أن يعزله إذا كان كفءاً أميناً، لأنه منصوب بإرادة الميت لا بإرادة القاضي (ر: الدر المختار أول باب الوصي). ولكنه إذا كان عاجزاً، أو خان في عمله المؤتمن عليه أو قصر، يعزله القاضي عزلاً، ويضمنه ما خان فيه أو قصر من حقوق القاصرين أو الوصية. وهذا الوصي الذي اختاره المتوفى في حياته يسمى: الوصي المختار، وهو الذي يكون العهد إليه من الموصي عقداً كما بيناه. فإذا لم يعهد الإنسان في حياته بوصاية إلى أحد، وترك وصية مالية أو أولاداً ينصب القاضي بمقتضى صلاحيته العامة وصياً لتنفيذ الوصية المالية، وإدارة أموال الأولاد فيسمى عندئذ وصي القاضي، ويكون نصبه أعمال القضاء الإدارية لا عقداً بالمعنى الاصطلاحي في هذا المقام . ويفترق الوصي المختار عن وصي القاضي بأحكام تتسع فيها صلاحية الأول عن الثاني. ولإدارة أموال الأيتام نظام خاص في بلادنا منذ العهد العثماني قيدت به صلاحيات الأوصياء، وألزموهم فيه بطرائق قانونية تحت إشراف مجلس يسمى : مجلس الأيتام مرتبط بالمحكمة الشرعية وذلك حفظاً لحقوق الأيتام التلاعب. ثم صدر لدينا قانون الأحوال الشخصية سنة / ١٩٥٣ م/ من فنظم هذه الأحكام من جديد. وترى في كتاب الأحكام الشرعية في الأحوال الشخصية للمرحوم قدري باشا المصري أحكام الوصاية وصلاحيات الأوصياء ومسؤولياتهم مفصَّلة في المواد / ٤٣٥ - ٤٨١ منه ؛ وأحكام الوصية في المواد / ٥٣٠ - ٥٥٨/ ؛ وأحكام الزواج وما يتفرّع عنه من طلاق ونسب ونفقة وولاية وغيرها في المواد / ١ - ٤٣٤/ . ٦٢٩
2/28 - (25) The contract of appointment of a guardian or (guardianship): It is a contract by which a person during his life appoints another person to act on his behalf after his death in executing the testator's will and managing the rights of his minor children. It is said: so-and-so appointed so-and-so when he entrusted him with guardianship. The appointer is the testator. The person entrusted with guardianship, whether male or female, is the guardian (wasi, with shaddah on the ya). The guardian may accept or reject the guardianship after the testator's death as well, like a financial will, but his practice of the duties of guardianship serves as acceptance. Once he accepts, he becomes a legal representative, so he has the authority to execute the financial will, manage and preserve the assets of minors, and provide necessary maintenance until they reach maturity. 1 .. 628 : Chapter 46: Survey of the Most Important Named Contracts. He may not remove himself from guardianship once he has accepted it after the testator's death, nor may the judge remove him if he is competent and trustworthy, because he is appointed by the will of the deceased, not by the will of the judge (ref: al-Durr al-Mukhtar, beginning of chapter on guardian). But if he is incapacitated, or betrays his entrusted work, or is negligent, the judge removes him and holds him liable for what he betrayed or neglected regarding the rights of minors or the will. This guardian chosen by the deceased during his life is called the chosen guardian (al-wasi al-mukhtar), and his appointment by the testator is a contract as we explained. If a person did not appoint a guardian during his life and leaves a financial will or children, the judge, by virtue of his general authority, appoints a guardian to execute the financial will and manage the children's assets; he is then called the judge's guardian (wasi al-qadi), and his appointment is an administrative act of the judiciary, not a contract in the technical sense in this context. The chosen guardian differs from the judge's guardian in rulings, with the former having broader authority than the latter. For managing the assets of orphans, there is a special system in our country since the Ottoman era that restricted the powers of guardians and required them to follow legal procedures under the supervision of a council called the Orphans Council, attached to the Sharia court, in order to protect the rights of orphans from manipulation. Then the Personal Status Law was issued in our country in 1953 CE, which reorganized these provisions. You can see in the book 'al-Ahkam al-Shar'iyya fi al-Ahwal al-Shakhsiyya' by the late Qadri Pasha al-Masri the detailed provisions of guardianship, powers of guardians, and their responsibilities in articles 435-481; provisions of wills in articles 530-558; and provisions of marriage and its derivatives such as divorce, lineage, maintenance, and guardianship in articles 1-434. 629
Source pages p. 595

Commentary
This passage defines the contract of guardianship (wasaya) in Hanafi fiqh. The author explains that it is a contract by which a person (the testator) appoints another (the guardian) to act on his behalf after death for executing his will and managing the affairs of his minor children. The guardian may accept or reject after the testator's death, but engaging in the work constitutes acceptance. Once accepted, the guardian becomes a legal representative with full authority over the will and the minors' assets. The guardian cannot resign after acceptance unless incapacitated or dishonest; otherwise, the judge may remove him for cause. Two types are distinguished: the chosen guardian (wasi mukhtar) appointed by the testator himself (a contract), and the judge's guardian (wasi al-qadi) appointed by the court (an administrative act). The chosen guardian has broader powers. The passage also discusses historical and modern regulations in the author's context, including Ottoman-era reforms and the 1953 Personal Status Law in Syria.

Key Terms
الوصاية (al-wasaya): عقد يوجب به الإنسان نيابة عنه بعد وفاته لشخص آخر في تنفيذ وصيته وإدارة حقوق أولاده القاصرين
الوصي المختار (al-wasi al-mukhtar): من اختاره الموصي في حياته ليقوم بالوصاية بعد وفاته
وصي القاضي (wasi al-qadi): من ينصبه القاضي بمقتضى صلاحيته العامة عند عدم تعيين وصي من قبل الميت

Key Takeaway
The contract of guardianship (wasaya) creates a binding agency after death; acceptance cannot be revoked except for cause, and the distinction between contractual and court-appointed guardians affects the scope of authority and removal rules.

Legal Philosophy and Theory

Classification of Contracts: Trusts and Guarantees
Explains overlapping classifications of contracts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between contracts of trust (uqud al-amanat) and contracts of guarantee (uqud al-damanat) in Hanafi fiqh, and explain how this classification affects liability for loss.

Core Idea
In trust contracts, the holder is liable only for misconduct; in guarantee contracts, the holder bears all risk of loss.

The classification of contracts into trusts and guarantees is fundamental to Hanafi contract law. This lesson examines this primary classification alongside the related distinction between real and consensual contracts. Understanding these categories is essential for a mufti to correctly determine liability when property held under a contract is damaged or lost.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two liability categories
List the two main categories: contracts of trust (uqud al-amanat) and contracts of guarantee (uqud al-damanat). Note the examples given for each.
Trust: wadi'ah, i'arah, ijarah, shirkah. Guarantee: bay', qismah, sulh, qard.
2
Understand the liability rule for each category
For trust contracts, the holder is not liable for loss unless caused by his transgression (ta'addi) or negligence (taqsir). For guarantee contracts, the holder is liable for any loss, even from an act of God.
3
Recognize the cross-classification with real vs consensual contracts
Some trust contracts (wadi'ah, i'arah, qard) are real (uqud 'ayniyah) and require delivery for completion; others (ijarah, shirkah) are consensual. Guarantee contracts like sale and lease are consensual.
This affects when ownership and risk transfer.
4
Apply the classification to a practical scenario
Given a scenario where property is damaged, determine if the contract is trust or guarantee to decide liability.
Practice with examples from the passage.

Chapter 47: Classification of Contracts. It is the habit of legal authors in the theory of contract to discuss the classification of contracts before researching the general provisions. We, however, have chosen to postpone our discussion on the division and classification of contracts to the conclusion of the topics on the general provisions of the contract, so that the student may have acquired sufficient information to help him understand the division and classification. 47/2: Indeed, contracts in general differ from one another in the basis upon which they are founded, the subject they aim for, the characteristics they possess, the attributes and provisions that affect them, and other legal considerations. And each group and category shares a common aspect that unites them in some respects and considerations, although there are differences and distinctions in other aspects. For example, deposit (wadi'ah), loan (i'arah), lease (ijarah), and partnership (shirkah) share that they are contracts of property to which the contract and execution apply; the property in the hands of the depositary, borrower, lessee, or partner is a trust (amanah), and he is not liable for what befalls it of loss or damage unless by his transgression or negligence. As for sale (bay'), division (qismah), settlement (sulh), and loan of money (qard), they are considered contracts of guarantee (damanat). Upon their execution by delivery, the property becomes guaranteed (madhmun) against loss or damage by any cause, even a heavenly calamity, and the recipient bears the responsibility and account. At the same time, we find that these two different categories in terms of liability may also be united by a common bond from another perspective. Deposit, loan, and loan of money are real contracts (uqud 'ayniyah) which are not completed except by execution and delivery, as previously stated. While sale, division, settlement, lease, and partnership are not real contracts; they are completed and their rulings are established without being contingent upon execution. The buyer owns the sold item and the lessee owns the usufruct by mere offer and acceptance. Therefore, it is necessary to classify contracts into various categories according to the different considerations by which one category is distinguished from another, which distinction is a basis for different legal results and rulings, or a basis for arranging these contracts in a manner that achieves proportionality and consistency among them, organizing their study and treatment of their rulings. Contracts are classified into different categories based on many considerations, as follows: 47/3 - (First) By considering naming and non-naming: Contracts under this consideration are classified into two types: A - Named contracts (uqud musamma), which the legislation has given a name indicating their specific subject matter and original rulings that follow from their formation, such as sale, gift, lease, partnership, and others mentioned in the previous chapter. B - Unnamed contracts (uqud ghayr musamma), for which no specific name has been coined for their subject matter, and the legislation has not assigned rulings specific to them. Unnamed contracts are many and unlimited, as they vary according to the need of the contracting parties and the agreed subject within lawful objectives, and they are collectively referred to by the term 'contract' or 'agreement'. Many new contracts have arisen in Islamic jurisprudence in different eras, and jurists have given them special names and established rulings for them, thus they became named contracts.
Source pages p. 598

Commentary
This passage from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm by Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa discusses the classification of contracts from a legal theoretical perspective. The author first explains his methodological choice to postpone the discussion on classification until after the general provisions, so that students have sufficient background. He then identifies two fundamental categories based on liability: contracts of trust (amanat) and contracts of guarantee (damanat). In trust contracts (e.g., deposit, loan of use, lease, partnership), the property in the hands of the counterparty is a trust, and they are only liable for loss if they commit transgression or negligence. In guarantee contracts (e.g., sale, division, settlement, loan of money), the property is guaranteed from the moment of delivery, meaning the recipient bears the risk of loss even from an act of God. Then the author notes that these two categories intersect with another classification: real contracts (uqud 'ayniyah) versus consensual contracts. Some trust contracts (deposit, loan of use, loan of money) are real, requiring delivery to be complete, whereas guarantee contracts like sale and lease are consensual, perfected by offer and acceptance alone. This distinction affects when ownership and risk transfer. Finally, the passage introduces classification by naming: named contracts have specific names and default rulings in the Shari'ah, while unnamed contracts are those that do not have a predetermined name or specific rulings. The author notes that many new contracts have been developed by later jurists and became named, showing the dynamic nature of Islamic contract law. This passage exemplifies the Hanafi approach to legal theory, emphasizing practical consequences of classifications.

Key Terms
عقود الأمانات (uqud al-amanat): Contracts where the property in the hands of the counterparty is a trust; the holder is not liable for loss except in case of transgression or negligence.
عقود الضمانات (uqud al-damanat): Contracts where the property is guaranteed by the recipient; the recipient bears the risk of loss from any cause.
عقود عينية (uqud 'ayniyah): Real contracts that are not completed except by delivery of possession.
عقود مسماة (uqud musamma): Contracts that have a specific name and default rulings established by the Lawgiver.
عقود غير مسماة (uqud ghayr musamma): Contracts that have no specific name or special rulings; their validity is based on general principles of contract law.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is a contract of trust (amanah) and also a real contract?
A) Sale (bay')
B) Lease (ijarah)
C) Deposit (wadi'ah)
D) Partnership (shirkah)

Key Takeaway
The distinction between trust and guarantee contracts is foundational in Hanafi fiqh: it determines who bears the risk of loss and reflects the nature of each contract.
